BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE
PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
1. SECTOR AND PROJECT BACKGROUND
A. SOCIO-ECONOMIC CONTEXT
Geography, Population And Poverty
1.1    Most of the land area of Bangladesh is a low-lying, nearly flat, fertile, alluvial flood plain.
Three major river systems flow through the country into the Bay of Bengal. 80% of the annual
rainfall, which averages from 1,200 mm to 3,500 mm in different parts of the country, occurs during
the monsoon season and large areas are subject to 'normal' annual flooding from the monsoon and the
snow melt in the Himalayas, making Bangladesh one of the wettest areas in the world. In addition,
Bangladesh is prone to natural disasters including abnormal flooding and cyclones. These geographical
features make the construction and maintenance of transport infrastructure particularly difficult and
expensive, as land transport requires the building of higher embankments and many bridges and
drainage structures, and as river transport requires frequent dredging to remove silt and also faces
safety problems. The country also lacks adequate supply of natural stones, and therefore road works
have to depend on costly bricks baked out of clay for use as road materials.
1.2    With a population of about 120 million (1995), Bangladesh has a population density of about
813 persons per sq.km, making it the most densely populated country among non-city states. It is also
one of the poorest countries, with a per capita income of about US$240. Its Gross Domestic Product
(GDP) which grew at a low 1.8% per annum during the period 1980 to 1992, has recently increased at
a much higher rate of 4.4% during Fiscal Years (FY) 1992-95 rising to 4.7% in FY96; but this is yet
to make any significant dent on poverty. Poverty is more widespread in the rural areas and among
women; about half the rural population lives below the level of "absolute poverty" (defined as an
intake of 2,122 calories per person per day), and more than a quarter live below the "hard core" (with
per capita intake of less than 1,805 calories). Bangladesh remains primarily a rural society, with about
80% of the population living in rural areas.
Rural Economy
1.3    Bangladesh remains primarily a rural society, with about 80% of the population living in rural
areas. Agriculture is the major rural economic activity, contributing about 33% of GDP in 1995 (a
drop from 55% in 1970), providing about 69% of all employment and directly or indirectly producing
most of the country's exports.  About 66%  of the total land area of the country is cultivable.



2
Agricultural land is intensively utilized, with no unsettled land left to exploit, and is fertile. The
cropping intensity, taking account of multiple cropping, is more than 170%. About 70% of the
agricultural GDP is from crop production, the remainder from forestry, livestock and fisheries.
1.4    There is a growing trend towards diversification of agriculture, which requires more timely
transportation and marketing, and yields higher income. The growth of agricultural production in
recent years is the result of increasing use of high yielding variety (HYV) seeds, fertilizers and
irrigation, and a move to higher value crops. The current agricultural strategy of the Government of
Bangladesh (GOB) focuses on continuing development and diffusion of seed, fertilizer and water
technologies; increased crop diversification into higher value crops; a more competitive internal
marketing system with a greater role for the private sector; an improved rural transport infrastructure;
and improved access for rural people to sustainable employment opportunities.
1.5    As a predominantly rural country, rural infrastructure plays a vital role in supporting economic
growth. Studies in Bangladesh and elsewhere have shown a strong linkage between improved rural
access and market facilities, on the one hand, and increased agriculture production, employment, farm
and non-farm incomes, social development, and poverty alleviation. In Bangladesh, where poverty is
pervasive particularly in the rural areas where the bulk of the population lives, improving rural
infrastructure, which is in very poor condition at present, is an important strategy of GOB for
increasing and diversifying farm production, for raising incomes and living standards, and for reducing
poverty. Further, special emphasis is being given while designing rural infrastructure projects to
maximize employment and poverty impact by using labor-intensive technology wherever viable and by
targeting the poor for receiving income generating opportunities under the projects. Under recent and
ongoing projects, with considerable IDA and other donor support, GOB has made a good beginning in
strengthening infrastructure-related institutions and policies, in setting good quality standards, and in
making physical progress in improving infrastructure, but there is still a long way to go to meet the
massive investment needs in the sector.
B. THE RURAL TRANSPORT AND TRADING SYSTEM
1.6    The characteristics of high population density, high productivity of land, intensive cultivation,
small-scale farming and poverty-induced sale of even subsistence level production, search for
employment by the under-employed in farms and non-farm activities, and an active non-farm sector,
together generate intensive trading of goods and services in the rural areas and a high level of demand
for movement of goods and people; as a result the level of traffic in the rural areas of Bangladesh,
particularly in using the popular non-motorized forms of transport and the local "hats" or markets,
remains high. Although rural Bangladesh is poor, it has many characteristics which result in a highly
active rural cash economy in which mobility and trading are of crucial importance.
1.7    The main elements of Bangladesh's rural transport and trading infrastructure are: (1) rural
roads, which are, though extensive, mainly poor quality earth roads, requiring improved surfacing and
pavements on a selective basis, and interspersed with many gaps to be filled with drainage structures
for allowing uninterrupted traffic; most of these are seasonal roads, generally not usable in the rainy
season, creating considerable hardship for transportation during the monsoon period; and road
trangport with a strikingly dominant share of non-motorized transport (manually pedaled cycle-
rickshaws, passenger rickshaws and rickshaw vans, and to some extent animal -drawn carts) which
provide reasonably efficient rural transport suited to the small- parcel loads needed by most farmers,
and also provide employment to a large number of the rural poor. (2) inland water transport (IWT).
the traditional transport system of Bangladesh, which criss-crosses the country and carries a large part
of rural traffic through small country boats, but needs to be better integrated with road transport with
the provision of more landing facilities (river ghats) for country boats, while also requiring other



3
improvements such as channel dredging, navigational safety aids etc; and (3) the rural market
network, with over 8000 rural markets or "hats' spread over the country, effectively constituting the
nerve center of the rural economy, facilitating the trading of all rural produce and most farm inputs
and consumer goods; most of these markets lack basic facilities (paved areas, sheds, clean water,
drainage, and garbage disposal facilities) and good transport access, resulting in unhygienic and
inefficient conditions for marketing produce. GOB has designated about 2,100 of these markets, based
on objective socio-economic criteria, as growth center markets for priority development. About 300
of these markets have been improved during the last ten years. These separate elements of the rural
transport and trading system are further discussed below.
1.8    At the national level, in 1992/93, the share of passenger traffic carried by roads is 75%, IWT
13% and railways is 12%: the share of freight-km traffic is 61% by roads, 32% by IWT and 7% by
railways. In the case of rural transport, road transport plays the major role in the rural movement of
people and goods, carrying over 60% of all traffic. Water transport holds the next largest share, and
particularly during the monsoon when many areas are flooded and the water level is high for easy
navigation. Rail transport is of only marginal significance in rural areas. More details on the level,
structure and pattem of rural traffic is presented in Document 1 (PID-1) in the Project Implementation
Documents (PIDs) (see Annex 4.1).
The Road Network
1.9   Responsibility for the total road network in the country is divided between the Roads and
Highways Department (RHD) of the Ministry of Communications, which focuses on the national and
regional roads, and the Local Govermnent Engineering Department (LGED) of the Ministry of Local
Govermment, Rural Development and Co-operatives (MLGRD&C), which focuses on rural and feeder
roads. The RHD-managed network comprises:
National Highways:         Connecting  the  national capital Dhaka with  Divisional
headquarters and other important urban centers, ports, and
international highways.
Regional Highways:         Connecting regional (greater district) centers and District
headquarters with the national highways.
Feeder Roads Type A (FRA): Connecting Thana headquarters to the regional and national
(sub-regional roads)       highway system.
1.10   There are about 5,500 km of National and Regional Highways, predominantly bitumen-
surfaced, and about 10,000 km of FRAs mostly paved with bitumen or brick surface. There are many
unbridged river crossings on RHD roads but vehicle ferries serve these gaps. Improvement of the
management of the road network under RHD is being pursued currently under various  donor-
supported projects initiated by GOB, with the leading projects setting the framework agenda being the
IDA-supported Roads Rehabilitation and Maintenance Projects 1&2, (Credits 1827-BD and 2638-BD)
which are currently making good progress.
1.11   The Local Government road network, i.e. the rural road network, most of it managed through
the LGED which is the technical body established to assist the local bodies in infrastructure planning,
construction and maintenance, comprises:



4
Feeder Roads Type B (FRB): Connecting growth centers (important rural markets described
below) to the regional road network (RHD roads) or to the
Thana  headquarters.   (Prescribed  Standards:  7.32  m
embankment crest width; 3.7 m carriageway width).
Rural Roads Type 1 (RR1):   Connecting local markets and Union headquarters with the
Thana headquarters or the 'road system' (i.e. FRBs or RHD
roads).  (Standards: Embankment crest width 4.87  m;
carriageway width 3.05 m).
Rural Roads Type 2 (RR2):   Connecting villages and farms to local markets and Union
headquarters. (Embankment crest width 3.72 m).
Rural Roads Type 3 (RR3):   Roads within villages. (Embankment crest width: 2.44 m).
These roads are intended to be managed by the local governments, but currently the local governments
are very weak (see para. 1.30), and therefore LGED plays a leading role in their improvement and
maintenance.
1.12   Although classified as 'roads', most RR3s are footpaths within villages and should be strictly
excluded from the category of engineered roads, particularly for purposes of comparison with other
countries. Excluding RR3s (which are managed and maintained by the UPs), the length and surface
condition of the LGED road network is as follows:
Feeder Roads Type B (FRB): About 8,400 km of which 23% paved with bitumen or brick,
the remainder earthen.
Rural Roads Type 1 (RRI):   About 38,000 km, predominantly earthen.
Rural Roads Type 2 (RR2):   About 49,000 kIn, predominantly earthen.
LGED is currently revising the road classification in the light of the recent increase in the number of
classified growth center markets from 1400 to 2100 (which should also increase the number of roads
designated as growth -center connecting roads or FRBs).  In view of the importance of road
classification for investment prioritization, it was agreed during negotiations that GOB would review
with IDA by September 30. 1997. road classification revisions made by LGED. and would take into
account IDA's views while finalizing the revised classification.
1.13   While the length of the rural road network is impressive, their technical quality is very poor.
Most of the LGED roads were first built as earthen embankments using Food For Work Program
(FFW), with no funds for mechanical compaction or provision of structures so that, while providing an
important service to rural areas through partial use, a large part of the network is less than fully
functional as roads providing unbroken and all-season mobility. Roads selected as FRBs (growth -
center connecting) which have generally high present or potential traffic, require additional earthworks
for raising them above flood level, bridges and culverts to provide drainage, and surfacing for smooth
flow of motorized and non-motorized traffic. A particular problem on FRB, RR1 and RR2 roads is the
lack of cross-drainage structures (culverts and bridges).  Additional cross-drainage structures are
needed on many embankmnents to allow balancing of water levels during floods, and there are a large
number of 'gaps' in embankments that have never been bridged. Further, many of the existing
structures are in poor condition, or are damaged and require immediate repair. According to a recent
survey, even the higher priority Feeder-Roads Type B are in very poor condition; only 17% of them



5
are in "good" condition, and only 23% of them are paved roads the rest being mainly earthroads
without any hard surface. Of the lower category rural roads (RR1 and RR2) about 97% are poor
quality earth roads, with about 35,000 gaps needing bridging or culverts (PID-1 gives more details on
road conditions). These gaps force closure of roads in rainy season and block smooth flow of traffic in
fair season. While FRBs need expensive improvements to bring them to adequate standards, in the
case of RI and R2 rural roads (and R3 roads), which is extensive, the prime need is to construct these
missing drainage structures, since the level of traffic on these roads can generally function without
major earth works and other surface improvements. For the whole network, the focus should be and is
on selective improvement of existing roads, with no new road construction.
The Road Vehicle Fleet and Non-Motorized Transport
1.14   A characteristic of the road transport system  in Bangladesh is the co-existence, and
complementarity, of a growing mechanized vehicle fleet with large numbers of non-motorized transport
vehicles (NMT), and the overall larger share of rural traffic carried in NMT. The motor vehicle fleet
has grown quite rapidly, but non-motorized means of road transport remain important and the numbers
of cycle rickshaws and bicycles continue to grow. The motor vehicle fleet in 1994 comprised about
0.35 million vehicles, equivalent to one per 350 persons, and grew by 40% from 1989 to 1994, an
annual growth rate of 6.9%; the major growth was in numbers of auto-rickshaws (which doubled
over the five-year period) and motor cycles which now account for nearly half the total motorized fleet.
(PID-1 gives more details).
1.15   The role of non-motorized transport remains very important for movement of people and goods
in rural areas, complementing the expanding services provided by motor vehicles and reaching down to
lower levels of the road network. Because of the informal nature of the operations, detailed and
reliable official statistics are not available. The main rural non-motorized means of transport, the
estimated numbers in use and their functions are summarized below: (a) Bicycles: There are about
1.5 million, 80% of them in rural areas, and they are owned by nearly 8% of households. They are
used for personal transport, and to carry goods, including bulky loads, on rural roads and earthen
paths; (b) Cycle rickshaws, which consist of passenger rickshaws (or simply rickshaws) and rickshaw
vans are pedal tricycles fitted with a two seater, passenger-carrying body (though they are also often
used to carry goods). Rickshaw 'vans' are similar to passenger rickshaws but used mainly for goods
fitted with a general-purpose flat body that can carry goods and/or passengers, and more robust
wheels. The current stock of rural rickshaws is conservatively estimated at about 300,000. Rickshaws
are found throughout the country, but rickshaw vans are more common in the west and north-west, and
are increasing in number and progressively spreading eastward. Passenger rickshaws and rickshaw
vans operate on rural roads including unimproved earth roads, albeit often with difficulty. They
provide the most extensive transport services in rural areas, and reach further down the system than
motorized vehicles. Rickshaw vans are better adapted to movement of both goods and people, and
provide a cheaper service for passengers. Rural rickshaw pulling provides employment for about
400,000 poor men. According to a recent survey by the Bangladesh Bureau of Statistics, rickshaw's
share in yearly value addition is 30 percent of the transport sector's total contribution to the GDP; and
(c) Bullock carts: There are over 600,000 bullock carts, though there is evidence that numbers are
declining. They are concentrated in the higher, drier regions of the west and north-west. They tend to
be owned by larger farmers, and are used to move agricultural cargoes between the home and the fields
and on-road, and are also operated for-hire. The large diameter wheels with narrow width steal rims
damage paved roads at a faster rate. Recent studies show (see para. 1.49, PID-1) that both motorized
and non-motorized vehicles increased by about over 140% following road improvement.
1.16   The road designs under ongoing projects, and even more so under the proposed RRMIMP-2,
take into account the special needs of the non-motorized traffic, in providing smoother riding surface,



6
hard shoulders (so that passenger rickshaws, rickshaw-vans and bicycles can easily give way to
motorized traffic to pass, without falling into a lower level shoulder), and in the provision of parking
and access in markets, as well as road safety training for operators.  The proposed project also
includes a pilot component to help improve the design of the NMT vehicles in use with a view to
improve their safety, ridability and efficiency.
The Water Transport System
1.17   Bangladesh has more than 24,000 km of perennially and seasonally navigable waterways.
Historically, the main means of transport was by non-mechanized, wooden country boats. The
Bangladesh Inland Water Transport Authority (BIWTA) is responsible for the conservancy and
maintenance of some 6,000 km of waterway; for important inland ports, launch facilities and ferries;
and for the regulation of some 4,500 registered vessels. Below this level the informal, rural inland
water transport sector is much more extensive, comprising of many thousands of kilometers of smaller
waterways; many river jetties/ghats (boat landing, unloading and loading sites), though lacking basic,
safe, facilities; and rural ferry, passenger and cargo transport services provided by country boats.
1.18   The number of country boats is variously estimated at between 730,000 and 1.8 million
including large numbers of small, domestic boats. Of most significance are the 200,000 cargo and
100,000 passenger boats operating commercially on inland waterways. The operation of country boats
has suffered from siltation of some waterways; creation of barriers on some navigation channels
through construction of road embankment and low-level bridges; a perception that they are unreliable
and insecure; and increasing competition from the developing rural road transport system. However in
the 1980s there was a rapid process of mechanization of commercial boats, using low-cost diesel
engines imported to drive irrigation pumps - about 80% of commercial boats are now mechanized.
This has revived the country boat sector, making the boats faster, more reliable, more secure, and
more competitive with road transport. Efforts have also been made to increase the visibility and
'legitimacy' of the country boat sector through the formation of the Bangladesh Country Boat Owners
Association (BCBOA).
1.19   An ongoing IDA-supported IWT project (Credit No. 2232-BD) is currently addressing major
issues in institutional reforms and strengthening, training, navigation safety equipment and facilities,
dredging of river channels, and river port improvements, though its main focus is on the "formal"
sector of classified navigation channels and ports under the control of BIWTA, referred above. The
proposed RRMIMP-2 would provide improved facilities in many river ghats in the project area to serve
the informal boats sector and also provide support to BCBOA for assisting the country boat sector
through better information sharing and safety education. (See paras. 2.26 and 2.32).
The Rural Market Network
1.20   Bangladesh has an extensive network of about 8,000 rural markets, or 'hats', comprising:
*     6,000 primary markets, used primarily by farmers selling surplus produce to local
consumers and local traders.
*      1,500 local assembly markets, used by farmers and local traders exchanging with
intermediary traders who move agricultural produce up to higher levels of the
marketing system, and for the sale of foodstuffs, agricultural inputs and retail goods.



7
*     about 450 secondary markets, typically serving a Thana.  They are used by
intermediary traders exchanging with large buyers, and are focal points for wholesale
and retail sale of agricultural and non-agricultural goods and services.
1.21   Complex, multi-stage trading systems prevail in rural Bangladesh for the accumulation and
evacuation of surplus agricultural produce and the distribution of 'imported' goods. Crops and other
goods change hands several times between producer and final consumer, and different categories of
trader are present at different levels of the marketing system. The more important markets are
characterized by the presence of permanent and semi-permanent structures including shops, storage
facilities, crop processing enterprises, banks and post offices. Many of the more important markets are
centers for the trading of a wide variety of agricultural produce and other goods, but some focus on the
exchange of large quantities of particular commodities such as rice, cattle, or locally-produced cloth.
1.22  In 1984 the GOB adopted a policy of identifying selected important markets as growth centers,
to be focal points for rural development where investments in rural economic and social infrastructure
should be concentrated (see para. 1.43). 1,408 rural local assembly and secondary markets were
designated as growth centers by the Planning Commission in 1984 selected with participation by the
local authorities based on a set of guidelines that included the revenue potential, trading volume,
area/population served, and a minimum distance between neighboring growth centers. In 1994 an
additional 700 markets were selected by the Planning Commission, reflecting population growth and
regional growth, giving a current total of about 2,100 growth centers. Each Thana has at least three
growth centers, some more; there is about one growth center for every two Union Parishads.
Market Organization and leasing
1.23   The markets (market facilities for public use) are managed by the Union Parishads under which
they are located, through Union Market Management Committee (UMMC) which is responsible for
operation and maintenance. (Annex 1.1 gives more details related to this section). The UMMC has
members from local market users, from the UP, and Thana level officials. Most market operations are
on designated market days (about two days in a week), when the markets operate at the busiest level,
or in some cases, on all days. The market vendors (mostly small farmers and poor traders) use
markets on short-term daily basis, and permanent shops are not allowed on the public market area,
though many permanent private shops develop and prosper around these public market centers. Each
market is leased out, for operations and collection of user charges from vendors, to the highest bidder
based on a competitive sealed tender system. The unit market rates or market tariff (per bag, basket,
or weight of goods sold) are fixed by Government at a uniform level for all markets in a District; these
tariff are increased from time to time. Developed markets earn more revenue with the increase of
turnover of goods transacted, as these markets attract more vendors and customers. The lease-holder
is responsible for regular cleaning of the market, while the UMMC is responsible for supervision of
operations by the lease-holder, and for regular maintenance and development of the market.
1.24   The market lease amount is distributed among UP, Thana Development and Coordination
Committee (TDCC, see para. 1.30), and GOB according to a prescribed formula. 15% of the lease
value is allocated for maintenance to the UMCC and another 10 % to the Thana for development and
emergency repairs after possible floods etc. (see Attachment 4, Annex 1.1). These provisions fully
cover operations and maintenance needs to keep the developed markets functioning on a sustainable
basis. Data from markets developed under RRMIMP-1 show that on an average, market improvements
of a medium-size market cost about Taka 2.4 million (about US$60,000); an average medium-size
developed market yields a lease revenue of about Taka 200,000 and needs an estimated Taka 18,000
for regular maintenance. The balance of the lease revenue, reflecting a positive financial return on



8
investment, goes to the UP, TDCC and GOB for use in other development works. A study of 28
markets completed under RRMIMP-1 shows a wide variety of lease income levels: The average lease
income for all of them was Taka 850,000, with the following break-up: for the 9 large markets, the
average lease income was Taka 2,300,000; for the 10 medium markets, it was Taka 208,000, and for
the 9 very small markets (which also cost less), the average was Taka 64,000. As discussed below,
there is scope for increasing lease income by improving the leasing processes.
1.25   The market development, which provides basic facilities for hygiene and cleanliness (for
slaughter of animals for sale of meat, for drainage, garbage disposal, clean water, and sale of food
produce and perishable goods under sheds on a paved platform rather than on muddy ground) is a
valuable social investment in public health considerably benefiting the mostly poor traders and vendors
who use the markets. UPs and GOB do not favor the idea of a higher tariff level (tariff per unit of
quantity sold, such as per basket, bag, etc.), as this may drive the vendors to use unauthorized,
undeveloped and unhygienic locations along roadside, losing revenue for UPs and health benefits for
the public; they point out that higher revenue from increased turn-over, using the same tariff level
would be adequate for cost recovery, particularly since a 30-40% increase has been observed in lease
values after development of markets due to increased turnover. Moreover, the present system allows
full recovery of operation and maintenance costs, with minor exceptions. Field Studies also show that
the lease holders actually get much higher revenue (often about 100% more) than the bid lease value,
due to possible collusiveness in the bidding process; and the lease holders often charge more than the
fixed unit tariff from the poor vendors. With a view to increase revenue from the markets for use in
other development works, without hurting the poor vendors who are the main market users, the project
proposes measures to increase transparency and competition in the bidding process to increase lease
value by squeezing the surplus profit made by the lease holder (see para. 1.35); there will be also more
user participation in managing the markets better.
C. INSTITUTIONAL FRAMEWORK FOR RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS
The Administrative Structure
1.26   Bangladesh is divided administratively into six Divisions (Dhaka, Barisal, Chittagong, Khulna,
Rajshahi and the newly created Sylhet Division) plus four Metropolitan urban areas (Dhaka,
Chittagong, Khulna and Rajshahi). The Divisions are divided into Districts and then into Thanas (and
Pourashavas or Municipalities for urban localities within the Districts), Unions and Villages. The
characteristics of the rural administrative structure are summarized as follows:
64 Districts (also         average population, 1.8 million
known as Zilas):           average land area, 2,300 sq.km
460 Thanas (formerly       average population, 250,000
known as Upazilas):        average land area, 320 sq.km
4,451 Unions:              average population, 25,000
average land area, 33 sq.km
About                      average population, 1,350
85,000 Villages:           average land area, 1.75 sq.km
1.27   At central government level the Planning Commission develops rural infrastructure policies
and strategic plans. The plan period of the Fourth Five Year Plan (1991-1995) has now ended. In



9
place of Five Year Plans, the Planning Commission has prepared a longer-term fifteen year Perspective
Development Plan which is now under review by the Government. This Perspective Plan emphasizes a
participatory approach to development, and there has been considerable participation from local bodies
and local interests in its preparation. The Ministry of Local Government, Rural Development and
Cooperatives (MLGRD&C) is responsible for rural development and for managing and supervising the
local government system.  It comprises the Local Government Division (LGD) and the Rural
Development Division (RDD). The Local Government Engineering Department (LGED), which is an
agency of the LGD, and has a decentralized set up, plays the most important role in rural infrastructure
development in coordination with the local government bodies.
1.28   Agencies and departments of central government are strongly represented at Divisional,
District and/or Thana level. In addition each Division has a Divisional Commissioner, each District a
Deputy Commissioner (DC), and each Thana a Thana Nirbahi Officer (TNO), all employees of central
government. Thana is the lowest level where central government offices operate.
Local Government Bodies
1.29   When the ongoing RRMIMP-1 project was approved by IDA in 1988, local government in
Bangladesh had an elected body at the level of the Thana (called the Upazilla Parishad) which had
significant responsibilities in many areas of development and social services, including the maintenance
and partly development of rural infrastructure, financed by funding allocated to them from central
Government and revenue raised on their own. The Union Parishads (about ten in each Thana) were
the elected bodies which operated at the Union level, below the Upazilla Parishads. The RRMIMP-1
project had proposed many measures to further improve the operation of the Upazilla Parishads with
regard to rural infrastructure.
1.30   The system of local government changed radically with a legislation passed in 1991 which
abolished the Upazila (thana) Parishads. This also made the local government structure much weaker
than before. The only level of elected local government is now the level of the Union (group of about
twenty villages). The Union Parishad (Council) has existed in one forn or another in Bangladesh since
the 19th century. Union Parishads (UP) have now been given increased powers of local revenue
generation and responsibilities for rural development activities. They are responsible for certain small
infrastructure projects, for maintenance of Rural Roads (except important RR1s for which LGED takes
responsibility) and, through the Union Market Management Committees (UMMC) for operation and
maintenance of markets. The Upazila Parishads have been partially replaced by the establishment of
Thana Development Co-ordination Committees (TDCCs). The Chairmen of each Union Parishad
constitute the voting membership of the TDCC; Thana-level officials of central government agencies,
including the LGED Thana Engineer, sit on the TDCC as non-voting members, the TNO acts as
secretary and local Members of Parliament acts as advisors. The TDCCs are essentially concerned
with co-ordination and monitoring of development project activities within the Thana. More details of
the local government system are given in Annex 1. 1.
1.31   Each UP has ten elected members, (nine ward members plus Chairman) and three women
members elected by the ward members. At present the Union Parishads are weak, and they are still in
the process of adapting their new responsibilities under the revised local government system. The
physical area of each UP is very small (about 30 sq. km. and a population of about 25,000), which
limits their involvement in planning of certain types of infrastructure development. They have very
limited executive staff capacity (one Union Secretary), so that the work of the UP must be done by its
elected members.



10
1.32   UPs are also financially weak, their average annual income, though widely varying among
them, being only about US$16,000 equivalent, from all sources: from directly raised revenue, share of
market leases, and the annual "Block Grant" from central government which is allocated based on
some socio-economic criteria for spending under pre-fixed guidelines. UPs have significant local
revenue raising powers but at present collection performance is very poor, averaging only about Taka
5 per adult per annum. A proportion of the revenue from the annual leasing of smaller markets is
channeled directly to the UPs, while a proportion of that from larger markets is channeled to the
TDCC to be allocated to development activities in the Unions. At present the major source of revenue
for development activities is the GOB Annual Development Program (ADP) Block Grant for Union
and Thana Development. This amounts to an average of Taka 0.45 million per Union in FY95/96 and
is channeled through the TDCC so that decisions over its allocation to development projects are made
by the UP Chairmen; 25-60% of the Block Grant is earmarked for work on roads, culverts and bridges
in the Unions. (Annex 1.1 gives more details on the financial status and revenue distribution).
1.33   Union Parishads are already involved in the planning and implementation of infrastructure
development activities including the LGED/CARE RMP, to which they contribute 10% of the cost; the
LGED/CARE RRNC project, to which they contribute 10% of the cost of cross-drainage structures;
the LGED/WFP GCCR program; and GOB food for work schemes (see paras. 1.37 and 1.38). The
powers of the UPs to implement infrastructure projects are limited by law. Except for earthworks and
tree plantation they can only contract for works up to Taka 50,000 in value. Infrastructure works in
excess of this value, including those financed from the Block Grant, are tendered through a Thana
Tender Committee (on which the concerned UP Chairman sits), contracts are let by the LGED Thana
Engineer, and the funds are managed by the TNO and the Thana Engineer. This system gives good
control and accountability for project funds.
1.34   There is considerable potential to strengthen the capability of the Union Parishads and related
bodies. GOB and LGED have recently taken some measures to increase local participation within the
existing local government framework and LGED has taken leadership to help increase their planning
capability through the introduction of the Union Plan Book. Much more needs to be done to develop
their capacity for effective operation and maintenance of markets and ghats; the proposed project
would assist in this regard. The most important issue is to increase the financial resources of the
Union Parishads, so that they can hire staff to take on specific tasks, and mobilize funds for
improvement and maintenance of local-level infrastructure. The proposed project addresses this issue,
and several measures have been identified to increase local financial resources.
1.35   One of the main financial issues affecting the project would be that the UPs, which are the
lowest level elected Government in the country have a weak financial basis, and are unable to find
adequate funding for maintaining the infrastructure under their jurisdiction on a sustainable basis and
for constructing essential drainage structures to fill gaps on their roads. A related issue is the poor
arrangements for revenue collection from growth center markets and an inadequate distribution system
of the market lease revenue which gives a very low share of income to the UPs and does not leave
enough incentives for UPs to improve their management. In dealing with these issues, the project
includes two sets of actions: First, up-front actions completed before Board presentation of the project;
second, follow-up measures within about 24 months after Board presentation in the light of future
studies funded under the project.
(a)    The up-front actions (all of these have been completed by October 1996) are the
following: (i) Government reduced its share of market lease revenue from the present
35% to 25% and allowed the additional 10% to go directly to the UPs (this may add
average US$500 equivalent, to UP annual revenue); (ii) Government allocated half of
the land-transfer-tax revenue going now to the Zila Parishad (district), to the UPs



11
generating this revenue, compared to none at present (average allocation from this
source is currently about 10 million Taka per district). Therefore, average increase in
UP revenue from half of this reallocation will be about US$1,800 equivalent). Since
land value increases with improved rural infrastructure, this is a good way of levying
user charges for maintenance; (iii) Government increased the lease value threshold for
UP-managed markets from the current value of Taka 50,000 to Taka 100,000, keeping
the present 40% allocation of lease revenue to the UPs; and (iv) More transparent
procedures and wide based competition have been introduced to reduce collusion in
market leasing, with better information sharing arrangements, publicity arrangements,
tendering done by district level agencies and appropriate user participation under
agreed guidelines. This should apply at least to the developed markets in the project
area. Measures (i) to (iii) may increase the average UP income by about 15% from its
present level of about US$16,000 equivalent. The impact of these measures on central
Government's revenue will be negligible (a reduction of about 0.3% from the present
annual revenue of Taka 141 billion.
(b)   In addition to above interim measures, a study on improving revenue mobilization by
UPs (para. 2.32) including better system for ghats and markets management and
leasing should be completed by about December 1997.  It was agreed during
negotiations that GOB would complete the study on UPs Financial Resource
Mobilization by December 31. 1997 and, in consultation with IDA. prepare a plan of
action based upon the study results, and carry it out by December 31. 1998.
The Local Government Engineering Department (LGED) - (The project implementation agency)
1.36   LGED is currently one of the remarkably successful organizations in the Government sector in
Bangladesh. LGED originated as a small cell in the LGD in the late 1970s. It has evolved, through
the formation of the Works Program Wing in 1982 and of the Local Government Engineering Bureau
in 1984 (which was upgraded to a Department in 1992), into a large and dynamic engineering agency.
LGED has its headquarters in Dhaka, and its infrastructure works are executed though fully established
offices in each of the 64 Districts, each headed by an Executive Engineer (XEN), and in each of the
460 Thanas, each headed by a Thana Engineer (TE). LGED has a decentralized set-up, with an
engineering staff of about 700, and works closely with UPs and TDCCs, and more recently has been
trying to build in more community participation in its operations. Almost all works are contracted out
to private contractors and in the case of some small works to worker groups, with LGED providing
overall management and supervision on behalf of central and local governments.  The present
organization structure of LGED, and its staffing, is in Annex 1.2.
1.37  In accordance with the 1984 Strategy for Rural Developments Projects LGED is implementing
a portfolio of donor-supported RDPs in different regions of the country (see Annex 1.3, Table 1),
primarily concerned with improvement of the rural road and market network. Up to end FY1994/95,
some 2,300km of FRB have been improved, about 33,000 running metres of bridges and culverts
constructed, and about 300 growth center markets improved, under these projects.
1.38   The RDPs are complemented by the following food-aided rural development projects, also
implemented through LGED:
*  The WFP-supported growth center Connecting Roads Program (GCCR) which constructs
improved FRB earthen embankments (about 4,000km to date) and small FRB cross-
drainage structures.



12
*   A nationwide medium and large bridges construction program financed by UNSAID PL-
480 monetized food-aid.
*   The LGED/CARE Road Maintenance Project (RMP), recently transferred from  the
Ministry of Relief. This national program uses monetized CIDA food-aid to support the
maintenance of Rural Roads by teams of assetless women.
*   The Rural Road Network Component (RRNC) of the USAID-supported Integrated Food
for Development (IFFD) program. This national program, implemented in partnership
with CARE, supports earthworks improvements and the construction of small cross-
drainage structures on Rural Roads,
*   The EC-supported Integrated Food-Assisted Rural Development Project (IFADEP).
LGED implements the IFADEP component concerned with improvement of earthern
growth center Connecting Roads.
1.39   The rural road and market related infrastructure development and maintenance projects under
the responsibility of LGED, as listed in Annex 1.3. Tables 1 and 2, have a total estimated cost of
about US$1.3 billion with estimated expenditure (foreign and local) in FY95/96 of about US$180
million. This excludes allocations for rural road maintenance (US$16.3 million in 1995/96) also
handled through LGED.
1.40   LGED's broader infrastructure responsibilities include small-scale water resources, irrigation
and flood control, rural water supply and sanitation, urban infrastructure development in secondary
and rural towns, and construction of cyclone shelters and primary schools. LGED contributes to the
generation of productive employment by emphasizing the use of labor-intensive methods for
construction and maintenance works wherever appropriate, including the employment of labor groups
known as Labor Contracting Societies (LCS, organized by workers as contracting firms for small
works).
1.41   Many of the donor-supported projects in LGED have technical assistance inputs which aim to
develop planning, implementation and management capability.   The SIDA/NORAD  financed
Institutional Support Project (ISP) provides long-term support focusing on physical planning and
mapping, technical design and management, and training. The proposed third phase of ISP (1996-
2000) was appraised in late 1995. As part of RDP-18 the ADB is financing long-term technical
assistance on maintenance management, training, and the Management Capability Strengthening Study
(MANCAPS) for LGED. The institutional components for strengthening LGED proposed under
RRMIMP-2 are designed in close coordination with these efforts by SID/NORAD, ADB and other
agencies.
1.42   LGED is recognized by all funding agencies as efficient in managing and implementing
projects in co-ordination with donors. The first phase of MANCAPS has produced an action plan for
the management strengthening of LGED1 which concludes that the organization is effective and
successful, and is characterized by team work, field orientation, dynamic leadership and hard work.
However, following a period of rapid growth LGED personnel are now overloaded with work, and
this has led to a number of ad hoc organizational arrangements. As LGED reaches maturity and enters
a new phase of development it is important to consolidate and strengthen the organization. MANCAPS
I          Draft Action Plan - Directions in Management Development of LGED. MANCAPS, March
1995.



13
therefore recommends a comprehensive re-organization of LGED including a new level of management
between the Districts and headquarters so as to reduce the present span of control under which about
80 officials report directly to the Chief Engineer; strengthening of the headquarters management
structure; the integration of ISP functions into the established structure including the creation of a
Design Cell; and the strengthening of planning, monitoring and evaluation, management information
systems (MIS) functions. The MANCAPS recommendations, and the proposed organization structure,
are in Annex 1.2. The RRMIMP-2 Appraisal Mission agreed with many of the MANCAPS
recommendations and, in co-ordination with inputs from other financing agencies and GOB, a number
of them will be implemented and financed under the project in order to contribute to the management
strengthening of LGED. In addition, the project would also reinforce LGED's ongoing efforts in
making it a participatory organization with active involvement of project beneficiaries, by
incorporating participation specialists and training in its organization (see paragraphs 2.33 and 3.1).
D. RURAL INFRASTRUCTURE STRATEGY
1.43   The Government has followed a consistent strategy in recent years to improve rural
infrastructure on a selective basis while also improving its highway network, with emphasis on better
integration of the transport sector investments and policy, and better coordination among transport
agencies, such as RHD and LGED. GOB published a "Rural Development Strategy" in 1984, which
enunciated a strategy based on focusing resources to develop "growth centers" with maximum potential
for growth  in rural areas, selected on the basis of clearly defined criteria of socio-economic
importance and development potential; this also defined Government's rural infrastructure strategy,
directed to help growth center development as its main focus. The rural infrastructure strategy as
defined in the Five Year Plans, in line with the above Rural Development Strategy, has been to focus
on improving: (a) Type B feeder roads (FRBs) (also called growth - center- connecting roads) which,
by definition, connect selected "growth centers" in sub-districts (Thana) to thana headquarters or the
nearest all-weather road; there are 2100 "growth centers" in the country; (b) selected other rural roads
(Rl, R2), which connect villages with growth centers or feeder roads; (c) provision of drainage
structures (culverts/bridges) on important rural roads; and (d) market structures and facilities in the
growth centers. The "growth center connecting roads" is a good concept in selectivity, this being
treated as the core of rural roads; though classified as FRBs, they are mostly earth roads and need
upgrading into reasonable minimum quality standards to provide expected service. Similarly, the
selected growth center markets form a core group of high priority, from among about 8,000 local
markets. The emphasis has been on improving existing facilities, with minimal new construction,
particularly in the case of roads. In the light of a recent review of the working of this strategy which
basically endorsed its continuation (see para. 1.45 below) the Government is now continuing with this
rural infrastructure strategy into the future, through the Perspective Development Plan for the Country
(1995-2010) recently formulated by the National Planning Commission.
1.44   Recent Progress: The 1984 Strategy Paper envisaged a 10-year infrastructure improvement
program (1985-1995) with construction/upgrading of 6,400 km of FRBs, 9,600 km of rural roads,
132,000 running meters of culverts/bridges, and 1,400 markets. This infrastructure program is being
implemented mainly through some eight different ongoing projects (and some completed projects)
(called Rural Development Projects - RDPs, please see Annex 1.3. Tables 1 and 2. listing ongoing
RDPs), spread over the country, all with financial assistance from aid-agencies (including IDA, and
ADB: IDA has an ongoing project, this proposed one being a follow-up project; ADB has completed
one project, and has recently launched a second project). Many of these ongoing projects are regional
projects focusing on different regions, while two are national projects (one funding rural bridges in the
country, financed by USAID, and another, funded by Swedish aid, providing technical assistance and
institutional support to LGED for improving planning and implementation of rural road programs in
coordination with the local Government agencies.  Separate regional projects allow  separate



14
procurement and other rules applicable to different funding agencies, make projects more manageable,
and the projects are well-coordinated by the GOB and LGED and fit into national and regional
priorities, as shown by the results of a recent review (see para. below). LGED has five additional
national programs fully funded by Government, focusing on rural infrastructure development. The
total cost of all these ongoing projects is about US$1,300 million equivalent, of which infrastructure
components (roads and markets and related technical assistance) are about US$1,100 million
equivalent. LGED is recognized by all funding agencies as efficient and competent in managing these
projects. These projects are progressing satisfactorily and have already produced good impact on the
rural areas where they serve, as demonstrated by impact studies. However, in terms of physical
progress, these projects have collectively been able to achieve so far only about 30% of national targets
for 1995 fixed 10 years ago, due to (1) the ambitious nature of these targets which were not matched
by resources and projects needed to meet them, and (2) the slow start of many projects due to the
learning process in building up institutional and contractor capacity for efficient implementation. This
has left huge unmet demands for improvement, further increased by population growth. This, and the
generally poor condition of rural infrastructure in the country, underline the need to continue efforts at
a faster pace.
1.45   A review of the GOB's rural infrastructure strategv and performance was recently undertaken
jointly by IDA, LGED and GOB agencies, in consultation with other domestic stakeholders and
cofinanciers (see Bangladesh Rural Infrastructure Strategy Study by IDA/LGED, February 1996; BD-
SR-15732). The main conclusion of the review is that the current strategy's growth center approach
remains valid and no major changes are required; only some readjustments or 'fine tuning' may be
justified in the light of the experience acquired by the different Rural Development Projects, as
discussed below: Targets will have to be reset after the recent increase from 1400 to 2100 growth
centers; and some minor readjustments will be needed in the spatial distribution of infrastructure
investments to be fully in line with agricultural production and potential. The review recommends,
further, to put more emphasis on user/community participation in planning, implementation and
monitoring and in funding; improved use of local resources such as local materials and the use of labor
based techniques; coordination in the use of complementary modes of transportation, specifically
waterways; increasing the role of the private sector, strengthening contractor capacity, and use of
bigger contracts (compared to average 2-3 km contracts now); and, institutional strengthening of
LGED and its wide network at local levels with a greater orientation towards community participation.
The review emphasizes greater selectivity in investments in keeping with priority needs, and much
greater emphasis on building and funding a sustainable maintenance system. The study has established
that rural infrastructure investments have produced significant impact and that "improved roads and
markets" are strongly correlated with higher agricultural production. A spatial analysis (presented in
the report) has demonstrated that past investment in rural roads and markets through the growth -center
based strategy has responded well to the regional location of demand for services, demonstrating that
the strategy has led to rational distribution of rural road investments.
1.46   The strategy study also estimates that larger investment than in the past decade and the current
annual level would be needed to meet essential needs of estimated demand for improved infrastructure.
Currently, Bangladesh spends about 5.5% of its development budget (based on foreign and local
resources) on rural roads and markets development (about 5.5 billion Taka; see Annex 1.3). The
strategy study notes that to achieve a modest target of meeting about 70% of the current infrastructure
improvement needs over the next 10 years, a total of Taka 63 billion would be needed (about US$1.5
billion in 1995 prices), indicating an annual development expenditure of about Taka 16.3 billion (about
US$155 million) for the next 10 years. In addition, maintenance cost will have to go up from the
present requirement of 1,300 million Taka to about 2,900 million Taka by year 2006.  Using
cumulative annual average maintenance for the next five years, these figures indicate an annual
resource requirement of about 8.1 billion Taka (6.3 billion Taka for development and 1.8 billion Taka



15
for average annual maintenance) over the next five years, followed by similar development and more
maintenance funding in the subsequent 5 years, for reaching the goal of improving 70% of the existing
network by year 2005. Resources available at present are about 6.8 billion annually (54% foreign,
46% domestic) (Annex 1.3). This underlines the need for raising more resources, particularly from
domestic resources.
1.47   GOB has accepted in principle the changes suggested above (para. 1.45) in future infrastructure
strategy, and the proposed project, in particular, is designed on the basis of the findings of the above
strategy review.
E. MAINTENANCE AND SUSTAINABILITY
1.48   To ensure sustainability of the rural infrastructure being improved, it is important to build up a
good maintenance system and a viable funding mechanism based on local resources, particularly, in
view of the increased maintenance needs estimated above (para. 1.45).   GOB has accepted this
principle and has recently allocated adequate funds for maintenance. The LGED has set up a national
maintenance system but this system needs to be made operationally more efficient, which is sought to
be done through the proposed project. Currently funds for maintenance of most rural roads and
highways come from domestic Government revenue and some UP revenue (the amount collected from
road users through fuel levies and other charges is estimated to be adequate to cover maintenance needs
of the total road network); only in the case of Rural roads (Rl, R2 and R3) the foreign-funded Food-
for-Work program funds part of maintenance through the Road Maintenance Program (RMP) funded
by Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA); this is a well-designed program targeting
destitute women as workers, and with participation of the UPs in road selection and in financial
contribution of minimum 10% of the maintenance costs (see para. 2.15) for maintenance allocations.
This foreign aid program which covers about 44% of total maintenance funding may be expected to
continue for some years as this is an effective poverty -reduction program and may therefore receive
priority in the aid programs for Bangladesh, one of the poorest countries in the world. During Credit
negotiations. assurance was obtained from Governrnent to match these funds from own or other
sources, if this RMP program is downsized or discontinued in future. It is important for sustainability
and ownership that the local government resources for maintenance should progressively increase; the
proposed project seeks to do this through measures to enhance the resource base of the UPs (see para.
1.35) and increased Government allocation for maintenance. Maintenance of markets is adequately
financed by direct user charges levied from market users (see para. 1.25), and a similar system would
be introduced also for the developed river jetties under the project. The ongoing use and promotion of
labor-intensive technology for maintenance with appropriate small equipment makes infrastructure
maintenance technology affordable and available in the rural areas, enhancing sustainability, in addition
to generating employment. Assurances on adequate maintenance funding and arrangements in the
project area and at the national level would be obtained during Credit negotiations. (See paras. 2.15,
2.16, and also Annex 2.8 on maintenance details).
F. IMPACT OF RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS DEVELOPMENT
1.49   A major study on the impact of rural infrastructure in Bangladesh was published in 19902.
This surveyed 129 villages which were classified as "developed" and "undeveloped" based on access to
transport, modern inputs, electricity and economic, social and administrative services, and were
2          R. Ahmed and M. Hossain. Developmental Impact of Rural Infrastructure, Bangladesh.
International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI) in collaboration with Bangladesh Institute
of Development Studies (BIDS), 1990



16
comparable in all other respects. The study found that, comparing the two categories, the villages with
"developed" infrastructure were significantly better off as reflected in: higher farm agate prices for
produce, lower fertilizer prices paid, higher house-hold income, higher wage rates benefiting the poor,
and higher level of employment and income for the landless and the poor women.
1.50   Socio-economic monitoring and evaluation (SEM&E) studies carried out under different Rural
development Projects (RDP) implemented by LGED, including the IDA-supported RRMIMP-1,
provide consistent evidence that FRB road improvements:
(a)    generated a substantial increase in the total movement of people and goods: on
improved RRMIMP-1 roads the volume of both passenger and cargo traffic (in
passenger-km and ton-km) has increased by over 70% in the case of cargo and over
174% in the case of passenger traffic within a year of development;
(b)    reduced transport charges: on improved RRMIMP-1 roads unit transport charges for
goods and passengers have decreased by 63 % and 59% respectively (reflecting reduced
operating costs and shift to more efficient modal composition, such as reduced head-
porterage and bullock cart use);
(c)   resulted in a transfer to more efficient modes, with substantial growth  in vehicular
traffic - both non-motorized and motorized -- and a reduction in pedestrian and bullock
cart trips;
(d)   increased the number of both motorized and non-motorized vehicles by 165%  and
146% respectively; and increased the relative share of traffic volumes (tkm and pkm)
carried by motorized traffic: from 47 % share to 73 % share after road development in
the case of cargo, and from 22% to 47% in the case of passenger traffic;
(e)    stimulated the operation of bus services for the first time (except on short FRBs below
about 2 kIn) which bring many social benefits;
(f)   reduced the operating costs and transport charges of rickshaws and rickshaw-vans by
up to 60%, because of the provision of a smooth running surface; and
(g)    produced significant new employment for the poor through direct and indirect impacts.
1.51   The LGED SEM&E studies have also analyzed the impact of improving marketing facilities.
They show that, after improvement, the number of market visitors and the volume and value of goods
traded increases, losses due to spoilage decrease, the lease values (bid prices) of the markets rise, and
new shops and commercial activities start up around the markets, and land value of the areas near the
market increases significantly. The markets become more efficient, and busier and more active trading
and commercial centers.
1.52   Based on a partial analysis (that is, counting only easily quantifiable transport cost savings
from road improvements, and spoilage reduction due to market improvements, and ignoring the several
other economic and social benefits from increased access and mobility such as improved school, health
and banking facilities, and more employment creation particularly for the poor) completion studies
under RRMIMP-1 sub-projects show an average 22% (range 14 - 45%) economic rate of return (ERR)
from road projects, and about the same rate from markets, which shows the very high economic impact
of these investments. They also show major impact in creating employment (see para. 4.3).



17
1.53   The Rural Infrastructure Strategy study (see para. 1.45) also documents the significant
regionwide impact of road and market development on agricultural productivity. Another recent IDA-
sponsored study3 shows significant impact of rural infrastructure investment on non-farm production,
especially trade and services. Another recent IDA-sponsored study shows significant impact of rural
infrastructure investment on on-farm employment. Since SEME impact studies have been focused
mainly on traffic impact and partial benefits, the proposed project would finance a fuller study of the
impact of the road and market investments on poverty, income levels, and agricultural productivity
based on more comprehensive data collection including house-hold surveys and analysis, which should
help guide the massive investments needed in the sector. (Annex 1.4 gives more details on past impact
studies).
G. IDA INVOLVEMENT IN THE SECTOR: LESSONS
1.54   IDA's first major investment in Bangladesh in rural infrastructure development (apart from
infrastructure components in other projects), has been in financing the Rural Roads and Markets
Improvement and Maintenance Project (RRMIMP-1, approved in 1988 with an IDA credit of SDR
45.0 million with cofinancing from KfW and SDC) implemented by LGED, and aimed at improving
rural infrastructure conditions in Districts of the Northwest region, and at institutional strengthening of
LGED. The project comprises the improvement of 500 km of FRB roads to bitumen-surfaced
standard; tree plantation on the sides of the roads; construction of improved public facilities - internal
roads, paved areas, raised and covered selling areas, drainage, potable water supply and latrines - at 65
growth center markets; construction of 4,000 meters of structures on Rural Roads; maintenance of the
inproved roads; and institutional development activities including construction of a new LGED
headquarters building, construction of LGED District offices and laboratories, training, contractor
development, and socio-economic monitoring and evaluation. It also included rehabilitation works
following major floods in 1988. In view of the need to evolve new systems, standards, procedures and
local capacity of LGED, contractors and consultants in planning and implementing rural infrastructure
projects on a sustainable basis, this project was designed and approved as an experimental project in its
early phase, with an implementation period of 8 years. The project faced initial delays in appointing
supervision consultants and disruptions caused by two major floods in the project area, but is now
making excellent progress, has issued all contracts for works, and is expected to complete in June
1997, fully achieving all its physical and most of its institutional objectives, within the original cost
estimates. The project investments have also made significant economic impact in removing rural
mobility and marketing constraints, and shown high economic returns from completed components (see
para. 1.49). The project has been very successful in establishing good quality standards for road
construction with local materials and expertise through trial prograrns, which are now being replicated
nationally, and also in enhancing capacity of LGED and local consultants and contractors. Some
lessons from the experience of this project are: (a) starting bidding processes before project start, and
appointing supervision consultants before Credit approval so as to avoid delays, (b) more local
community participation in priority setting, design and implementation, (c) greater emphasis on rural
road structures, (d) emphasis on river jetties to better integrate inland water transport and road
transport, (e) continued training and equipment support to small contractors but at the same time
supporting increase in contract sizes to enhance contractor output and efficiency (f) early action on land
acquisition with suitable compensation procedures, (h) greater emphasis on field-level training and
implementation of maintenance programs, (i) overhauling rural market management and leasing
systems with greater user participation to improve efficiency and increase revenue and (j) strengthening
the financial status of local bodies (Union Parishads) in ensuring sustainability of infrastructure
3   Khandker, Shahidur R. 1995 'Bangladesh: Rural Non-Farm Growth and Targeted Credit Interventions".
(Memo. The World Bank, Washington. D.C.).



18
maintenance.  These lessons from  the ongoing project and other RDPs have been carefully
incorporated in designing the proposed larger RRMIMP-2, which is designed with a shorter
implementation period of five years since the experimental phase is considered over and much more
prior preparation has been done before start of the project, and LGED has now proven capacity to
implement projects more expeditiously.
1.55  IDA's experience as reflected in the Operations Evaluation Department's (OED's) reports of
similar "regional" rural road projects have shown that they would achieve their developmental
objectives with greater impact only if the project components also include improving national standards
in maintenance, design and planning, and promotion of network-wide maintenance. This has been
taken into account in designing the proposed project by supporting the implementation of an effective
maintenance program in the project area to be replicated nationally and by requiring allocation of
adequate maintenance funds for the national rural road network and designation of maintenance
engineers in LGED's field offices. (see para. 2.16 and 2.17).
1.56  In the overall transport sector, IDA has funded eight projects so far, of which three have been
completed and five are on going. The completed projects are - two highway projects, and one port
project. The road projects faced implementation problems which were resolved, and their Completion
report (1989) highlighted lessons for future action (such as preparation of road master plans for
prioritizing rehabilitation and maintenance, better maintenance systems, improved contracting
procedures, and contractor strengthening) which have been incorporated into the subsequent projects.
The port project was very successful and its Completion Report (1993) highlighted the need for timely
funding of equipment along with berth facilities, and greater emphasis on container facilities; these
lessons are being taken into account in the ongoing port sector studies and port project preparation,
with emphasis on private sector provision of equipment, other facilities and operations. The five
ongoing projects are: (i) Road Rehabilitation and Maintenance Project (RRMP-1 approved in 1987,
with IDA credit of SDR 78.1 million, with cofinancing from OPEC Fund), which will complete in
December 1996, (ii) and RRMP-2 (approved in 1994 with IDA credit of SDR 103.9 million, with
cofinancing from the British Overseas Development Administration (ODA); both these projects focus
on the physical rehabilitation and maintenance needs, and on planning and maintenance systems, and
institutional support, for the national and regional roads managed by RHD (as distinct from rural roads
managed by LGED); (iii) the RRMIMP-1 project for rural roads and markets, already discussed in the
previous paragraph; (iv) the Inland Water Transport Project for which IDA credit of SDR 31.3 million
was approved in 1991; and (v) the Jamuna Bridge Project for which IDA credit of SDR 143.6 million
was approved in 1994, with cofinancing from ADB and OECF. The IWT project faced many initial
problems due to delays in starting important institutional reform studies, which have later been
resolved. All ongoing projects are progressing satisfactorily. IDA has also been involved as executing
agency for a UNDP-funded study on transport sector planning and strategy, which was completed in
1995; Government has accepted its recommendations for follow-up.



19
2. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT ORIGIN AND CONCEPT
2.1    At the Mid-Term Review of the RRMIMP-1 project in May 1992 it was agreed to prepare a
new rural infrastructure Improvement project as a follow-up to continue similar programs. The concept
of the proposed project has been developed during the course of the ongoing project and of IDA's
review of the GOB rural infrastructure strategy, which highlights continued need to invest on
improving rural infrastructure. GOB's and IDA's review of agriculture strategy also emphasizes rural
infrastructure improvements as a priority. Much has been learnt from the experience of the RRMIMP- 1
(see para. 1.53), and from the simultaneous SDC-financed rural development project (RDP-6) in
Manikganj District of Greater Dhaka, and also from other RDPs. During the period of implementation
of these projects the LGED has grown and evolved rapidly as an effective, rural infrastructure
development institution.
2.2    The proposed RRMIMP-2 project will be co-financed with the SDC and will include additional
physical works in the Northwest Region but with greater emphasis on works in the contiguous Greater
Dhaka Area where there has been only limited rural infrastructure investment to date. As noted,
individual projects under RDP series focus on infrastructure needs of particular regions, funded by
different agencies, well coordinated by LGED. The project area for RRMIMP-2 was selected for the
following reasons: (a) continuity with on-going IDA and SDC work; (b) relatively higher poverty
level; (c) absence of any previous RDP in the Greater Dhaka Area (except in one District); and (d)
poor infrastructure conditions in the whole project area.
B. PROJECT OBJECTIVES
2.3    The overall development objective of the proposed project is to help increase rural employment
and incomes and reduce rural poverty by establishing an improved, sustainable rural transport and
trading infrastructure. More specifically, the project objectives would be to:
(a)    help remove physical bottlenecks, improve quality, and reduce costs in rural transport
and marketing;
(b)    create employment and income-generating opportunities among the rural poor, and
particularly for disadvantaged women;
(c)    promote participation of local communities and NGOs in project activities; and
(d)   increase institutional capacity for efficient rural infrastructure management, including
maintenance;
2.4   Performance indicators for monitoring progress in achieving the project objectives are given in
Annex 3.7.
C. RATIONALE FOR IDA INVOLVEMENT
2.5    The project would support GOB's and IDA's overall strategy to increase agricultural
production and rural incomes, and to reduce poverty through removing rural infrastructure constraints.
IDA's Strategy for the Transport Sector and rural infrastructure in Bangladesh follows the 1995



20
Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) discussed by IDA Board in September 1995, which emphasized
improving physical infrastructure, their maintenance, and related services, as they are critical for both
international competitiveness of exports and the growth of agriculture and rural incomes. It also
emphasized, for rural infrastructure, encouraging greater participation by local communities, increasing
resource mobilization, adopting national standards, and strengthening local contractors.  CAS also
stresses labor-intensive growth, and special measures for poverty-alleviation. The proposed RRMIMP-
2, which relies on appropriate labor-intensive technology for most civil works with special provisions
for employing the poor, and which gives high priority for improving rural infrastructure and
community involvement is fully consistent with the CAS.
2.6    The ongoing project, RRMIMP-1, has set many good, new standards in rural road design
specifications and maintenance; it is important to provide continuity by replicating them in other areas
and by building on institutional and policy gains already achieved through the first project so as to
keep the momentum  of progress in rural development going at an accelerated pace.  IDA's
involvement, which is much appreciated by the Government and other donors, will provide such
continuity.  IDA support is needed also to: (a) encourage appropriate technology, cost-effective
standards; and sustainable maintenance systems, (b) attract cofinancing support; and (c) help implement
the findings of the IDA/GOB rural infrastructure study.
D. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
Project Components
2.7    The main components of the project are:
(i)    improvement and upgrading of about 575 km of feeder roads Type B (FRBs) in
the Project Area (14 districts in the Northwest and Greater Dhaka areas, see
para. 2.9), with small bridges, culverts and tree planting alongside the roads.
It also includes bridges and culverts on other FRB roads not selected for full
improvement, and road safety improvements;
(ii)   establishment and  implementation  of a planned  routine  and  periodic
maintenance system for the LGED road network in the project area covering
all FRB roads, important RR1 roads, and structures on rural roads for
replication nationally in the following years;
(iii)   construction of about 6000 culverts and small bridges (about 31,600 linear
meters) of drainage structures on existing rural roads (mainly RR1 & RR2)
(Nationwide), together with ancillary earthworks, which will fill existing gaps
on about 10,500 km of rural roads and make them passable year-round, and
also correct drainage problems created by existing rural roads; this component
will particularly emphasize local participation, use of labor-intensive
technologies, and employment of landless poor and disadvantaged women;
(iv)   improvement of rural markets (i.e., raised and covered sales platforms,
walkways, drainage, potable water supply, sanitation) in about 136 growth
center markets in the project area, and improvement of about 14 UP center
markets in the project area;
(v)    rehabilitation/construction of 41 priority river jetties, or river 'ghats', in the
project area, to improve landing/unloading facilities for country boats;



21
(vi)   pilot program to improve safety and efficiency of NMT, particularly rickshaw
vans and passenger rickshaws, which are extensively used in the country in the
movement of goods and passengers in rural areas, with participation of
appropriate NGOs;
(vii)  supply of equipment for road construction and maintenance, of vehicles for
supervision of works, and of office and training equipment;
(viii)  institutional and human resources development for increasing and strengthening
managerial and technical capacity of LGED, local Government agencies, and
the local construction industry, including support for fiscal and technical
audits; and;
(ix)   implementation support including  technical assistance  for the  design,
supervision, monitoring and evaluation of annual work programs, and support
for a follow-up rural infrastructure project preparation.
2.8   The additional features in this project compared to the first are: addition of river transport and
integration of rural transport; support for improving non-motorized transport; more community
participation, raising more local resources and more emphasis on rural drainage structures on existing
rural roads; and greater emphasis on maintenance, and on adoption of national standards in
construction and maintenance.  The proposed project, in design and concept, will provide the
cornerstone for a new generation of road transport and infrastructure projects; it will build on
experience and lessons learned from the ongoing RRMIMP-1, and will complement and reinforce its
achievements.
Project Area
2.9   The project area (Map IBRD No. 27808) covers 14 Districts of Bangladesh (out of total 64)
comprising: (a) six Districts of Greater Dhaka Area (Map IBRD No.27727) - Manikganj, Munshiganj,
Narayanganj, Gazipur, Narshingdi and Dhaka Districts - of which only Manikganj District has
benefited from other RDPs; and (b) eight districts of the Northwest Region (Map IBRD No. 27726) -
Rajshahi, Naogaon, Natore, Nawabganj, Pabna, Sirajganj, Bogra and Joypurhat Districts - which
already benefit from the ongoing RRMIMP-1 in the Northwest Region. The Northwest Area has 64
Thanas and 608 Union Parishads (UPs), and the Greater Dhaka Area has 33 Thanas and 361 UPs. In
addition, the rural structure component (25 % of the project cost) covers all UPs in the whole country.
2.10   The project area covers about 25,000 sq. km and has a population of about 22 million people
within this area. The Northwest Area is larger (72% of the land area) but less densely populated (62%
of the population) than the Greater Dhaka Area. The construction of the Jamuna Multi-Purpose Bridge
will significantly improve communications between the Northwest Region and the rest of the country,
and create opportunities for economic development. The rural road network in the project area
comprises about 3200 km of FRB, and 29,800 km of rural roads (9,450 km of RR1, 10,150 km of R2
and 10,200 km of R3), and there is a total of 425 growth center markets. By mid-1997, when the
RRMIMP-1 and the SDC financed RDP-6 (which covers Manikganj) projects end, about 31 % of the
