FRB network (39% in the Northwest Region and 14% in the Greater Dhaka Area) will be in good,
bitumen-surfaced condition, and 18% of the growth center Markets (25% in the Northwest Region,
6% in the Greater Dhaka Area) will have been improved. Even after the completion of the proposed
project, the total coverage of rural infrastructure improvement needs in these 14 districts will be about
50-60%.



22
Detailed Features
(a)   Improvement and Upgrading Feeder Roads B: (Base cost US$58.60 million)
2.11   Many FRBs in the project area are earthen embankments - most of them constructed under
food for work programs but without mechanical compaction - often with unspanned 'gaps' across
rivers and flood land. Some FRBs have been paved with Herring Bone Bond brick (HBB) but often
without full compaction of the embankment resulting in a rough riding surface, and a few sections have
been given a bituminous seal. The project would improve and upgrade about 574 km FRB roads (312
km in the Greater Dhaka Area and 262 km are in the Northwest Region on about 69 roads in all),
including construction of 1915 metres of bridges and culverts, to provide all-weather road access to
serve rural growth center markets. In addition to the full improvement and upgrading of FRB roads to
bitumen-surfaced standards (including all necessary structures), the project includes a sub-component
'construction of structures on other FRB roads'. This reflects the need to bridge existing gaps and
replace damaged structures on some of the other FRBs not selected for full upgrading in order to
extend the mobility on the FRB road network in the project area. This would comprise construction
of about 1,750 linear meters of small bridges and structures; two-third of these, will be in the more
riverine Greater Dhaka Area. The project would include provision for improved road safety.
2.12   The FRB roads and structures have been selected using a participatory approach involving
consultation with local bodies - UPs, TDCCs and NGOs - to derive a short-list of roads based on local
priorities, which were then subjected to economic appraisal. All selected roads have an EIRR greater
than 12%. Structures larger than 30 linear meter span will be subjected to economic appraisal before
bidding. The selection procedures and criteria, and implementation procedures and time-frame, using
active involvement of beneficiaries, based on specially designed participatory processes (para 3.1) for
proposed physical investment schemes under various project components are given in Annex 2. 1; these
criteria, procedures and pro grams were agreed during negotiations. Most FRB roads and FRB
structures have been selected; if changes are needed they will follow the agreed selection criteria, and
the changes will be reviewed during mid-term review. The list of proposed FRB roads, markets and
river jetties, is given in Annex 2.2; this was confirmed during negotiations.
2.13   The proposed FRB design standards (PID-3) are based on  the design standards of ongoing
RRMIMP-1, with refinements made in the light of experience for various RDPs and trials. The
pavement structure will comprise an improved sub-grade, coarse aggregate (brick) sub-base and water
bound macadam (WBM) base course using brick aggregate, and a 25 mm bituminous carpeting (BC)
surface. In specific circumstances where the traffic level on FRB is high, the thickness of BC will be
increased to 40 mm provided if it is economically justified, and where the traffic level on FRB is low,
the thickness of the BC will be reduced to 15 mm. The surfacing specification of providing 25 mm BC
under normal traffic conditions, is based on a studies carried out by LGED and the consultants, on the
performance of several trial sections of different specifications observed during the implementation
phase of various RDPs. Furthermore the design of 25 mm BC for surface treatment on FRBs, has
been determined from the extensive discussions held between the experts of IDAISDC and LGED in
the context of all economical and technical implications involved in the selection of pavement
thickness. A special factor to take into account in the context of Bangladesh is the fact that the broken
bricks (used for surfacing in lieu of natural laterite stones due to non-availability of the latter) lacks
plasticity to bond; such paving eliminates the risk of these surfacing materials being washed away in
the heavy rains common in Bangladesh, thus giving longer life and lesser maintenance costs. It also
significantly reduces the roughness of the surface, allowing the predominant cycle-rickshaws (human-
pedaled) easy ridability; transport cost savings justified the additional cost on paving of these important
roads. The project includes the construction of trial sections in order to promote the use of possible
bitumen emulsions, which if successful can prove to be highly cost-effective in the construction of



23
rural roads. The base course will be extended 600 mm on either side of the carriageway to provide a
hard shoulder on both sides. This hard shoulder will protect the earth shoulder from damage during
rains, and will also improve safety particularly under the prevailing conditions where the traffic is mix
of motorized and non-motorized.
2.14   Groups of disadvantaged women will be contracted to plant and take care of the trees alongside
FRB roads and to carry out routine maintenance of the embankment slopes and earth shoulders, thus
creating long-term employment. Based on GOB policy, ownership of the trees will be arranged so that
the income they generate provides revenue for the UPs and gives benefits to the destitute women
caretakers. The project will also finance the construction of a new LGED District
office/laboratory/workshop facility in Dhaka District (which is the one project district where LGED
does not yet have such a facility) and improvement of existing facilities in other Districts.  The
technical standards and unit cost for various road construction activities are given in PID-3.
(b)    Road Maintenance: (Base cost US$10.76 million)
2.15   The project attaches great importance to building an adequate maintenance management system
in the country, based on best practice and techniques. LGED received its first GOB annual revenue
budget for maintenance in FY 1992/93, and the budget has increased progressively since then - Taka
300 million (US$7.5 million) in 1992/93, Taka 400 million (US$10.0 million) in 1993/94, Taka 550
million (US$13.8 million) in 1994/95 and Taka 650 million (US$16.3 million) in 1995/96. A Rural
Infrastructure Maintenance Cell (RIMC) was established within LGED in 1992, and since then it has
made considerable progress in establishing the framework for road maintenance by setting up a road
inventory system, introducing national maintenance guidelines and establishing a training program.
However actual maintenance works in the field is still inadequate in many respects and need
improvements. The other main source for funding maintenance of rural roads is the CIDA/CARE
Road Maintenance Program (RMP), which is designed as a poverty-alleviation program for destitute
women.  To complement and support its efforts for setting up an effective, planned periodic
maintenance system, the project would provide 50% of the periodic maintenance costs in the project
area on a declining percentage basis over the implementation period, starting 80% in the first three
years and decreasing to 30% in the last two years. The project would finance: (a) TA for setting up
and designing an effective and efficient management system in the project area, which would be part of
the DSM consultant services (para. 2.38); and (b) provide partial funding of periodic maintenance in
the project area. This assistance would be provided in coordination with RIMC's program and other
ADB financed inputs for maintenance strengthening.
2.16   Tables 3 and 4 in the Annex 2.8 provides an estimate of the needs and costs (including unit
cost) of routine and periodic maintenance within the project area over the project period.  These
figures will be used for preparing annual maintenance plans and budgets (see para. 2.17). These
amounts will be revised by mutual agreement in the light of further studies planned to be carried out
during project implementation.
2.17   The project also attaches significant importance to the routine and periodic maintenance of the
national rural road network. RIMC is taking leadership in improving national maintenance strategy.
The recently prepared GOB rural infrastructure strategy paper has projected that the country will need
an annual recurrent cost (annualized routine and periodic maintenance costs) of about US$33 million
equivalent in 1995-96, increasing to about US$73 million annually in ten years as the road network is
upgraded requiring more maintenance (See Annex 1.4). Although GOB has increased its maintenance
budget allocations substantially in the current year 1995/96 to US$16.3 million to meet full current
needs compared with the allocations made in previous years (para. 2.15), it will need to make increases
in this amount in future as maintenance capability increases. The estimated maintenance needs for



24
1995-96 are being fully met from various sources, including Government budget allocation for
maintenance, UP contributions, some project inputs and the food-aid-supported Road Maintenance
Program (RMP) funded by Canadian International Development Agency (CIDA) (about US$11 million
for 1996-97), which is a well-designed project targeting income generation for destitute women by
involving them and UPs in a very effective and participatory rural road maintenance program. Based
on some projection of continued UP and other project contributions (see Annex 1.4), and assuming the
continuation of the RMP program, it has been estimated that Government's budget allocation for rural
road maintenance will have to increase as a minimum to about US$21 million in 1997-98 and US$31
million in 2001-02. To get assurance of meeting full maintenance funding, agreement was obtained
during negotiations that the GOB would provide adequate fundinig for meeting full routine and periodic
maintenance costs for all maintainable FRBs. RRs and structures during the project period, and for this
purpose would increase its budget allocation for rural road maintenance to US$21 million in 1997-98,
and thereafter annually increase it by US$2.5 million up to the year 2001-02. It will also be agreed
that in case the RMP program, which currently provides about US$11 million equivalent for rural
infrastructure maintenance, is downsized or discontinued, the Government would provide additional
budget allocation or ensure other funding to continue similar program and to make up for the resulting
reduction in maintenance funding. These amounts will be revised by mutual agreement in the light of
further studies planned to be carried out during project implementation. In addition, the poor rural
roads which are not in a maintainable condition due to prolonged deterioration, are in the immediate
need of rehabilitation. At present there is no separate line for rehabilitation works under LGED's
budget system. It is therefore necessary that the GOB and LGED should include separate allocation
for rehabilitation until the roads are improved and placed under a sustainable maintenance program.
2.18   Agreement was reached during negotiations that LGED would agree with IDA before June 30
of each year annual maintenance program for the project area for the following fiscal year; the
program for 1996-97 would be provided by January 31, 1997. The annual maintenance program would
include the estimated funding requirements for routine and periodic maintenance separately for FRB,
RRs, and structures for the project area. In order to carry out maintenance operations more effectively
and efficiently in the field, agreement was reached during negotiations that LGED would designate a
maintenance engineer in each district and thana by March 31. 1997. In addition, as part of agreed
implementation procedures (para. 2.12 and Annex 2.1), LGED would promote greater community
participation in road maintenance, and for this purpose would, in consultation with IDA, pilot greater
community involvement in maintenance in two districts, starting not later than June 30, 1998. LGED
would also set up District-level road user committees in the project area (para. 3.2).
(c)    Construction of Drainage Structures on Rural Roads (SRR) (Base cost US$55.08 million)
2.19   This component, which is national in scope and not limited to the 14 districts, would assist
through participatory process, to fill large number of gaps existing in the rural road network and will
help local communities in removing a major constraint they face in rural transport; it will also help to
build new drainage structures where existing roads block cross-drainage. In Bangladesh, there are
about 40,000 gaps (200,000 linear meters), yet to be bridged on its rural road network, which make
the roads fragmented and less effective for all-weather mobility. Since the central government has to
focus on FRB improvements costing over US$1.5 billion over the next 10 years, the only solution
would be for the UP's to raise own resources and take on their own initiative to fill these road gaps.
These gaps force closure of roads in rainy season, and block smooth flow of traffic in fair season. The
structures are good investments in reducing transport costs and mobility constraints in the rural areas
and reducing waterlogging in farms.  Because it involves a large number of small, dispersed
investments where priorities are best defined locally, the SRR component provides the opportunity to
encourage the participation of communities, local bodies and other interest groups in planning,
decision-making and implementation. Field interviews show that there is enthusiasm among UP



25
Chairmen to participate in this program. All the participatory aspects of the component are detailed in
Annexes 2.1 and 2.3. This component, through participatory process, community ownership, and
initial deposit by UPs to signal (para 2.22) their commitment, will start a new culture in the planning,
development and maintenance of transportation infrastructure in the rural areas.
2.20   On the 16,000 km of RRI and RR2 roads in the main project area there are estimated to be
5,600 unspanned gaps with a total length of about 30,000 linear meters, and a further 3,400 existing
structures, with a total length of about 14,000 linear meters, which are in poor or damaged condition
and require replacement or repair. This equates to more than one gap or damaged structure for every
1.75 km of Rural Road. The project will finance the construction of about 6,000 structures (31,600
linear meters) on RRs all over the country - primarily pipe and slab culverts and small bridges - to
span gaps and replace damaged structures, together with ancillary earthworks. It will also finance new
structures where existing road embankments block drainage between farm areas. This will make about
10,500 km of seasonal rural roads into all-weather roads, thus enhancing the value of existing
investments (one structure of US$8,000 releasing full economic use of about U$50,000 worth of
investment on 1.75 km roads).  The annual construction program, and its geographic scope, is
presented in PID-2. It is expected that some of the structures will be built on R3 roads subject to UP's
preferences.
2.21   An amount of about US$55.08 million as base cost, is allocated for this component, of which
US$47.70 million equivalent is for drainage structures (culverts and small bridges not exceeding 20 m
length) on rural roads, and about US$7.38 million equivalent for ancillary earth works on rural roads
(as approaches to the structures or elsewhere on the rural roads).  As an indicative figure (not
restrictive), the average length (span) of culvert is likely to be 5 meters, at a cost of about US$8,000
equivalent. All UPs will be allowed to participate on a first-come-first-served basis, according to the
date of registration. The participating UP has to deposit an initial Taka 10,000 (US$250) into LGED
SRR account for registration, and indicate the location for the structures. After registration, LGED will
inspect the selected site and make drawings and estimates and discuss with the UP choices of types and cost
estimates. Technical standards for structures were developed under RRMIMP-1 and would be further
improved as indicator in PID-3. Before award of contract, the UP must deposit the remaining contribution
(above the initial deposit) to LGED's SRR account. UP's total contribution will be 10% of total costs
during the first three years of the project and 20% during the last two years of the project. After selection
of site and design by the UP, LGED Thana Engineer through the TDCC Tender Committees, will invite
bids from small contractors and eligible labor contracting societies (LCS)/Labor Groups, and select lowest
evaluated bidder following usual procedures acceptable to IDA.  Earthworks outside the structure
approaches will be awarded to LCSs/labor groups of poor women/landless laborers based on negotiated
contracts and unit rates under procedures acceptable to IDA. These selection criteria and processes are
listed in Annex 2.1.
2.22   The construction of SRR will be a continual process and the sites will be selected by UPs
through a participatory process involving two stages (see para. 3.1 and Annex 2.3) and in accordance
with the agreed selection criteria given in Annex 2.1. In addition, each participating UP should
undertake to provide: (a) regular maintenance of structures after its completion; (b) monitoring data
relating to funding resources, length passable, traffic growth and employment of the poor. The
implementation procedures agreed (para. 2.12) require that UPs should conform to the following and
other criteria/procedures given in Annex 2. 1: (a) site selection criteria agreed for the construction of
SRR; (b) initial deposit of Taka 10,000 into the LGED SRR account for registration; (c) deposit of
total UP's contribution before inviting bids; (d) sustainable maintenance program for the completed
structures, and (e) provide agreed data for  monitoring project success and evaluation of its
development impact on the rural economy. LGED will undertake to provide the completed structure
within one year of registration by UP; this guarantee will apply only to the first 1500 structures



26
registered for each year, because of possible capacity limitations, and further there may also be a limit
on the total number of structures per UP.
(d)   Improvement of Rural Markets: (Base cost US$10.69 million)
2.23   Growth center markets are focal points for the sale and processing of rural produce, for the
distribution of agricultural inputs and consumer goods imported into rural areas, and for other
economic and social activities. Unimproved markets are congested, unhygienic, dusty in the dry and
muddy in the wet - a problem compounded by the fact that many markets are low-lying, close to rivers
and not free-draining. Many markets trade in meat and fish, but without adequate facilities for
hygienic operations. The project would finance basic infrastructure and improved common facilities at
136 growth center markets, 67 in the Greater Dhaka Area and 69 in the Northwest Region. The main
improvements would include covered sheds for the sale of perishable goods, slaughter slabs, sales
platform, internal roads and walkways within the market areas and connecting with the main feeder
roads serving the market area, drains, latrines, garbage pits, provision of parking space and an office
for market administration. Production of bio-gas from garbage pits will be tried on a pilot basis. The
project would also include improvement of about 14 other rural markets - one in each project district -
to be developed as model UP market centers with improved transport terminal facilities and access.
2.24   Priority has also been given to integrating road, inland water transport, and market
improvements in order to enhance the efficient operation of the rural transport and trading network.
The selection procedure, and criteria  for markets are given in Annex 2.1, and the list of selected
markets and is given in Annex 2.2. The design standards and costs for market improvements are
detailed in PID-4. The 'Users Input' participatory approach will be applied to the detailed design of the
markets, which will involve market vendors and other users in design. The user participation in
selection and detailed design will develop a sense of local ownership of the improved markets.
Additional participatory measures will be adopted to improve the operation of the markets. The UPs
will contribute 10% of investment costs. The project will assist and support the formation of Market
Management Committees, and will provide them with training and assistance in planning and managing
the operation and maintenance of the improved markets. Maintenance will be financed from the lease
revenue of improved markets which is allocated for this purpose and which is considered generally
adequate (see para. 1.24).  According to the implementation procedures agreed (para. 2.12),
LGED/UPs will use participatory processes, involving market vendors, to improve the structure and
operation of the existing Market Management Conmmittees, and to improve market leasing processes to
enhance revenue.
(e)    Rehabilitation/Construction of River Jetties (Ghats): (Base cost US$2. 00 million)
2.25   Inland water transport (IWT) through country boats remains important for the rural movement
of people and goods. The project will provide improved facilities for the safe, dry, loading and
unloading of cargo and people at 41 rural river ghats, 14 in the Northwest Region and 27 in the
Greater Dhaka Area which is more riverine. This will increase the efficiency of loading/unloading,
increase the role of IWT, reduce transport costs, and will reduce the accidents, injuries and losses that
presently occur during this process and will provide a healthy environment.  This component
emphasizes local participation in selection, detailed design and operation of the improved ghats, using
the same methodology as for the growth center markets. The ghats to be improved have been selected
by the same procedure as for the markets, and the UPs will contribute 10% of the costs of
improvement. Attention has also been given to integrating ghat improvements with the improved FRB
and market network. The selection procedures and criteria are given in Annex 2.1, the list of ghats in
Annex 2.2, and their annual construction program is given in PID-5.



27
2.26   The ghat improvements will consist of 'river-bank' facilities for safe loading and unloading and
for mooring, and 'on-land' facilities comprising a paved area with drainage, footpaths, a passenger
shed, clean water supply, sanitary latrines, garbage bins, a small administration office and an internal
access road to connect the ghat to the nearby FRB or main road. The aim is to provide low-cost but
effective river-bank facilities, and design standards will be evolved as the project develops practical
experience of designing for different conditions. Most of the ghats selected for improvement serve
Growth center markets, and their improvement will contribute to the integration of the water/road
transport and trading network. Ghat users and boat operators will participate in the detailed design of
the ghats, through application of the Users Input approach. Details of ghat designs and costs are in
PID-5. All ghats construction works will be on public land, and all improved ghats will be operated
through competitive leasing.   Measures will be adopted to establish effective operation and
maintenance of the ghats by the ghat management committees, using the same participatory procedures
as for the markets component. Involvement of country boat operators/owners associations, one of the
main user groups of ghats, in the management of the ghats will be emphasized. It was agreed as part of
implementation procedures (para. 2.12) that GOB will: (a) cause ghat management committee to be
established for all developed ghats before completion of each ghat improvement, modeled on the
market management committees; (b) ensure that operation of these ghats and markets will have
adequate user participation.
(f)    Pilot Program to Improve Non-Motorized Transport (NMT): (Base cost US$0.96 million)
2.27   NMT (rickshaw-vans, cycle-rickshaws, bicycles and bullock carts) play a very important role
in rural transport system in Bangladesh (see para. 1.15). The project includes a pilot component for
promoting safety and design improvements in rickshaw and rickshaw-vans (which carry more than
50%  of rural road traffic) and bullock-cart wheels (which damage road pavement).  This pilot
component will focus on the development and introduction of improvements in the design of rickshaw
vans, cycle rickshaws and bullock carts on making trial runs through user-groups to further improve
designs through their feedback; and on measures to stimulate the provision of credit for the purchase of
the improved designs. While there have previous efforts to improve designs, they were not supported
by adequate field trials and promotion.   The component, if successful, will increase the
competitiveness and service level of rickshaw vans and passenger rickshaws by improving their
performance, cost-efficiency, safety and load carrying capability, and will sustain and increase income-
generation for the rural poor from rickshaw pulling, it will also reduce the efforts and hardship
involved in rickshaw driving. The component will also help increase the efficiency and reduce the
damage caused to improved roads by, bullock carts.
2.28   The pilot component will adopt a participatory approach to technical innovation. It will be
implemented through experienced local NGOs and vehicle users, and can potentially make a major
impact on rural transport in the long run. The approach will be to develop specific improvements to
the design of cycle rickshaws and rickshaw-vans - e.g. bodies, braking and gear systems - which can
be fitted to the existing rickshaws as well as incorporated into new vehicles. For bullock carts the
emphasis will be on introducing wheel/axle assemblies using rubber tires. The improved designs will
be disseminated through promotion, demonstration and training, in collaboration with the Bangladesh
National Working Group of the International Forum for Rural Transport and Development (IFRTD,
and other local NGOs and community groups. This will develop an informed network of organizations,
including credit-financing NGO agencies, to assist the dissemination of the improved designs and to
encourage the provision of loans for the purchase of the improved designs by the rural poor. The
project will also provide for the mobility need assessments related to the improvement and operation of
NMT. The design, strategy, related issues, implementation program and cost estimate for the
proposed pilot NMT component are described in more detail in Annex 2.4.



28
(g)    Equipment: (Base cost US$7.27 million)
2.29   The project will provide: (a) Construction equipment mainly for compaction and concreting to
complement existing LGED equipment stocks in the project area; (b) laboratory and field testing
equipment for the five project Districts in the Greater Dhaka Area that do not have these facilities, and
a limited provision to upgrade the existing laboratories in the other nine project Districts in the
Northwest Region; (c) supervision vehicles to complement the existing LGED fleet; (d) office and
training equipment for project implementation; and (e) the urgently needed MIS related accessories.
The equipment to be provided under the project is listed in Annex 2.5.
2.30   The project will help develop, and introduce in the project area, through design improvements,
innovative items of low-cost, appropriate, locally made equipment to improve the quality and
efficiency of construction and maintenance activities. Examples are: (a) temperature-controlled bitumen
heater, temperature-controlled aggregate heater, drum mixer and trolley - to improve the quality and
efficiency of production and laying of batched quantities of BC; (b) bitumen sprayer for use with both
hot bitumen and bitumen emulsion; (c) a mobile bitumen trolley to carry one drum of emulsion and
with a hand pumped spray lance; (d) a manually propelled compaction roller to improve compaction
for ancillary earthworks on Rural Roads.
2.31   The equipment produced under the project will ensure the timely completion of project because
the small local contractors do not have their own equipment and largely depend on LGED equipment
fleet. LGED fleet is not sufficient to meet the project requirements. The existing scale of rental
charges for LGED equipment is not sufficient to recover their operating and replacement costs, though
they are adequate to recover operating and original investment costs. In order to avoid low LGED rent
rates, discouraging the contractors in buying their own equipment, and to encourage the private sector
to enter the equipment rental market, agreement was reached during negotiations that GOB would: (a)
ensure that charges for renting such equipment are sufficient to cover the maintenance and replacement
costs of such equipment. and (b) make available to LGED annually, through budget allocations into a
separate identifiable 'item' in LGED's budget, for maintenance and replacement costs of such
equipment, funds equivalent to the estimated rent collections for the following financial year. To
promote labor based methods and encourage local contractor to have their own equipment, project
would help promote use of low-cost locally produced equipment (para. 2.30), and also provide
mobilization advance to contractors to help purchase of own equipment. To ensure adequate quality
control in the execution of civil works the project would also finance urgently needed laboratory and
testing equipment. The DSM consultants would provide technical assistance to LGED to set up an
efficient equipment management system.
(h) Institutional and Human Resource Development: (Base cost US$3.54 million)
2.32   Technical Assistance (Capacity Building): The project will provide Technical assistance to
strengthen the institutional capacity and capability of LGED, local government bodies and the private
sector contracting industry. The main focus would be on training district and thana level staff in: (a)
supervision, quality control and financial and reporting procedures; (b) maintenance planning and
management; (c) implementation procedures for the SRR component; (d) the methodologies and
procedures for enhancing local community and beneficiary participation with an emphasis on
information sharing and "Users Input" approaches in the selection, design, operation and maintenance
of investment schemes, and in (e) improving maintenance systems. The project will also develop the
capability of local contractors through training in technical and management aspects of contracting,
prompt payment of bills, progressive introduction of larger construction contracts, and measures to
encourage contractor-ownership of construction equipment.  It will also support NGOs active in
improving the country boats operations in the informal sector, in extending information-sharing, safety



29
education and training. The experience gained in Socio-economic monitoring and evaluation (SEM&E)
of the project effects under RRMIMP-1, would be further harmonized in order to develop better rural
investment planning and policy making. Technical assistance will be provided through consultants
(local and foreign) as part of Design, Supervision and Monitoring (DSM) consultants, and as additional
specialists, and through training programs (internal and external). Further details are given in Annex
2.6. The training program will be reviewed during negotiations, which will be subjected to annual
review before March 31 each year.
2.33   The project will support LGED efforts in its capacity building, and based on the MANCAPS
recommendations (para. 1.42), the project would finance seven incremental high level staff positions
given in (Annex 1.2), which have been found necessary to implement project activities and will form
part of LGED's subsequently reorganized establishment. Agreement was reached during negotiations
that LGED will fill these positions by December 31, 1996, and GOB would fund these seven positions
from the revenue budget from the fifth year of the proiect (2000-2001) onwards. Agreement was
reached with GOB that it would implement the MANCAPS (see para. 1.41) recommendations on: (i)
improved financial management and internal auditing: (ii) reduction of span of control through more
delegation of responsibilities, and creation of 6 regional offices headed by Superintending Engineer
before March 31. 1997. The other details including MANCAPS recommendations and proposed
organization chart for the management strengthening of LGED are given in Annex 1.2.
2.34   Technical Assistance (Policy Support): It is proposed to carry out the following studies on:
(a) long-term socio-economic impact to evaluate and quantify the impacts of rural infrastructure
investment in the project area on the transport and trading system, on economic and social
development, and on poverty alleviation (which will supplement traffic impact studies carried out as
part of SEM&E (see para. 1.53)); (b) small scale contracting industry to develop a better understanding
of its financial, management and operating characteristics and of the constraints to its development; (c)
study of pavement behavior of various trial sections constructed on FRB roads under the project; (d)
road construction contractor unit costs to develop competitive unit cost rates for different construction
activities; (e) Feeder Roads Regional prioritization study to define guidelines for the prioritization of
FRBs rural roads and markets improvements and for reviewing roads classification; (f) financial
resource mobilization of local bodies; (g) financial audit studies; and (h) technical audit studies. The
outline TOR for the proposed studies is shown in Annex 2.7, with details in PID; these were reviewed
and agreed during negotiations.
2.35   Development of Small Scale Construction Industry: LGED implements a large number of
construction schemes including rural roads, structures and markets through award of contracts. The
capacity of local construction industry largely consisting of petty contractors, is severely constrained
on account of lack of contractor's owned construction equipment (rollers and trucks), training in
contract procedures and management, and weak credit worthiness for receiving mobilization advances
from LGED. Limited construction season, generally December to May, is another key factor which
discourages the small contractors from buying construction equipment. In order to keep the contract
size within the capacity of local contractors, and to help train them, LGED has been awarding
contracts of smaller size (2-3 km with contract value not more than US$100,000) with the time limit of
one construction season, and has also been giving their equipment on rent. The proposed project
would encourage to enlarge the size of contracts by extending the time limit into two construction
seasons. To strengthen the capability of local construction industry, the project will initiate several
next steps including: (a) improving technical and management capacity of small contractors through
training; (b) financing of equipment for leasing to contractors at commercial rates; (c) encouraging the
contractors to own low-cost locally made construction equipment; (d) progressive increase in the size
of contracts. To review these initiatives systematically, a study on small scale contracting industry



30
(para. 2.34) has been proposed and based on its finding an action plan would be prepared to develop
the existing construction industry in Bangladesh.
2.36   Promotion of Private Sector: To promote private sector participation, Working Committee
consisting of representatives from LGED, RHD, Ministry of Industry, Bangladesh University of
Engineering and Technology (BUET), Contractor's Association, leasing companies, and private
investors, would be created. This committee would help information sharing regarding future road
investments and private sector's role and growth potential in road sub-sector industries. It is expected
that the committee could speed up the introduction of plant hire pools for the small contractors, the
production of bitumen emulsions and the manufacturing of precast concrete units for the construction
of drainage structures on rural roads. Agreement was reached during negotiations that the GOB would
create a Working Committee with representatives from private and public sectors, for promoting
private sector in the rural infrastructure sector before March 31. 1997, and would make best efforts to
implement its recommendations.
2.37   The project will support improved co-ordination and co-operation between the LGED and the
RHD. Possible areas for co-ordination and co-operation are: (a) training of engineers, technicians and
small contractors; (b) promotion of a consistent maintenance approach with increased participation of
road users and clear assignment of responsibilities; (c) selection criteria for upgrading and
improvement and FRB and FRA roads; (d) standard design and specifications for road pavements and
drainage structures; (e) information sharing on the performance of pavement trial sections; (f) quality
control procedures, development of construction equipment, rental charges for plant and equipment,
and unit rates for construction cost estimates.  IDA will promote such cooperation through its
continued project involvement with both LGED and RHD.
(i)   Implementation Support: (Base cost US$14.92 million)
2.38   Given LGED has sufficient experience in implementation of similar rural development projects
and performance of the RRMIMP-1 is quiet satisfactory, LGED can well handle the implementation of
the proposed project on its own. However, for the major project components especially improving
FRB roads and construction of structures on rural roads, where the quality control is a matter of
concern, the project would strengthen the institutional capacity of the Project Implementation Office
(PIO) by financing DSM consultant services, incremental staff positions and the urgently needed
operating costs following similar arrangements under RRMIMP-1. The proposed implementation
support would ensure the timely completion of all project components, will build institutional capacity
and technical capability of LGED in the planning and management of RDPs. The project will include:
(a)   Incremental staff and Operating Costs: (US$3.82 million). The project will finance
incremental staff positions and operating costs  for project implementation, but
excluding staff whose costs can be recovered from fees and charges, e.g. equipment
operators. It will include 394 staffing positions or about 1961 person-year. Annex 3.2
provides more details of project incremental staffing.  LGED's established staff
strength cannot handle many additional tasks related to the project; these staff
requirements were estimated after using private sector consultants to the extent
feasible.
(b)    Design. Supervision and Monitoring (DSM): (US$10.59 million) The project will
finance DSM consultant services. The DSM consultants will be responsible for
engineering design and checking of supervision including signing certificates of
payment and disbursement; will assist LGED in project planning, contracting,
implementation, training, monitoring (including SEM&E) and reporting; and will



31
provide technical assistance on different aspects of the project, including TA in setting
up a maintenance management system in the project area. It is estimated that about
3,376 professional staff months of constancy services will be required including 220
staff months of international consultants. The Terms of Reference for the DSM
consultants are in PID-6.
E. PROJECT COSTS
2.39   The total project cost, including physical and price contingencies, is estimated at US$192.36
million, of which the foreign exchange component is US$46.22 million (24%). Duties and taxes are
estimated at US$13.49 million. Physical contingencies are estimated at 5-10% for civil works, 10%
for equipment and, because of uncertainties about market values of land, 25% for land acquisition.
Price contingencies and foreign exchange rates are estimated on the basis of expected rates of local and
foreign inflation. Local inflation is estimated at rates ranging from 2.5% per annum during the first
year and 4.5% during the remaining period, while foreign inflation is estimated at 2.5% per annum. It
is assumed that exchange rate adjustments would, on average, maintain purchasing power parity during
the project implementation period. The project cost is summarized in Table 2.1 below and details are
in Annex 2.9.

2.40   Project cost estimates are based on consultant's preliminary design and are derived from: (a)
proposed design standards, and costs of recently awarded contracts for similar works and current
LGED unit cost rates; (b) preliminary survey of market prices for land; (c) inquiries on current prices
of equipment in Bangladesh and costs of recently procured equipment on similar projects; (d)
prevailing staff costs; and (e) prevailing fees for consultants on rural infrastructure projects in
Bangladesh. Duties and taxes are estimated at 7% of gross cost of civil works executed by contractors,
13% of the cost of locally procured equipment, and 45% of the cost of imported equipment and 75%
for imported four-wheel-drive vehicles.
F. PROJECT FINANCING
2.41   The project would be financed by IDA, SDC, GOB and UPs. The overall financing plan is:
IDA credit US$133.00 million (69% of the total cost); SDC grant US$15.00 million (8% of the total
cost); GOB, US$36.42 million (19% of the total cost); and UPs, US$7.94 million (4% of the total
cost). The IDA credit would cover: 80% cost of the civil works (FRB, SRR, growth center markets
and river jetties); 100% cost of equipment excluding taxes; 100% cost of technical assistance (policy
support); 14% cost of the project implementation support; 14% cost of pilot program to improve
NMT; and average of 50% of the periodic maintenance on FRB roads in the project area on a
declining scale (para. 2.15). The SDC grant of US$15 million would be under joint financing
arrangements, which means that IDA procurement procedures will be applicable to the total project.
SDC participated in the IDA-GOB credit negotiations, and has subsequently signed its Grant
Agreement with GOB. SDC and IDA would sign a trust-fund agreement covering joint-financing
arrangements. SDC Grant would cover: 100% cost of the training and study tours; 86% cost of the
pilot program of NMT; 86% cost of DSM and audit services; and 66% cost of the incremental staff
and operating costs. Project cost estimates and financing plan were reviewed during negotiations.
Fulfilling the conditions of effectiveness of the cofinancing agreement between GOB and SDC (other
than those related to the effectiveness of the GOB-IDA credit agreement) would be a condition of IDA
credit effectiveness. Table 2.2 summarizes the project financing plan by components.
2.42   The Government counterpart resources would finance : 100%  cost of the land acquisition;
average of 50% of the periodic maintenance in the project area; 20% cost of civil works (FRB); 10%
cost of Structures of Rural Roads (SRRs) (only for the first three years); 10% cost of the growth
center markets and river jetties; 20% of the incremental project staff and operating costs; and all costs
(US$13.79 million) in taxes and duties. The UPs will finance 10-20% of the cost of SRRs (10% in the
first three years and 20% in the last two years); 10% of the cost of the growth center markets, and
river jetties. These local contributions are detailed in Annex 2.1.
2.43 One of the main financial issues affecting the project would be that the UPs, which are the
lowest level elected Government in the country have a weak financial basis, and are unable to find
adequate funding for maintaining the infrastructure under their jurisdiction on a sustainable basis and
for constructing essential drainage structures to fill gaps on their roads.  The project proposes
measures to partly improve this situation (see para. 1.35).

G. STATUS OF PROJECT PREPARATION
2.44   The LGED has prepared the project in the light of its considerable past experience, with
assistance from international and local consultants, and in partnership with the IDA and the SDC. The
main project components (roads, markets, ghats) have been selected following an intensive process
involving beneficiary participation, subject to possible essential changes in future; for smaller
components (rural structures etc.) selection process will start following credit approval.  Out of the
list of FRB roads selected for improvement under the project, five roads in Greater Dhaka Area (41
hcm) have already been tendered under retroactive financing (para. 3.21) and work started in January
1996. Supervision consultants for this part of the work have also been appointed following IDA
procedures.
2.45   Engineering designs of FRB roads, markets and ghats proposed for implementation in 1996/97
(first year) have been completed, and contracts for most first year works, particularly for FRB roads,
have already been awarded. Further preparatory work, including preparation of the engineering
designs and bidding documents for second year (1997-98) FRB roads, markets and ghats, is in an
advanced stage and would be completed by end of January 1997. LGED has appointed DSM
Consultants in November 1996, following IDA guidelines. LGED has already appointed Project
Director and other key staff of the Project Implementation Office (PIO). Work on designing markets
with community iilputs is in progress with assistance of preparation consultants, and has been
completed for about eighteen markets. Process of selecting NGOs for further NMT component has
been started and will be completed soon after IDA credit approval. The Land Acquisition Action Plan
(para. 3.28), and the Sector Environmental Assessment (para. 3.29) for the related civil works have
been prepared in accordance with IDA guidelines and have been agreed during negotiations.
2.46   Considerable preparation for the component of SRR component has been completed including
the agreement on participatory procedures in consultation with the local bodies, UPs, TDCC and the
local communities. The actual physical work on this component will start only after the project
approval, since the local bodies who are required to put up initial deposit to signal their participation
in this component, need confirmation on project funding from IDA. The low-cost engineering design
for all sizes of structures have already been developed under RRMIMP-1 and it will take only about
two days for LGED field engineers to finalize the design after site selection is done by local
communities. About 1200 similar structures have already been constructed under RRMIMP-1, and the
experience shows that this component would move expeditiously. The UP chairpersons have shown
enthusiastic support for this component and have indicated that if each UP goes for two structures, the
proposed 6,000 structures would be fully committed early in the project cycle. Since these are several
separate contracts to be executed under the supervision of LGED Thana Engineers, contracts in the
different locations can move simultaneously. The procurement and account-keeping arrangements
acceptable to IDA for small structures have already been established under RRMIMP-1 and would be
followed under the proposed project. To increase public awareness of this nationwide component and
to encourage local community to prepare for active participation in it, the LGED has already launched
public campaign through the media and other channels. Given the innovative features introduced on
participatory process for the structure's site selection and the requirement of an initial deposit by UPs,
it is likely that the physical progress of this component may be slow during the first year, but should
gain momentum in the subsequent years.



36
3. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
A. AN OVERVIEW OF THE PARTICIPATORY PROCESS
3.1    The project is considered by IDA as a "flagship" operation in using participatory approach in
view of its emphasis on using borrower and beneficiary participation in the selection, design and
implementation of the project. The participatory approach was emphasized right from the start of
project preparation by organizing several participatory meetings with 'users', rural communities,
beneficiaries,  local government and the related government and non-government organizations.
Locations of main project components (roads, river jetties, markets), have been selected using active
involvement of beneficiaries. Rural structures (SRR costing more than 35% of the project costs), will
be selected using specially designed participatory processes agreed during negotiations (see Annex
2.1). The local bodies (UPs) will put 10-20% (para.2.42) of the structure costs up-front to signal
their financial stake. Actual selection of the structures will be done through a participatory process
involving two stages: (i) selection by UP residents through group meetings for discussing alternatives
using user-inputs techniques (such as, map-making etc.), most likely with assistance of LGED's
community organizers and the projects' participation facilitators (which assistance may not be available
in all cases); and (ii) voting by UP council on the proposed sites (see para. 2.17). Road designs take
into account user preferences, particularly, concerns of NMT operators who are major users. In the
case of growth center markets, the design and layout of facilities is being done in full day meetings
with poor vendors and others who would be the main users of these market facilities; they will also be
given a key role in managing the markets (regarding level of user charges, auctioning of market lease,
maintenance arrangements and funding), through market committees of users, reporting every six
months to an "assembly" of market users, with special emphasis on information sharing. Similarly,
country boat operators and other user groups will be actively involved in Ghat selection, design and
management. For road components, user committees will be set up to give feed back on design and
quality of on-going works. LGED will also initiate pilot programs in at least two districts to try out
new ways for greater community involvement in road maintenance.  Many of the participatory
processes used are based on lessons learned from different pilot programs undertaken in Bangladesh in
which LGED has been involved.
3.2    To ensure the participatory process to be more transparent and well implemented, the project
would finance 17 positions of local specialists in community participation (sociologists or equivalent)
and one expatriate expert with local experience; of these one community specialist will be placed in
each project district, and three community specialists (supervisors) will be assigned to advise and
assist the Project Director and LGED headquarters. In addition, suitable LGED personnel at Thana
level will be trained as "community organizers;" LGED already trained and appointed 10 community
organizers with effect from February 1996. Project implementation actions (para. 2.12. Annex 2.1)
include appointment by LGED of 17 qualified community specialists before March 31, 1997: eight of
them have been appointed, as an advance measure agreed during negotiations. In addition, LGED
would set up, before December 31, 1997, District level Road User Committees in the project area
which would include representatives from different user groups and will promote information sharing
and selection of priorities, and facilitate feedback from users on quality of on-going works.
3.3    The participation specialists will also assist poor workers and destitute women to organize
labor contracting societies (to work as contractors in labor based road works and road maintenance) in
two pilot districts, with the help of NGOs. UP's financial position will be strengthened under the
project (para. 1.35) so that they can effectively participate in their infrastructure maintenance and



37
improvements. Professional participation facilitators will help implement many aspects of the project,
and will also help create a participatory institutional system for future projects. The project agency
(LGED) is fully committed to promoting local participation in projects, and has good track record in
working closely with local communities; overall, the project will strive hard to promote effective local
participation in the design and implementation of rural infrastructure projects, and more importantly
promote a sense of community ownership of rural infrastructure.  Further details of community
participation in rural infrastructure development under the project are given in Annex 2.3.
3.4    The project emphasizes participation of women, who would be some of the main project
beneficiaries in receiving and in getting better facilities as market vendors and users. To increase
participation of women, as part of project implementation actions (see para. 2.14 and Annex 2.1),
LGED would nominate one of its senior staff (the sociologist in the LGED headquarter's office
provided as incremental staff) to look after gender issues. LGED would also recruit more women
engineers as its staff (it has currently only 30 women engineers out of total engineering staff of about
700), and would give special emphasis to the training of the women members of UPs under its
participation training programs under the project so as to strengthen their role in local development
matters. LGED/UPs will also help increase the number of women members in the market and ghat
user committees. The participatory procedures agreed with GOB (para. 2.12 and Annex 2.1) include
these actions.
3.5    The project components have been agreed with the government after extensive deliberations
with the concerned agencies and main user groups. LGED deputed three high level staff to participate
in the pre-appraisal and appraisal teams. In addition, two local consultants with specialized knowledge
on participation also contributed in designing participatory process.
B. PROJECT MANAGEMENT
3.6    LGED, a well established department with a proven record on implementation of similar rural
roads and market improvement projects. Following the successful model of RRMIMP-1 a separate
Project Implementation Office (PIO) with full-time Project Director (PD) who reports to the Chief
Engineer, LGED has recently been established with its headquarters in Dhaka. The PD will be
responsible for: (a)  rural infrastructure components of the project; (b) selected Institutional and
Human Resource Development sub-components which are specific to the project area; (c) procurement
of equipment and goods; (d) management of the DSM consultants; and (e) financial management and
reporting. The Additional Chief Engineer (Design, Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation) will be
responsible for: (a) the implementation of the Institutional and Human Resource Development sub-
components which focus on LGED as an organization and/or have a nationwide perspective; and (b)
the pilot program to improve NMT; and (c) the development of low-cost, appropriate construction
equipment. The project management organogram, and the division of responsibilities between the PD
and the ACE, are detailed in Annex 3.1.
3.7    The PIO will work directly with the offices of the LGED Executive Engineers in the 14 project
Districts and, through them with the offices of the Thana Engineers. At headquarters, the PD will be
assisted by two Deputy Project Directors (DPD): one DPD will head a Unit to look after the project
components in the Greater Dhaka area; and the second DPD will manage project activities related to
the SRR which is national in scope. One senior engineer will be in charge of performance reporting.
To strengthen technical, managerial and administrative capacity,  the PIO  incremental staff at
headquarters will include one Economist to undertake the SEM&E work and two Sociologists (one of
them for SRR follow-up) to work on participatory aspects of the project. The incremental staffing at
District level will include one Sociologist per District to provide additional capacity on land acquisition



38
and participatory aspects of the project. LGED has already appointed Project Director and other key
staff of PIO in October 1996. as agreed during nezotiations.
3.8   Defects Liability Period of Last Year's works: In view of the 5-year implementation period and
since no foreign contractors are likely to be involved in the project works, it has been agreed that the
one year guaranteed maintenance period (defects liability period) of the last year's contract will be
monitored by LGED; the retention money for this maintenance period will be retained by LGED (from
the Government's 20% contribution) in a separate account and paid to the contractors on due
completion. This arrangement will be confirmed during negotiations.
C. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
3.9   The project will be implemented over a period of about 57 months between Board date and
Project Completion date (December 1996 to September 2001); Credit closing date will be March 31,
2002.  The proposed implementation schedule is shown in Annex 3.4; this was agreed during
negotiations. For the civil work components the peak level of construction activity is scheduled for the
years 2, 3 and 4 with a minimum level of activity in the year 5.
3.10   Equipment will be procured in two phases: (a) first phase in 1996/97 to equip the PIO and the
five 'new' Districts that have not previously benefited from an RDP; (b) second phase in 1998/99 to
replace existing equipment at District level that has reached the end of its useful working life. The low-
cost, appropriate construction equipment will be developed in the early stages of the project so that it
can be tested in selected Districts in 1997/98 and introduced to all Districts in 1998/99. The impact
study will be carried out in three phases: (a) to collect baseline data; (b) to examine short-term effects
of infrastructure improvements; and (c) to examine longer-term socio-economic impacts - starting in
early 1997 and ending in end-2001. Technical assistance studies will be carried out in the first half of
the project period so that their findings can be applied during the project period.
D. PROCUREMENT
3.11   Procurement of civil works and goods under the project will be in accordance with World
Bank Procurement Guidelines. Table 3.1 summarizes the procurement arrangements for the project.
The procurement plan for civil works packages appears in Annex 3.5.

3.12   Civil Works: In view of small size of contracts scattered over a large area, civil works for the
improvement and upgrading of FRB roads, for construction of FRB structures, for periodic
maintenance of roads, for construction of structures on rural roads (SRRs), for improvement of
markets, for rehabilitation/construction of ghats and for the construction of the LGED Dhaka District
office are expected to be procured on the basis of National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procedures
which do not preclude international participation, using the latest Bank's Standard Bidding Documents
(SBD) for Procurement of Smaller Contracts. The individual value of these contracts will not exceed
US$1.5 million equivalent, and their aggregate value will not exceed US$148 million equivalent. All
civil works contracts with individual value exceeding US$1.5 million equivalent will be procured
under International Competitive Bidding (ICB) following IDA Guidelines. Prequalification of
contractors for inclusion in a roster for the works will be completed in accordance with
prequalification procedures agreed with the World Bank prior to project-start, and the prequalification
will be updated mid-way through the project period. Contract awards for all works estimated to cost
more than US$200,000 equivalent (some 60 contracts) will require IDA's prior review and approval.
About 30% of the total estimated civil works and goods procurement would be subject to IDA's prior
review; post review would be undertaken of the remainder.
3.13   For the early implementation of FRB works in the Greater Dhaka Area in 1995/96, estimated
to cost US$3.0 million, pre-qualified contractors under the ongoing IDA financed RRMIMP-1, RDP-6
and RDP-14, together with LGED licensed Special Category contractors in the five concerned
Districts, will be allowed to bid for contracts, but will be eligible for awards only up to the value of
works for which they are pre-qualified.



40
3.14   Because of the social objectives of the project, which encourage involvement of labor
contracting societies (LCS) set up by landless laborers and destitute women, small work contracts for
tree plantation and care-taking on project FRBs will be procured by direct, annual contract with groups
of disadvantaged women and laborers at fixed payment rates. Also, some SRR works for installation
of pipe culverts, including production of the pipes, estimated to cost up to US$8,000 each, will also be
procured by direct negotiated contract with LCS and other organized labor groups at fixed payment
rates based on the prevalent rates for works. Similarly, ancillary earthworks for the SRR component
will be procured by direct contract with LCS and/or organized labor groups, and by casual
employment of laborers at agreed daily rates. Contract documents for procurement of these works will
be based on existing LGED procedures, acceptable to the Association; LGED has sound procedures for
such contracts, and performance will be closely monitored by DSM consultants and technical auditors
(para. 3.25). All these direct contracts for small works will not exceed cumulatively US$10 million;
their individual contract value will not exceed US$10,000 equivalent.
3.15   Equipment and Goods: Imported equipment and goods will be procured by International
Competitive Bidding using the World Bank's Standard Bidding Documents for Procurement of Goods,
except that minor items or groups of items estimated to cost less than US$200,000 will be procured on
the basis of national competitive bidding within an aggregate value of US$5 million. Procurement of
equipment and goods, with items or groups of items estimated to cost less than US$50,000 equivalent,
upto an aggregate value of US$1 million, will be procured by local shopping. This will include the
procurement of production batches of appropriate, low-cost construction equipment and improved
NMT vehicles.
3.16   Contracts for procurement of equipment and goods and works with a value of over
US$200,000, and the first two NCB contracts for works, will be subject to IDA's prior review and
approval. Procurement of construction equipment and vehicles shall be made with due consideration, in
the preparation of procurement documents and the evaluation of bids, of (a) necessary quality and
durability; (b) the standardization of the LGED equipment and vehicle fleet; and (c) the availability in
Bangladesh of reliable spare parts supply and maintenance support service.
3.17   Consultancy services and technical assistance: under the project will be procured in accordance
with principles and procedures satisfactory to the IDA, based on the Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and The World Bank as Executive Agency (August 1981). The
Bank's Standard Form of Contract for Consultant's Services for Time-Based Assignments (June 1995)
will be used for consultancy contracts. The proposed consultancy services under the project would
involve contracts of the following sizes: one costing around US$10 million; one costing around
US$1.5 million equivalent, and about ten costing less than US$0.5 million equivalent each. The short-
list, terms of reference, final selection and contract of the DSM consultants, the private auditing firm
and the consultants for the preparation of the follow-up project will be subject to review and approval
by the Bank.
3.18   Consultant services for the conduct of the Impact Study (approximate cost US$0.4 million) will
be procured by direct negotiated contract with a premier economic research institute in Bangladesh
with considerable international recognition. This is essential for continuity of the impact study work
from that which is already being carried out by this institute (Bangladesh Institute of Development
Studies (BIDS)) under RRMIMP-1, and because of their specialized expertise. The terms of reference
and the contract for the study are subject to review and approval of the IDA. The services of suitable
NGO's under sub-contract to LGED to execute the Pilot Program to Improve NMT and for building up
labor contracting societies and other participatory processes, estimated to cost a total of US$1.0
million, will be procured by invitation and evaluation of proposals from selected NGOs and/or
consortia of NGOs who have been identified as having the necessary capabilities to undertake the



41
work. The terms of reference, evaluation and contract will be subject to review and approval by the
IDA. Small contracts for other studies and technical assistance services will be allowed under
negotiated contracts subject to IDA's approval. All consultancy contracts with an individual value of
over US$50,000 equivalent will be subject to IDA's prior review and approval.
E. DISBURSEMENTS
3.19   Disbursements under the proposed credit and SDC funds will be made as indicated in Table
3.2 below. The project Implementation Office (PIO) will prepare all withdrawal applications. Full
documentation will be sent to the IDA in support of withdrawal applications except for (a) civil works
and goods contracts awarded under national competitive bidding procedures costing less than the
equivalent of US$200,000 each; (b) civil works let by direct contracts to labor groups or through the
Union Parishad, i.e. tree plantation on FRBs and installation of culverts and ancillary earthworks on
Rural Roads; (c) consultants contracts costing less than US$50,000 equivalent each; and (d)
expenditures on training, incremental staff and operating costs. Withdrawal applications for such
expenditures will be supported by certified Statements of Expenditure (SOE), the documentation for
which would not be submitted to IDA but retained by the PIO and made available for review during
the course of project supervision. It is estimated that about US$70 million equivalent would be
disbursed through SOEs. All withdrawal applications in respect of civil works apart from SRRs and
ancillary earthworks will be accompanied by certificates from the DSM consultants attesting that the
works concerned are consistent with the sub-component selection criteria, that the works and materials
meet the design standards and specifications agreed by IDA, and that payments are in accordance with
the works completed. For the SRRs and ancillary earthworks the DSM consultants will annually
inspect all works after completion and certify that they comply with the selection criteria, that they
conform to the agreed technical standards, and that payments which have been made are consistent
with the completed works. Accredited private auditors will conduct financial and performance audit
including sample checking of payment certificates and progress reports by field verification. The
technical and financial audit (see para. 3.25) will have a special focus on reviewing expenditures
through SOEs.
3.20   Disbursements will occur over a five-year period compared with the 7.5 year standard
disbursement profile for Bank funded road projects. The estimated disbursement schedule is given in
Annex 3.6. Civil works are expected to be completed by September 30, 2001, and, with allowance for
six months for final disbursements, the Credit closing date will be March 31, 2002. The Mid-Term
Review will provide an opportunity to adjust the quantum of works under different categories to reflect
implementation capacity as judged from experience in the first half of the project period.
3.21   Retroactive financing and special account: To expedite early start up, IDA would retroactively
finance some urgently needed road works under the project, which were agreed in principle during
appraisal and on which expenditure was incurred after January 1, 1996, provided IDA procurement
procedures and land acquisition guidelines are followed strictly, and the implementation and civil
works have been adequately supervised and the payments have been properly certified by the
consultants acceptable to IDA. Some retroactive financing for consultancy services (supervision and
impact studies, surveys for fixing bench marks) has also been agreed. The total limit for retroactive
financing would be US$3.5 million. To facilitate timely payment of project expenditures, and to avoid
payment delays to the small contractors, a Special Account amounting to US$10.0 million (equal to
four months' average disbursement) would be established at the Bangladesh Bank in Dhaka. IDA
would provide an initial Special Account deposit of US$6.0 million; the Borrower would receive the
full authorized Special Account allocation of US$10.0 million, when aggregate amount of withdrawals
from the Credit Account plus the total amount of all outstanding special commitments shall be equal to
the equivalent of SDR 15.0 million.
