F. REPORTING
3.22   LGED would prepare, with the assistance of the DSM consultants, quarterly progress reports
covering all components of the project and other pertinent information as required. LGED would
submit to IDA: (a) quarterly report within one month of the end of each quarter; and (b)
implementation completion report in accordance with IDA guidelines. These reports will include: (a)
progress achieved against agreed implementation and disbursement schedules, and key monitoring
performance indicators; (b) work program and cost estimates for the coming quarter and for the total
project. LGED will also prepare an Implementation Completion Report in accordance with IDA
guidelines.
3.23   Mid-term Review: To examine project progress, IDA and GOB would carry out a mid-term
review before the end of third year (allowing completion of fair-weather season for works). PIO and
LGED would organize the review and prepare terms of reference in consultation with IDA. During the
review the compliance to covenants and performance standards and item wise implementation progress,
against original targets will be closely assessed; based on careful review of progress and possible
future slippage, if likely, the project could be restructured if considered necessary by IDA. The review
would focus on: (a) overall progress achieved both in terms of physical and financial targets and key
indicators for the evaluation of development impact; (b) value of contracts issued as against targets,
and likely programs for issuing, completing new contracts; (c) progress in implementing individual
components, particularly the component of SRR, NMT and maintenance; (d) progress in promoting
participatory approach in the planning and implementation of civil works; (e) improvement in the
financial position of local government (UPs); (f) LGED institutional strengthening; (g) contractor's
performance and growth; and (h) equipment management procedures; revision of rental charges and
cost accounting system. The mid-term review of the project would be completed by October 31, 1999.
G. ACCOUNTS
3.24   LGED would maintain adequate records, in line with guidelines on financial reporting and
auditing of IDA projects, to identify physical progress and financial transactions.  Individually
identifiable accounts would also be kept for: (a) all expenditure for which credit withdrawals are made
on the basis of statement of expenditures (para. 3.19); and (b) the flow of funds from the Special
Account. The PIO will use the Financial Management System developed under RRMIMP-1 to record
details of all contracts including expenditures, and will collate the project accounts of LGED District
and Thana offices. The PIO will prepare consolidated statements for all project components and would
be responsible for maintaining project accounts and financial reports in accordance with IDA
guidelines.
H. FINANCIAL AND TECHNICAL AUDITS
3.25   Financial Audit (once in a year) is required to ensure that: (a) Credit proceeds have been used
in accordance with the terms of Credit Agreement and solely for the project purposes; (b) civil works,
goods and services have been procured by the methods specified in the Credit Agreement; and (c) other
Credit Agreement provisions have been complied with satisfactorily. Accredited private auditors,
acceptable to IDA, will be appointed by LGD to carry out financial audit of the project. Performance
of auditing under the ongoing RRMIMP-1 has been satisfactory. In addition to the traditional financial
audit, the project will introduce technical audit to ensure adequate quality of civil works. Technical
Audit (twice in a year) will include on a sample basis the verification of actual work performed and the



44
quality as per approved specifications. Technical audits would be performed by the qualified
consultants acceptable to IDA and may be contracted as a part of the financial audit or separately.
Agreement was reached that the Financial Accounts and Audit Report will be sent to IDA not later
than nine months after the end of the Fiscal Year. and the six-monthly technical audit report not later
than three months after the end of each six-month period of works. The TORs for the financial and
technical audit services are given in PIDs 12 and 13.
I. MONITORING, SUPERVISION AND EVALUATION
3.26   Monitoring indicators have been developed in consultation with the LGED (Annex 3.7) to
show the progress with respect to physical and financial targets on each component. Time-bound
monitorable quantitative and qualitative targets have been designed to measure implementation success
and progress of project components. For evaluation of project objectives and development impact,
performance indicators have been proposed, which will show the status at project start and progress
achieved by mid-term review and project completion. In addition to normal SEM&E studies and
surveys to assess impact of proposed project on traffic levels and transport costs, a larger socio-
economic impact study which will focus on distribution of project benefits and poverty impact, will be
carried out under the project (para. 2.34). All monitorable targets and performance indicators at the
sector level, project level and beneficiary level (shown in Annex 3.7) have been reviewed during
negotiations. The dispersed nature of the project, the involvement of different agencies, tiers of
government and communities and large number of small contracts would necessitate the use of
computerized project management (CPM) software (for example, see the output of software in Annex
3.4) for effective monitoring of project progress. LGED would make use of CPM (preferably
Microsoft Project software) for monitoring of project progress and in the preparation of quarterly
project reports, which would also summarize the activities of the past period, emerging problems and
issues, and recommendations for further steps.
3.27   Project supervision would be closely linked to the project objectives and the annual
implementation plans. It would involve monitoring a number of key indicators and assessing both
quality and quantity aspects of project implementation and their impact. In addition, the PIO would
oversee supervision of project activities to assess field implementation progress and problems,
involvement of beneficiary participation, and impact. The IDA would field normally two missions in a
year, each of about two or three weeks duration, to focus primarily on key implementation issues. The
first mission would concentrate on detailed review of the annual work plan at the onset of budget year;
the second would resolve problems, assess benefits, and draw lessons. The missions would review the
physical components of the project and the institutional and policy development measures proposed
under the project, and it would be done in the context of agreed targets. The LGED will co-ordinate
and provide all necessary information to these missions. Supervision between missions will be assisted
by the infrastructure unit in the Bank's Resident Mission. Main supervision missions (which are
expected to be two per year) will be done in coordination with SDC, which has agreed to actively
support project supervision. In case efforts of coordination in mission would result in timing conflict
between IDA and SDC, the mission timing proposed by IDA, the lead agency, would prevail.
J. LAND ACQUISITION
3.28   Most of the improvement works would be on existing alignments, with some additional land
needed for widening at a few points. Land acquisition of marginal pieces of land for feeder road
improvement will be needed (about 225 hectares, spread over about 575 km of roads in 14 districts;
this equates to about 0.4 ha of land per km of road, or strips of land 2.2 meter wide on either side of
the existing embankments). Rural families welcome proximity to an improved road as it gives better



45
access, and increases land values. Affected households will lose narrow strip of land from their plots.
Arrangement for prior payments of compensation based on market rates, before work starts on any
land to be acquired, has been agreed. No resettlement is expected to be involved. If the land
acquisition reduces the home-stead plot to less than an agreed minimum size, the household will be
fully compensated for purchase of a replacement plot; the number of such cases would be small, and
their location cannot be identified until the land acquisition is under way for each road. In the unlikely
event that genuine squatters are found on any land to be acquired, contingency resettlement measures
have been agreed. IDA reviewed the Land Acquisition Action Plan (Annex 3.8) prepared by the
Government and it was substantially agreed. Agreement was reached during negotiation with
GOB/LGED on the Land Acquisition Action Plan including the following: (a) grievances procedures
including UP members in the process: (b) monitoring by LGED. and periodic performance evaluation
by an independent local individual or agency: and (c) acceptance in principle of resettlement
procedures for affected persons to be applied if needed. A major difficulty observed in processing the
land acquisition payment, is the time consuming bureaucratic procedure for according administrative
approval by LGD of the land proposed to be acquired. In order to expedite the processing of land
acquisition payments, it was agreed during negotiations that LGD would set up a commnittee headed by
a Deputv Secretary (who will directly report to the LGD Secretary) with representatives from LGED
before Board presentation, which will: (a) ensure that the Ministry's administrative approval is given
within seven days of request by LGED, (b) liaise with respective district commissioners to speed up
the process of land acquisition and compensation. and (c) ensure that LGED will report to IDA on
progress of land acquisition. This committee has been set up in October 1996.
K. ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
3.29   An environmental analysis of the project was carried out by reviewing each project component
in accordance with Bank guidelines. Based on this analysis, the project was categorized as "B". The
project will have substantial positive environmental impact, and is not expected to have adverse impact
on the physical or ecological environment. No new road alignments are involved and the proposed
works are improvement, upgrading and maintenance of existing roads. The road works will have
positive environmental impacts - the provision of drainage structures will reduce water logging, the
bitumen surfacing will eliminate the dust hazard, the hard shoulders will improve safety, and trees will
be planted alongside all project roads.  The project will encourage the use of NMT which is
environmentally friendly. The physical environment, health standards and quality of life in the markets
and ghats will be improved through provision of clean, dry, free-draining areas, potable water supplies
and sanitation: bio-gas production from the garbage pits will be tried on a pilot basis in some market
improvement projects. Mitigation measures acceptable under IDA guidelines would be taken to avoid
any adverse environmental impact during project implementation. An environmental impact study and
Sectoral Environmental Assessment (SEA) have been carried out by the LGED with the assistance of
consultants. The SEA, and the proposed mitigation measures, monitoring plan and recommended
actions have been reviewed with IDA, and are acceptable under IDA's Environmental Assessment
Guidelines. LGED has agreed to ensure: (a) adequate provisions for waste water and drainage facilities
in the design of rural markets and ghats; (b) the embankment slopes in the design of FRB and
structures, to maintain soil stability; and (c) erosion protection measures, where necessary. It was
agreed during negotiations that LGED will take actions in conformity with SEA.



46
4. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION & RISKS
A. PROJECT BENEFITS AND ECONOMIC RETURN
Nature of project benefits
4.1    The project would benefit the project area through improvement in access and more efficient
marketing, and would also bring several other socio-economic benefits which are difficult to quantify.
The following discusses the general nature of benefits based on previous impact studies, and also
presents a partial economic return analysis of the proposed project based on easily quantifiable
benefits.
4.2    The critical importance of improving the condition of rural roads and markets in Bangladesh as
a way to improve rural income and reduce poverty has been recognized in the country's development
strategy.  The Rural Infrastructure Strategy study carried out by the IDA in collaboration with
GOB/LGED confirms that much of the rural road system, and most of the important markets, remain
in poor condition; it also found that investment on improving these facilities is essential to develop
rural economy and to reduce rural poverty, and recommends a larger investment program than
undertaken in the past decade. Recent IDA/GOB study on agricultural strategy also underlines the
need for improving rural infrastructure in support of agricultural diversification and growth, and
IDA's Country Assistance Strategy emphasizes rural transport development in view of its impact on
agriculture, rural economic growth, employment and poverty alleviation.
4.3    There is evidence to demonstrate the strong relationship between socio-economic well-being
and rural infrastructure supply, in the light of various studies in Bangladesh. Studies further show that
these investments benefit the rural poor more: the functionally landless and small farmers gained a
larger share of the increase from crops, wages, and livestock and fisheries, and there was a higher
proportion of employment of women.  Socio-economic monitoring and evaluation studies under
different LGED RDP projects show that improvement of FRBs produces a significant increase in non-
motorized and motorized transport services, a shift to more efficient transport modes, lower transport
costs for goods and passengers, a reduction in the dependence on travel by foot and head-porterage for
carrying of goods, and a substantial increase in traffic volumes, with overall improved access to goods
and to social services.  The same studies show that the improvement of rural market facilities,
combined with FRB improvements, leads to increases in throughput of goods, number of market
visitors, market turnover and market lease value, to reduced spoilage of goods, with the markets
developing into more efficient and hygienic trading centers for the poor farmers and vendors who are
their main users. More specifically, post appraisal of completed project components under the ongoing
RRMIMP-1 (RDP-7) show that traffic volumes in ton-km and passenger-km increased over 70% in
cargo and over 172 % in passenger traffic on the improved roads in the first year following
development, that bus services started for the first time on these roads which brought major benefits to
local people, and that partial quantified benefits from the project showed about 22% (range 14 - 45%)
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) from road investments and from market investments. Further,
estimates based on surveys of employment created under the completed rural infrastructure components
show, that on completion in 1997, these components, costing about US$64 million equivalent, would
have created: about 18,000 person-years of direct employment through labor-based construction
activities, and indirect employment at a rate of about 6000 person-years per year (in maintenance about
1000, and the rest in rickshaw-van and other transport operations, in markets, new road-side shops
etc.) which would be continued during the project life.



47
4.4    The Project Area: The Northwest Region remains economically and socially disadvantaged -
agricultural wage labor rates, GDP per capita, and male and female adult literacy rates are all below
the national average. Communications between the Northwest Region and the rest of the country will
be improved with the completion of the Jamuna Multi-Purpose Bridge; further development of the road
and market network will allow the area to exploit the economic opportunities that this will provide.
The rural Greater Dhaka Districts (except one) did not benefit from any RDPs, so far, and have severe
deficiencies in its rural transport and market infrastructure to serve its fast-growing high-price
horticultural crops which need timely transport and marketing; this area has higher population density
and higher traffic (NMT-dominated) than the North-West Region. Even after the completion of the
proposed project, both these areas would still meet only about 50-60% of the estimated current
requirements in infrastructure improvements.
Economic Return Analysis
4.5    The project's Economic Rate of Return (ERR) has been estimated based on quantifiable
benefits with reference to three main components: FRB road improvements, growth-center markets,
and Structures on Rural Roads (SRRs), as summarized below; a composite ERR for the project was
then estimated based on these three components.
4.6    FRB road improvements: The detailed analysis of economic costs and benefits of improvement
and upgrading of FRB roads, is given in PID-15. The analysis is at 1995 prices. The costs in the
economic analysis comprise the estimated investment in road improvement and upgrading and
construction of structures, land acquisition and tree plantation, routine and periodic maintenance over
the predicted 15 year life of the roads, and 7% of construction costs as an allowance for design and
supervision. Taxes, duties and contingencies are excluded. Costs of imported goods are taken at
border prices (cif prices), and the local costs are adjusted to border prices using a general conversion
factor of 0.9. Similar adjustments are also made for the value of benefits. The economic benefits
accrue to savings in vehicle operating costs (VOC). For vehicles, VOC savings are estimated from
input prices and cross-checked and verified against actual tariff charges in the rural transport market.
For non-motorized means of transport VOC savings are based on actual differentials in charges
between existing poor and improved roads, as they substantially reflect the cost variations due to
greater exertion and energy levels (food as fuel) needed for rougher roads, in the highly competitive
markets for these services. A good body of comparative data on these charges has been collected in
Bangladesh from past studies under ongoing projects and through surveys for the proposed project
which provide a consistent pattern of cost differences for different road conditions and vehicle types.
Impact studies on completed projects (see para. 1.49 and Annex 1.4) also show a consistent pattern of
changes in vehicle types as roads are improved, for example from walking to bicycles and buses, from
bullock carts to rickshaw vans and small trucks, etc. which provide reliable estimates of traffic pattern
and growth. Transport cost differences are estimated based on changes in modal-mix assumed based
on such information. Bench-mark traffic surveys have been made for all 69 roads selected for the
project.
4.7    Traffic growth has been estimated as follows: annual 8% for normal growth (about 1.5 times
the expected economic growth rate, based on available country-wide estimates); a generated traffic due
to road improvement using a price elasticity of demand for goods and passenger transport of -1 with
reference to transport cost reductions, and for diverted traffic based on the actual potential as assessed
through surveys as this varies from case to case. Overall , the traffic projected is very conservative
compared to actual growth recorded in the case of similar road improvements completed under the
IDA-SDC-KfW -supported RRMIMP-1, and under the GTZ - supported RDP-14, where traffic
increased steeply (motor vehicles and NMT vehicles by 160% and 146% respectively; passenger traffic
volume in passenger-kms by over 172% mainly because of starting of bus services, and cargo ton-kms



48
by 70%) the pre-development level within about a year after road development; this shows that the
dynamism of economic and traffic growth is higher than conventionally assumed growth rates, and that
even as a road is improved (preceded by a long process of preparation and public awareness) change
begins to take place without much time-lags. The traffic assumptions made in this study lead to an
overall traffic growth of the following order for a typical road: total traffic increase (in ton-kmn and
passenger-kin) after three years after work completion (giving time for full generated traffic and traffic
diversion to come about) of about 55% in passenger and 104% in goods, the latter consisting of about
26%  normal growth in three years (at cumulative annual rate of 8%), 37% generated traffic (total one-
time growth spread over three years) and 41 % diverted traffic (total one-time growth); after the three
years, total traffic is assumed to increase at the normal growth rate of 8%. Compared to the post-
appraisal traffic growth rates noted above, these projections are very conservative, particularly for the
passenger traffic. The transport cost differences are estimated using changed modal mixes and costs,
following development, as discussed earlier, taking into account the characteristics of each road. Full
transport cost savings are included as project benefits for normal traffic growth, and only 50% savings
for generated and diverted traffic as well.
4.8    The economic rate of return on the investment in FRB improvement and upgrading, based on
the transport cost savings, is estimated as 30.4%. The flow of costs and benefits for a sample project
road, and other details of the analysis are presented in PID-15.
4.9    SRRs:  The main economic significance of SRR investment is that an investment of about
US$8,000 on an SRR will convert about 1.75 km of existing rural road (costing about US$70,000)
from a seasonal to all-season road. The economic analysis of the Structures on Rural Roads (SRR)
component (see PID-16) is based on a 'typical investment scheme to construct small structures on a
length of Rural Road in order to span two gaps and provide a continuous road alignment. The costs in
the analysis include the investment in the construction of the structures and the ancillary earthworks,
routine and periodic maintenance of the road and the structures over the predicted 15 year life, and 7%
of construction costs as an allowance for design and supervision. The benefits from the investment
have been derived based on field surveys of a sample of Rural Roads with and without gaps. These
show that the major benefit from providing continuous alignments is to eliminate the delays that occur,
particularly in the wet season, in crossing gaps or diverting to other, longer routes to avoid the gaps.
The spanning of the gaps will stimulate the growth of wheeled traffic and generate passenger and
goods movement on the road. The benefits are estimated as the time savings to normal and generated
traffic - pedestrians, passengers and vehicles - from eliminating the delays caused by gaps plus the
savings in ferry charges - 'informal' ferry services operate across some gaps during the wet season.
The base case economic rate of return for the SRR component, based on a 'typical' scheme is 30.2%.
The flow of costs and benefits for a typical SRR scheme is presented in PID-16. Nearly 30% of the
benefits accrue to the poorest beneficiaries, the pedestrians who continue to travel on foot after the
structures have been constructed because the cannot afford to use other means of transport.  The
component will benefit them in terms of better access to work, to markets and to social services.
4.10   The SRR component will comprise a large number of small investment schemes (each structure
(usually a culvert) costing about US$8,000 equivalent, with significant variability in the structures
required and the traffic volumes between individual schemes. Tests have therefore been carried out to
analyze the sensitivity of the rate of return to these variables. If the size of the structures required is
larger than in the typical scheme, costs will increase. A cost increase of 20% reduces the rate of
return to 25%, and a cost increase of 50% reduces it to 19%. Costs must increase by over 120% to
bring the rate of return down to 12 %. If traffic volume is less than in the typical scheme, benefits will
decrease. A decrease of 20% in traffic volume reduces the rate of return to 24%. A traffic volume
45%  of that on the 'typical' scheme is sufficient to give an economic rate of return of 12%. The
economic justification for the SRR component is therefore very robust.



49
4.11   Growth Centre Markets: Many Growth Centre markets (public facilities for trading produce
and goods with basic facilities for cleanliness, drainage and paved areas partly under roof, each
costing about US$60,000 a piece) will be improved at the same time that the FRBs that serve them are
improved and upgraded; in some cases roads are already improved. It is therefore difficult to isolate
the respective contributions of the market investment and the road improvements. One benefit that is
specific to the market improvements is the reduction in spoilage of marketed produce. Because the
improved market is clean, and the produce is protected from the elements and sold more quickly,
spoilage losses for items such as rice, fish, meat, eggs, milk and vegetables are reduced.  The
economic analysis for market improvement (PID-17) is therefore based on the benefits from reduced
spoilage - this can be seen as a proxy for the increased profitability to sellers of produce directly due to
the market improvements. The costs included in the analysis are the investment in the improved
facilities, the routine and periodic maintenance over the assumed 15 year life of the market and 7% of
construction costs as an allowance for design and supervision. The RRMIMP SEM&E studies show
that market improvements reduce spoilage losses by about 3 % of the value of the vulnerable produce.
Based on the characteristics of, and the volumes of goods sold in, the Growth Centre markets proposed
for improvement, a spoilage savings of 3% will generate an economic rate of return for the project
markets of 20.2%. The return is higher for large markets (25%) and lower for small markets (14%).
The flow of costs and benefits for a medium-sized market is presented in PID-17. These rates of
return are based on partial benefits since:
(a)   there are other economic benefits to market users, particularly the regular traders, from
the much more hygienic conditions in the improved markets; (the usually dirty and
stinking market areas where the poor vendors trade would turn into clean dry areas,
garbage giving place to clean bio-gas in some cases where small low-cost pilot
facilities will be installed for the purpose); moreover, new permanent private shops
open up around the public markets while existing shops expand, land values and rents
increase).
(b)   the integration of investments in improvement of markets, roads and ghats will together
stimulate increased trading and economic activity at the markets. It is reasonable to
argue that a proportion of these benefits is attributable to the market investment.
4.12   Seen as a joint investment with roads (with market investment being about 10%  of the
investment on a typical 7 km road improvement, the latter producing about 30% ERR), the investment
has high ERR even if market benefits are excluded. Testing of the sensitivity of the economic rate of
return to the key assumptions made in the SRR analysis shows that if the costs are 20% higher than
estimated the rate of return is 17%. If the spoilage savings is only 2.4% rather than the 3.0%
predicted, the rate of return is 16%. A spoilage savings of 2% is sufficient to give an economic return
rate of 12%.
4.13   The composite rate of return for the three components for which economic analysis has been
carried out ( weighted average) is 29.5%. Table 4.1 shows that the three components for which
economic analysis has been carried out represent 71 % of the total financial cost of the project. The
economic costs included in the analysis amount to 75.5% of the total economic cost of the project.
The components that have not been analyzed will also generate economic benefits. The construction of
'Structures on Other FRB' can be expected to have similar impacts to the SRRs. The road safety
measures will reduce losses due to accidental injury and death. Periodic maintenance of roads is
known to be economically efficient, and the ghat improvements will generate benefits through reduced
spoilage and increased safety. The pilot component to improve NMT will ultimately lead to more
efficient provision of rural transport services, and the technical assistance will increase the efficiency



50
of LGED as a rural infrastructure provider. Further, as noted above, there are additional, unquantified
economic benefits from the three components that have been analyzed.
Other Development Impacts
4.14    The rate of return can be regarded as being based on partial benefits.  While transport cost
savings are the mechanism through which many of the economic impacts are generated, given that
transport and trading permeates so many aspects of rural life in Bangladesh the provision of improved
access will generate other effects such as new income-generation opportunities, faster diffusion of
agricultural technologies and increased utilization of health and educational services.

4.15    The main project beneficiaries will be the rural population within the influence of the project.
These investments are extensive and widely dispersed, and a substantial proportion of the population of
the project area will benefit. The project will have a direct impact on the income of farmers and farm
laborers, market traders, transport operators, construction and maintenance workers including
disadvantaged women, small and cottage industries, contractors, workshops and local manufacturing
industries, and local government bodies. More generally, the rural population will benefit through
increases in economic activity, employment and income-generation and through better access to work-
places, and to health, education, administrative and social services.  The improved transport and
trading system should lead to lower prices for basic commodities. Small and cottage rural industries
will benefit from improved access to raw materials and to markets. Rural health conditions will be
improved through the provision of clean, dry, hygienic facilities at rural markets and ghats.
4.16    The project will  also develop the capacity of local government bodies to assume greater
responsibility for local-level infrastructure and its financing, and will increase the participation of rural
communities in sustainable infrastructure development. The use and promotion of appropriate labor-
intensive technology in road improvement and maintenance will leave behind a valuable skill in the
rural areas for easily affordable maintenance systems strengthening future sustainability. The project



51
will also strengthen LGED' s management and implementation capacity and capability as the
government agency with the main responsibility for rural infrastructure, and will increase the
capability and involvement of the private sector in rural infrastructure development.
4.17   Employment and poverty reduction:  The Project is part of IDA's program  of targeted
intervention due to its focus on low-income commnunities. The critical importance of improving the
condition of rural roads and markets in Bangladesh as a way to improve rural income and reduce
poverty has been recognized in the country's development strategy. The project focuses on rural areas
where the incidence of poverty is more widespread (see para. 2); its special focus is on Northwest
region, which is the poorest region in the country. Choice of project facilities has given priority to
areas where people suffer from inadequate access and poor market facilities. Overall, the proportion
of the poor among project beneficiaries will be significantly larger than their proportion in total
population (see para. 3). Improvement of markets will provide better and more healthy facilities for
poor market vendors. The use of labor-intensive methods for civil works will create over 20,000
person-years of employment in construction, and more than 2,000 person-years of employment
annually in maintenance which will continue beyond the project period; based on RRMIMP-1
completion studies, other new employment as transport operators (mainly NMT), vehicle repairs and
service, and small trading and shop-keeping could exceed 10,000 persons annually. Most of the
beneficiaries from increased employment will be the landless poor, destitute women, and under-
employed small farmers.  Most works would be done through labor-intensive methods, using
appropriate small equipment, to maximize employment impact. Many construction works, and all
routine maintenance works, will be targeted at the landless and at disadvantaged women, particularly
through the use of labor contracting societies under which poor workers organize themselves as small
contractors thus earning more than the usual wage.
B. PROJECT RISKS
4.18   There are no major risks in proceeding with the project. Project risks have been minimized by
special design features and measures taken or built into project preparation and implementation. The
project concept has already been tested successfully through the ongoing RRMIMP-1. The project has
been carefully designed to achieve effective implementation, and many of its features such as definition
of the PIO management structure, incremental staffing, tasks of the DSM consultants, and monitoring
procedures, are modeled on the ongoing project experience with necessary improvements based on
lessons learnt. LGED has demonstrated its capability to implement projects efficiently and coordinate
effectively with donors, and the project will further strengthen its management capacity. To sustain the
flow of benefits from project investments, Government funds are available for FRB maintenance, and
LGED has already established the basis of a maintenance management system, which will be further
supported through the project components.
4.19   The following assesses potential project risks, and measures adopted to minimize them: the
risks discussed relate to: (a) possible constraints in contractor capacity to implement the project which
is much larger than the ongoing project and is to be implemented in a shorter period; (b) possible
constraints in LGED's implementation capacity for the larger project; (c) delays in land acquisition, (d)
the large national component on rural structures which was not in the first project; (e) possible
failure of the participatory processes being tried out for the first time on such large scale; and (f)
inadequate maintenance funding and arrangements.



52
(a) The project size and contractor capacity
4.20   Reference is invited to Annex 3.3 which  presents a full analysis of project size increase
compared to the ongoing first project, and assesses the capacity of contractors and LGED to implement
such a larger project. The proposed project is about 50% larger than the ongoing project comparing
levels of total physical output; considering also its shorter implementation period of five years, the
annual output level is about 107% more; if a six-year implementation period is considered, the annual
output is about 72% higher. Against this, the project area is about 75 % larger (14 districts versus 8),
which means that there is also about 75% increase in contractor capacity as the small size contractors
to do feeder road works and markets and river ghats are generally available in all districts, their
operations being generally limited by logistical problems to one or two districts; the project would
provide training and other support , including equipment on lease, to enhance the quality and capacity
of these contractors; in fact, recent bidding under retroactive financing for 45 km of roads (about 11
contracts) under the new project in the new project districts (greater Dhaka) has already shown an
average qualified bidders per contract.
4.21   Thus, about 75% capacity increase can be explained from the increased project area with its
own contracting capacity. The remaining extra capacity (of about 32%) is expected from the fact that
the new project is not a pilot project and is built on lessons already learnt from the first project in
trying out various specifications, standards and procedures.   LGED  have also become more
experienced; the new project has also many measures to increase contractor efficiency and capacity
(larger size contracts of average 4-6 km, with 2-year contracts, provision of mobilization advances for
buying own equipment, as against one-year contracts of 2-3 km without mobilization advance under the
first project; higher standards in prequalifying contractors, more training etc.); it also will give about
100 km of the roads to a new category of medium size contractors (for 10 km, US $ 1 m value
contracts) who currently work on the main roads projects under RHD, who will infuse new capacity to
the feeder road sector). In addition, 45 km FRB improvements have already started under retroactive
financing before project approval. Many initial delays which affected the first project would be avoided
by making the appointment of supervision consultants and bidding for first year works a condition
before Board presentation. The second project is much better prepared and its implementation in all
aspects would be more efficient. No significant risk in contractor capacity is therefore expected.
4.22   A sensitivity analysis shows that if a 6-year implementation period is used instead of 5 years,
the extra output and capacity needed is only 72% above the first project, which can be fully met from
the capacity gain due to area expansion; the factors of efficiency increase noted above will, in this
case, create a slack in capacity and give a large extra cushion. Therefore, if implementation is found
lagging, a decision can be made at mid-term review to extend the implementation period by one year or
to restructure the project with more allocation for fast moving components.
4.23   As regards the national components, the SRR components would be done mainly through a
different category of very small labor contractors (contracts of about $8000 each), for which there is
no shortage (see para. below on SRR). The NMT component would be implemented almost entirely
through experienced local NGOs with minimal supervision from LGED.
(b) LGED capacity to implement the project
4.24   LGED capacity to implement the project as noted has expanded very much under various
management improvement measures, and its institutional capacity would be further enhanced through
the project and through other donor support. LGED has become a much more experienced body over
the past decade (now implementing about US$1 billion worth of projects portfolio mostly funded by
foreign agencies) and is rated a successful and dynamic agency; (see para. 1.42). In addition, the



53
project would provide incremental staff in the project areas, will have a larger PIO with deputy
director for the new project districts as they need special attention and another Deputy Director to look
after the SRR components through participatory specialists to be recruited, and additional senior staff
for project monitoring and other functions (see para. 3.6), it will also have a large DSM consultants
support appropriate for the larger size project. A new feature would be technical audit by a private
sector firm, which will carry out regular six-monthly inspection of work verification and technical
quality as an additional  control and monitoring tool  to ensure  efficient and timely project
implementation. All these measures would minimize any possible risk about LGED's capacity to
manage the project.
(c) Delays in land acquisition
4.25   Procedure for acquisition of land in accordance with OD 4.30 will be more rigorous than on
the RRMIMP-1 in respect of compensating all affected persons at full market value of losses, and
paying compensation prior to commencement of construction works on the land to be acquired. The
risk is that land acquisition will take longer than planned so that implementation of FRB improvement
and upgrading works is delayed. This risk has been reduced by the following factors: Most works are
on existing alignments, and only marginal additional land needs to be acquired; LGED has experience
in land acquisition in the first project though compensation in that project was not insisted to be paid
before start of works (the typical land acquisition is for marginal addition to road width on existing
roads from land -owners alongside roads, who in many cases are willing to give land free for road
improvement since improved road will give them much better access to markets and health ,school and
other facilities, and will also enhance value of their remaining land); LGED has a decentralized field
office network which would help them to implement and monitor land acquisition work in
coordination with the various land-related agencies and local affected persons and UPs; LGED land
acquisition capacity will be reinforced with incremental staffing in each District and DSM consultant
inputs; and, a realistic action plan for land acquisition has been agreed.
(d) Large National SRR component
4.26   It is true that this is the first time a nationwide component with such participatory process is
being designed. However, risks of its failure are minimized by many factors. As noted (see para.
2.17) there is general enthusiasm among UPs, based on preliminary interviews, to go for this
component, as it meets a critical local need, with only 10-20% contribution of UP funds. The 6000
structures under the project could be absorbed by 1-2 structures in each UP, or more likely with more
in some UPs since a competitive system has been designed for eligibility. Though the initial selection
process may be delayed, their construction period is only about 2 months each. Contractor capacity
already exists as demonstrated by another ongoing nationwide structures project funded by USAID;
and the proposed project's efforts to involve labor-contracting societies will further enhance capacity.
LGED has already helped build about 1200 such rural structures with good success under the first
project (though selection was not participatory), and this has already established a good supervisory
and financial control system. The work will be supervised by LGED's Thana engineers who operate
in all Thanas; DSM engineers will also supervise the work on a selective basis. An experienced
Deputy Director will coordinate and provide overall supervision for the PIO. Even though UPs have a
major voice in selection and implementation, contracts will be awarded through TDCC Tender
Committees (which has LGED and UP representation) under IDA procurement procedures, as done
for the first project, and project accounts will be maintained as under the first project; therefore, there
is no risk of lack of financial control; private financial auditors and "technical performance" auditors
(new feature) would further keep close watch through sample checks of quality and financial aspects
with special emphasis on this component.



54
(e) Insufficient community participation
4.27   Effective community participation is an important innovative feature. It is intended to achieve
selection of SRR schemes and design of market and ghat facilities to meet local priorities and user
preferences, and to develop community ownership of rural infrastructure. This being a new feature
and a slow process, there is a risk this may slow down the project components or may not be effective.
The factors reducing this risk are: GOB's and LGED's own present strategies emphasize community
participation, and LGED's commitment has already been shown through training schemes they have
started under project preparation phase and their action (already taken) to train and designate some of
their local staff as social organizers; the project further provides some expatriate support and additional
Laval experts to assist LGED to facilitate the process, and there will be close supervision and
monitoring of this component.
0) Inadequate maintenance
4.28   While lack of adequate maintenance is a usual risk, the present project should be considered
free from it. This risk has been minimized by: LGED's and Government's willingness to increase
maintenance budget allocations to adequate levels as observed during the current year (see para. 2.17),
LGED's ongoing special efforts to improve maintenance efficiency which will be assisted through the
project's maintenance component, and the project's provisions seeking Government assurances for
providing adequate maintenance funds in the project area and nationally.
Sensitivity Analysis
4.29   Most of the risks mentioned above, if they materialize, would express themselves as delays in
project completion and deferment of benefits and possibly increased costs. These situations are
analyzed through the Table 4.2 which shows that the project will yield an ERR above 17%, exceeding
even under very adverse situations (of costs up by 20%, along with benefits down by 20% or deferred
by one year); moreover, this ERR estimation excludes several project benefits.  The project is
therefore free from significant economic risks, even if some delays and slippages occur.

4.30   The project's monitoring system provides indicators and warning signals to enable action to be
taken if delays or other problems materialize. In the event of significant, unforeseen delays, the
comprehensive Mid-Term Review would provide an opportunity to revisit project design and to
restructure it with light of actual performance of various components to incorporate lesson learned.
The road sections and markets and ghats are largely discrete, separate, items, and excluding some of
them from the final project at that stage would not reduce benefits from the remaining components.
However, the overall assessment is that such risks are minimal.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROAD AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
THE LOCAL GOVERNMENT SYSTEM,
AND MANAGEMENT OF MARKETS AND GHATS
1.    Introduction
Local Government is an important partner in the RRMIMP-2 for several reasons:
1.    Investment in rural infrastructure development continues to be a priority in Bangladesh.
Several Rural Development Projects (RDPs) such as the RRMIMP-2 provide this rural infrastructure.
This will increase the future requirements for maintenance for which responsibility lies to a great
extend with Local Government.
2.    In crder to make sure that the real needs of the rural population are addressed it is important to
associate Local Government and the local population from the planning stage on.  Responsible
management and maintenance of the rural infrastructure will improve when Local Government and the
population are involved early on in the process of provision of the infrastructure. Their participation
in planning and monitoring is therefore a necessary aspect of the project. Institutional strengthening
will improve the capacity of local governments to take up these tasks.
3.    The financing of infrastructure maintenance and investments is another aspect of the Local
Governments' responsibilities. The mobilisation of local resources, both to contribute to the cost of
infrastructure investment, and to finance the maintenance of certain types of rural infrastructure, is also
important in ensuring that infrastructure investment schemes meet local priorities, in developing a
sense of local ownership of the improved infrastructure, and in achieving sustainability of the
infrastructure investment. Finding ways to increase local resources will be necessary if the local
governments are to take up their responsibilities.
2.    Evolution of Local Government in Bangladesh
Local Government exists in the sub-continent since ancient times. In the Rig Vedas, the oldest Hindu
religious writings, dating from approximately 1200 B.C., forms of village self-government are
mentioned. Panchayets (village councils) seem to have existed since very early times even if their
functions, power and their place in the system of self-government vary considerably.
Under the early British rule a policy of curbing the powers of indigenous local institutions was
adopted, but by the end of the 19th century the need for responsible local government was recognised
and introduced by passing the "Local Self-Government Act of 1885". The "Bengal Village Self-
Government Act of 1919" introduced more representative local government bodies. The Union Boards
comprised two-thirds of elected members and one-third nominated, they covered 10 to 12 square miles
and a population of about 6,000 to 8,000 people. At the higher up level Local Board members were
chosen by open election and finally at the upper local government level, District Boards were for two-
thirds elected and one-third nominated.



59
Annex 1.1
Page 2 of 18
After the partition of India in 1947, the Government of Pakistan retained the system of Union and
District Boards - the intermediate local boards had slowly died out - , this situation persisted until
1959.                                                                                  The
military junta which staged a coup d'etat in 1958 introduced an authoritarian government and
promulgated the "Basic Democracies Order". In contrast with earlier local government enactments,
this order was a central legislation having jurisdiction over the whole of Pakistan.  The local
government system consisted of four tiers in rural areas: Union Councils, Thana Councils,
District Councils and Divisional Councils. One-third of the Union Councils were nominated but in the
three higher up Councils the official nominees could represent up to 50 percent of the membership.
In 1972 soon after the emergence of Bangladesh all local bodies except the Divisional Council were
dissolved and administrators appointed to take over their functions. The Divisional Council was later
abolished. In 1976 the new government promulgated a Local Government Ordinance with three tiers:
Union Parishad, Thana Parishad and Zila Parishad. After Ershad took power in 1982, local
govermnent was reorganised with the Thana as focal point of administration. Responsibility for all
developmental activities at the local level was transferred to the Thana Parishads, renamed Upazila
Parishads. The central government retained direct responsibility for all major development activities.
After the political upheavals of 1990, the new democratic government which came to power in 1991
abolished the Upazila Parishads. Further legislation created a two tier local government with Union
Parishads and District Parishads. At the thana level a Thana Development Co-ordination Committee
was created and at the village level a Village Development Committee.
3.    The Actual Situation of the Union Parishads (UPs)
The Union Parishad is an elected representative body. Its functions have been defined in the Local
Government (Union Parishad) (Amendment) Act, 1993, which lists about 38 functions, the most
important of these concern:
* maintenance of security;
* adoption and implementation of development schemes;
* promotion of family planning;
* development and use of local resources;
* protection and maintenance of public property such as roads, bridges, canals, embankments,
ghats, markets, telephone and electricity lines;
* registration of births, deaths, blind people, beggars and destitutes;
* conducting of census of all kinds;
* etc.
In addition the Union Parishad is also charged with the planning function, the UP is required to
prepare Five-Year and Annual development Plans. Unfortunately most UPs do not have the required
staff to carry out this activity.
The structure and staffing of Union Parishads are detailed in Attachment 1.
In the area of infrastructure the relevant responsibilities of Union Parishads can be summarised as
follows:



60
Annex 1. 1
Page 3 of 18
* Implementation of earthworks and tree-plantation works (excluding FRB works) and of
other infrastructure improvement schemes with a value of less than Taka 50,000.
* Maintenance of Rural Roads - though LGED may maintain some important RR1 roads and
is also responsible for maintenance of Structures on Rural Roads.
* Maintenance, through the relevant management committee, of markets and ghats within the
Union.
The key weaknesses in the new system are of institutional and of financial nature
(a) institutional:
* Unions are at very low level - 4,450 Unions, average area 30sq.km., average population
25,000.
* Union Parishads have very limited staff capacity or capability, and much of the work must
be done by the elected members - yet at the same time progressively increasing demands are
placed on the Union Parishads.
(b) financial:
* The new procedures, responsibilities and revenue powers are not well understood, and
funds do not necessarily flow, nor are they necessarily used, as defined.
* There is a political reluctance for the elected members to collect revenue from 'their
electorate".
4.1    The Union Parishads' Financial Situation
4.1.1  Union Parishad Revenue Sources
Union Parishads have several sources of revenue:
4.1.2  Annual Revenue Budget from Central Government
This contributes towards the costs of honoraria for the UP chairman (33%) and
members (50%), and the salaries of the Secretary, Dafadar and Chowkidars -
in each case the GOB contribution is 50% (see Attachment 1).
4.1.3 Revenue Raised Locally
Following the re-organisation of local government, Union Parishads have the
power, since 1993, to raise revenue from the following sources:
* Annual tax on value of homesteads, or Union 'rate'.
* Taxes on professions, trades and callings.
* Taxes on cinemas, dramatic and theatrical shows, and other entertainments and
amusements of like nature.
* Fees for licences and permits granted by the UP.
* Lease money from specific, bazaars, ghats and ferries within the Union
boundaries as determined by the GOB.



61
Annex 1.1
Page 4 of 18
* Lease money from specific water bodies within the Union boundaries, as
determined by the GOB.
4.1.4  Income From Leasing of Markets. (See Attachment 2)
Union Parishads should receive 15% of the lease value of all markets in the
Union for market development and maintenance; and 40% of the lease value of
markets in the Union with a lease of up to Taka 50,000 in the Bengali Year
1400, for Union development.
4.1.5  Financing at Thana Level for Infrastructure Development
Following the abolition of the Upazila (Thana) Parishads in 1991, and the
abolition of revenue-raising authority at this level, Thana Development Co-
ordination Committees (TDCC) were formed - See Attachment 3. Certain funds
flow to Thana level, and decisions over the allocation of those funds is made
by the TDCC. The key feature of the TDCC is that its voting membership
comprises the Chairmen of the Unions in the Thana. However Thana-level
representatives of Central Government also sit on the TDCC, and the Member
of Parliament acts as an adviser - in practice these persons inevitably have
some influence over decisions.
There are two sources of funds at Thana level:
Income from Leasing of Markets. As per Attachment 2, the Thana receives
10% of the lease revenue from all markets for "market development and
emergency and disaster work" and 40% of the
lease value of all markets with a lease of more than Taka 50,000 in the Bengali
Year 1400 for Thana development.
The Development Assistance Fund for Union and Thana Development -
financed by GOB from the Annual Development Program and known as the
'Block Grant for Union and Thana Development'. See Attachment 4. In
1995/96 the total block grant is Taka 2,000 million, allocated between Thanas
using a formula that takes account of population, land area and 'backwardness'.
The average grant per Thana is Taka 4.4 million, of which up to 60% can be
used for transport and communications works including construction and
maintenance of Rural Roads, small bridges and culverts.
4.2    Union Parishad Revenues: A Survey
At present revenue generation by the Union Parishads is weak. A survey of 15 Unions (by the
Project preparation consultants) indicated that:
*     The average local revenue collected (excluding market leasing) was about Taka 55,000
per Union per annum (range from Taka 21,000 to 135,000). This equates to about
Taka 2 per person, or Taka 5 per adult.
*     The revenue actually collected was only 46% of that budgeted (range 24% to 76%).



62
Annex 1.1
Page 5 of 18
Union Parishads do not exploit effectively their powers to collect revenue from
business activities and leasing of water bodies, though they do collect such items as
village court and pound fees.
*     About 15% of the revenue collected is used to cover collection fees.
*     Various collection methods are used including salaried tax collector, collection agent
on commission basis, village police on commission basis, and collection by UP
members.
Other sources indicate that revenue collection by Union Parishads is typically less than
Taka 100,000 per annum.
The present annual income of a 'typical' Union Parishad is estimated below (though it
should be emphasised that there are considerable variations around this 'typical' situation) is:

the locally collected revenue will be consumed by this item (the estimated

costs. Thus, a typically UP may have Taka 40-50,000 to devote to

4.3    Actual Union Parishad Involvement in Infrastructure Projects
Union Parishads are involved in certain infrastructure development projects because funds flow
to the Unions and/or because the Unions have to make financial contributions:
* The CARE Road Maintenance Project (RMP) which was implemented through the Ministry
of Relief, will transfer to LGED in 1995/96. The project monetises Canadian food-aid and

Annex 1.1
Page 6 of 18
channels funds to Union Parishads for routine road maintenance by crews of destitute
women. Under the revised system in LGED:
0 The project will operate in the majority of Unions (about 3,600) maintaining about
20km of road per Union. A new development is that some of the maintenance work
will be on earthen FRBs and the remainder on RRs, whereas previously it was all on
RRs.
0 Union Parishads will contribute 10% of the cost (Taka 10,000 to 15,000 per Union).
0 The GOB contribution will increase from 10% in 1995/96 to 15% in 1996/97 to 25%
in 1997/98.
* The LGED/CARE IFFD project finances structures and earthworks on rural roads.
Structures works are implemented by the Thana, with a 10% cost contribution from the
Union Parishad (this contribution can come eventually from the block grant). Earthworks,
with payment in food, are implemented through the Union Parishad.
* The WFP-supported GCCR programme is essentially implemented at Thana-level. However
the Union Parishad has to make a 5 % contribution to any small structures built on the roads
using monetised food-aid.
The new EC food-aided IFADEP includes provision for use of food-aid for maintenance of
improved embankments.
* There are also GOB food-for-work schemes, and a new GOB scheme for structures on
Rural Roads.
5.    Co-funding by UPs of Infrastructure Investments under RRMIMP-2
5.1   Structures on Rural Roads (SRR) Scheme
The UPs will participate in the investments at the rate of 10% of the construction cost
from July 1996 to June 1999 and 20% from this date on. Agreement has been reached
with LGED and CARE that LGED would co-ordinate between projects so that the
same figures are used on all projects. Discussion with beneficiaries showed an
enthusiastic response.
5.2    Market Improvement
The average market improvement will cost Taka 2.6 million, the most expensive about
Taka 4.5 million. A 10% local contribution is proposed, average Taka 260,000,
maximum Taka 450,000. The project will construct 136 markets in 97 Thanas, i.e. an
average of 1.5 markets per thana over a five year period. If the local contribution was
paid from the 40% of lease value of larger markets that is allocated to the TDCCs, this
would consume 12% of that allocation.



64
Annex 1.1
Page 7 of 18
5.3    Ghat Improvement
The average ghat improvement will cost Taka 1.7 million. A 10% local contribution is
proposed, average Taka 170,000. The project will construct 41 rural river jetties or
ghats over a five-year period.
6.    Recommendations for the Institutional Strengthening of UPs
Several actions are under way to improve the functioning of the UPs, in particular training by the
National Institute for Local Government (NILG) and the development of the Union Planbook and
training in its use by LGED.
The main problem encountered remains that not enough funds are available to hire qualified personnel
to assume the different tasks assigned to the UPs with the result that the elected members must execute
these tasks. The elected members are often not familiarised enough with these tasks and further
training will be necessary. The project in collaboration with the LGED training cell and NILG will
contribute to this training. As soon as more funds become available (see chapter 7) additional staff
may be hired and will have to be trained
As explained elsewhere community participation is an important issue in the project and the project
will assist UPs in this area with the help of the LGED community organisers.
7.    Recommendations for Increasing UPs' Financial Resources
The main bottleneck for an efficient functioning of the UPs is the scarcity of funds available.
The project will examine this issue and make recommendations in order to increase their resources.
Several areas of possible intervention have been identified.
1. Market Leasing
2. More Efficient Collection from Existing Revenue Sources
3. Transfer of Central Government resources to UPs
4. New Revenue Raising Measures
5. Contributions from Local 'Interest Groups
7.1    Market Leasing
Based on the analysis of market leasing in the project area, lease revenue is distributed
as follows:
Central Government: 35%
Thana-Level:        45%
Unions:             20%
The Union Parishads get only limited direct benefit from the markets within their
Unions.
Lease values are very substantially below the income potential of the markets. Surveys
indicate returns on investment to the leaseholder typically in excess of 200% per
annum.



65
Annex 1. 1
Page 8 of 18
The following measures are to be considered in relation to market lease revenues:
Allowing Union Parishads to lease larger markets, and to retain a greater proportion of
the revenue, would provide an incentive for them to push up lease values. The project
recommends as an up-front action the raising of the lease value threshold from Tk.
50,000 to Tk. 100,000. An in depth study to be executed under the project will
consider further raising of this threshold.
*     Another way to increase UP revenue from market leases would be to reduce the central
governments share and increase the UPs share. The project recommends as an up front
action to reduce the central governments share from 35% to 25% and allow the
additional 10% to go directly to the UPs;
*     Adopting measures to make the leasing process more truly competitive and reducing
collusion would generate substantial additional revenue. Based on the analysis of
market leasing in the project area, if leases were increased by 50% in the project area,
this would generate Taka 100 million per annum - which would cover routine
maintenance cost of 10,000km of Rural Road. Measures include:
- wider advertising of lease bidding (national level)
- monitoring of income from market to set "reserve value" for leases
- encourage NGOs to become involved in bidding for market leases
- tendering done by district level agencies
- appropriated user participation under agreed guidelines
7.2    More Efficient Collection from Existing Revenue Sources
Two direct approaches can be examined:
i)    Focus on collecting more revenue from beneficiaries of infrastructure investments:
c collect licence fees from bicycles, rickshaws, rickshaw vans
- generate revenue from motor vehicles that use the improved roads, e.g. bus stands,
parking fees at markets, mooring fees at ghats
* licensing of businesses that grow up around improved markets and along roads
ii)    General Increase in the Efficiency of Revenue Collection:
* Indexation
* Setting of Targets
* Contracting-Out Revenue Collection
* Incentives for Efficient Revenue Collection
* Collection Camps
Making matching grants available as in the SRR, Market and Ghat components may make tax
collection also more interesting to the UP authorities.



66
Annex 1. 1
Page 9 of 18
7.3    Transfer of Central Government resources to Ups
Another way to increase resources of UPs would be to transfer tax revenues directly to
UPs. An in depth study to be executed under the project will make recommendations in
this area.
As an up front action the project recommends that Government should allocate at least
half of the land-transfer-tax revenue going now to the Zila Parishad (district), to the
UP's generating this revenue, compared to none at present (average allocation from
this source is currently about 10 million Taka per district). Since land value increases
with improved rural infrastructure, this is a good way of levying user charges.
7.4    New Revenue-Raising Measures
Some suggestions have been made in this area.
*Convert disused land to ponds, and lease out
eLegitimise 'non-legal' use of land around markets
*Tree planting scheme on Rural Roads can provide income for UPs (fast-growing ipil-
ipil)
*UPs to collect marriage registration fee
An in depth study will be executed under the project and will make recommendations
in this area.
7.5    Contributions from Local 'Interest Groups':
Community participation work by the project preparation consultants indicates:
*  a willingness by market users to pay more for improved markets if the
improvements reflect their needs (participation in design)
*  willingness by communities to have system for paying for use of structures, e.g.
tolls.
People may even put up money in advance to contribute to investment schemes.
7.6   In Depth Study on UPs Financial Resources
As mentioned above an in depth study will be executed under the project and will make
recommendations on ways to raise UP resources. The terms of reference of this study
is given in Annex 2.12: "Outline Terms of Reference for Studies". (see also
Attachment 4).

ATTACHMENT 1:
STRUCTURE AND STAFFING OF UNION PARISHAD
Each Union consists of three wards. From each ward three members are directly elected to the Union
Parishad (Council) on the basis of adult franchise from the concerned ward. The Chairman of the
Union Parishad (UP) is directly elected on the basis of adult franchise from the whole of the Union. In
addition there are three reserved seats on the Union Parishad exclusively for women members, who are
elected by the nine ward members. Thus a UP comprises 12 members and a Chairnan.
Each UP has a full-time Secretary, appointed by the Deputy Commissioner (DC) of the concerned
District. In addition, each UP employs a Dafadar (a 'local' policeman) and Chowkidars (village
watchmen) who are appointed by the Thana Nirbahi Officer (TNO). Thus none of the UP staff are
directly recruited and employed by the UP.
The UP Chairman receives an honorarium (Taka 500 per month), one-third paid by the GOB and two-
thirds from revenue raised locally by the UP. The ward members receive an honorarium of Taka 200
per month, 50% paid by the GOB and 50% from revenue raised locally by the UP. The salary of the
Secretary is determined by the DC, and is in the range Taka 2,500 - 4,000 per month. 50% of the
salary of the Secretary, and of the Dafadar and Chowkidars, is paid by the GOB and 50% from
revenue raised locally by the UP. Thus the UPs have to contribute, from the revenues that they raise,
to the salaries of their staff and to the honoraria of the Chairmen and members. In practice these
expenditures are the first call on locally-raised revenue.



68
Annex 1.1
Page 11 of 18
ATTACHMENT 2:
THE THANA DEVELOPMENT CO-ORDINATION COMMITTEE DETAILS
Each Thana has a Development Co-ordination Committee (TDCC), whose functions are to:
* Review and co-ordinate project feasibility, area-wise priorities for development projects,
and other matters.
* Prepare development projects.
* Advise UPs in preparing and implementing rural development programs.
* Co-ordinate between locally prepared projects and national development programs.
* Advise on acceptance and implementation of development projects upon the request of the
UPs.
* Resolve inter-Union and inter-sectoral conflicts.
* Review the progress of development projects under implementation in the Thana.
The membership of the TDCC comprises:
* One person from amongst the UP Chairmen who acts as Chairman of the TDCC on a
rotating basis (the chairmanship changes for every meeting).
* The Chairmen of all Union Parishads of the Thana who are voting members of the TDCC.
* Three persons from the female members of the UPs in the Thana (nominated by the
government) who are voting members of the TDCC.
* Heads of different government offices in the Thana who are non-voting members of the
TDCC:
-     Thana Nirbahi Officer
-     Thana Engineer
-     Thana Health and Famnily Planning Officer
-     Thana Education Officer
-     Thana Agriculture Officer
-     Thana Co-operative Officer
-     Thana Livestock Officer
-     Thana Fisheries Officer
-     Thana Social Welfare Officer
-     Police Officer in charge of Thana
-     Thana Ansar and VDP Officer
-     Thana Food Controller
-     Thana Statistical Officer
-     Thana Rural Development Officer
The TNO acts as Secretary of the Committee. Members of Parliament whose constituency includes part
of the Thana act as advisers to the Committee.
The TDCC is important in respect of rural infrastructure development because it is responsible for the
allocation to projects of the Block Grant for Union and Thana Development, and for the allocation of
funds that flow to the Thana from market leasing.

Annex 1.1

ATTACHMENT 3:
THE BLOCK GRANT FOR UNION AND THANA DEVELOPMENT
The nation-wide budget allocation for the Block Grant for Union and Thana Development was Taka
1,000 million in 1993/94, increased to Taka 1,500 million in 1994/95 and Taka 2,000 million in
1995/96. The allocation between Thanas is based on a formula with the following weights - population
50%, land area 25%, and backwardness 25%. On average, the budget equates to an allocation of Taka
2.2 million per Thana (equivalent to Taka 0.23 million per Union) in 1993/94, Taka 3.3 million per
Thana (equivalent to Taka 0.34 million per Union) in 1994/95, and Taka 4.4 million per Thana
(equivalent to Taka 0.45 million per Union) in 1995/96.
The guidelines for allocation of the Block Grant (Government Circular of 25th October 1993) are:
Sector                       Minimum          Maximum
a.    Agriculture and Irrigation                 15%               30%

The guidelines state that priority should be given to:
Completion of unfinished Rural Roads and maintenance of finished schemes.
*  Construction of bridges/culverts on Rural Roads, particularly where one or two structures
will give complete access along a road.
*  Projects which facilitate inter-village and inter-Union communication and bring benefits to
the people.
*  Road-side tree plantation.
Implementation of projects financed by the Block Grant (and from the Thana receipts from market
leasing) is through a Project Supervision Committee comprising the relevant Union Chairman, a
member of the concerned Department at Thana Level, an SAE nominated by the Thana Engineer, and
a concerned ward member of the UP. Infrastructure contracts for earthworks, for tree plantation and
for other works up to Taka 50,000 can be administered by the UP. Other infrastructure contracts are
tendered through a Thana Tender Committee and let by the LGED Thana Engineer. The relevant
Union Chairman, the TNO and the Thana Engineer sit on the Tender Committee. The UP is
responsible for visiting the schemes regularly and reviewing progress. Infrastructure works are
supervised by the Thana Engineer. Funds for works tendered by the Tender Committee do not flow
through the concerned UP accounts, they are managed at Thana level with approval for payment by the
TNO and the Thana Engineer.
