ATTACHMENT 4:
MANAGEMENT OF MARKETS AND GHATS
1.    The Existing Situation
1.1    Market Leasing and Management
1.1.1. Leasing of Markets
A UP may lease one or more markets within the Union boundaries whose lease value does not exceed
Taka 50,000, based on the value in the Bengali year 1400. All other markets are leased by the Thana.
Leases are let annually, and this should be by sealed competitive bidding.
The distribution of revenue from market leasing is shown on the next table. 35% of the revenue is
transferred to central government, and 10% to the Thana. 15% is allocated to maintenance of the
concerned market. Until recently this was reduced to 5% if the market had been improved under an
RDP. However this has now been changed so that the allocation can be increased to 25% for an
improved market - this reflects recognition of the need to maintain the investment in the improved
markets. The balance of 40% is allocated as follows:
For small markets with a lease value up to Taka 50,000, to Union development activities.
For larger markets with a lease value of more than Taka 50,000, to Thana development
activities.
Market Leased by:
Distribution
Union Parishad          Thana
1.    Central government as land           5%                   5%
revenue
2.    Central government as                30%                  30%
market/bazaar revenue
3.    Union Parishad account for           15%                  15%
development and maintenance
of the market
4.    Thana account to be spent for        10%                  10%
market development and
disaster or emergency work in
the Thana
40%                 40%
5.    Remainder                          for Union           for Thana
Development      Development



72
Annex 1. 1
Page 15 of 18
Thus, only limited revenue from market leasing flows to the concerned UP, in particular because of the
division at a lease value of Taka 50,000. Unions obtain little direct benefit from important markets
within their boundaries.
1.1.2  Management of Markets
Each leased market is managed by a Union Market Management Committee (UMMC) which is
responsible for operation and maintenance. The membership of the UMMC comprises:
*     Union Parishad Chairman (chairman of the UMMC)
*     Three Union Parishad members, one from each ward, plus one female member, all to
be nominated by the UP.
*     One Government Officer in the Thana to be nominated by the TNO.
*     One Teacher from a High School in the Union to be nominated by the TNO.
*     The Union Tahsildar.
*     Two Representatives from shopkeepers and traders in the concerned market.
*     The Union Secretary who also acts as Secretary of the UMMC.
However it is reported that many of these UMMCs are not fully effective.
1.2    Ghat Leasing
The situation concerning the leasing of ghats is less clear, ghats can be leased out by:
. the RHD, this is the case when the ghats have been build by RHD and are being used by
ferry launches;
* the district (Zila Parishad), this is case for the majority of the ghats;
* the Union Parishad, in some isolated cases;
* and even in some cases by individuals who have invested in the construction of private
Ghats.
.
Sometimes when a ghat forms an integrated part of a market it can be leased out as part of the market
lease. The proposed project would require Government to set up ghat leasing procedures and ghat
management committees on the lines of UMMC's for the ghats to be completed in the first two years in
consultation with IDA. This process and procedures will be reviewed and refined further in the light of
the UP financial study is finalised.
2.    Weaknesses and Shortcomings of the System
Only limited revenue from market leasing flows to the UPs, in particular because of the lease value
threshold of Taka 50,000. Unions obtain little direct benefit from important markets within their
boundaries.
There is also considerable evidence to show that the present lease auctioning system is not fully
competitive and that cost recovery is very poor. A survey of 20 markets carried out as part of the
RRMIMP Upazila Financing Study in 1992 showed that on average the net income to the leaseholder
from leasing a market - ie. the toll revenue less the lease and the operating costs - was over 300% of
the amount paid to lease the market. In 65 % of the cases the return was more than 200%. Further, the



73
Annex 1.1
Page 16 of 18
return tended to be higher for the markets with lower lease values. This suggests that low lease values
to a considerable degree reflect uncompetitive lease bidding, rather than low income potential of the
markets. A feasibility study for an ADB-financed RDP found similar results. Based on data from eight
markets studied in detail under the RRMIMP SEM&E, the net income to the leaseholder is more than
250% of the lease value.
Many UMMCs do not function effectively. The defined membership puts the committee too close to
the UP; the functions and responsibilities of the committee cf. the market leaseholder are not well
understood; the leaseholder is considered to exercise the real power; the system for allocation of funds
to market maintenance is not fully understood and these funds do not appear to flow. one of the
consequences is that often the leaseholder of the market does not do the cleaning and routine
maintenance of the market. The UMMCs seem often to be powerless to enforce the conditions of the
contract.
3.    Economic Benefits and Cost Recovery from Market Improvements
3.1    Cost of Market Improvements
For project costing purposes, markets have been divided into three categories with different
improvement costs. Markets will be improved in two phases over two years. The costs of
improvement, and of routine and periodic maintenance, are:
Category A (small):  Improvement Cost:   Year 1, Taka 1.14 million
Year 2, Taka 0.76 million
Routine Maintenance: Taka 0.006 million per annum
Periodic Maintenance: Taka 0.036 million per 5 years
Category B (medium): Improvement Cost:   Year 1, Taka 1.56 million
Year 2, Taka 1.09 million
Routine Maintenance: Taka 0.009 million per annum
Periodic Maintenance: Taka 0.053 million per 5 years
Category C (large):  Improvement Cost:   Year 1, Taka 2.16 million
Year 2, Taka 1.44 million
Routine Maintenance: Taka 0.014 million per annum
Periodic Maintenance: Taka 0.074 million per 5 years
Assuming a 15 year life of the market after improvements are completed, with no residual value, and
an interest rate of 12%, the discounted investment and maintenance costs of the improved markets are:
Category A (small):   Taka 1.884 million
Category B (medium): Taka 2.630 million
Category C (large):   Taka 3.588 million
Market improvement costs (including daily cleaning) are borne by the leaseholder. Studies show that
average lease revenue of vendors is high enough to cover al remaining operation and maintenance
costs, and yield a positive financial return on the investment (though often less than 12%), making the
investment sustainable. The large hygienic, health and other social benefits and economic benefits to



74
Annex 1. 1
Page 17 of 18
the poor vendors and farmers further reinforce this investment. Various measures to increase lease
revenue would be considered under this project.
4.    Recommendations for Improving Management and Cost Recovery.
(see also para. 1.35 in SAR)
As in other countries, markets are potentially an important source of income for the development
budget of local government. But as indicated above, UPs get only part of the income (40%) from the
smaller markets and only a fixed lump sum (Tk. 20.000) from the markets with a lease above Tk.
50,000, the main income from the markets goes thus to central and thana level government. This
regulation does not create enough of an incentive to motivate UPs to develop their markets into
dynamic income generating operations. This is probably one of the main reasons for the ineffectiveness
of the Union Market Management Committees. Another reason for their ineffectiveness is probably the
fact that members of this committee are not rewarded for the work they do in this commission.
A number of measures could be taken to increase the part of the income going to the UPs in order to
motivate them into developing the markets, such as: increasing the limit of Tk. 50,000 to Tk. 100,000
or Tk. 200,000 and decreasing the part of central government. But it could also be argued that well
functioning markets are of prime importance for the country and that therefore important incentives
must be given to UPs to efficiently manage and develop their markets. This could be done by letting
them keep all the income generated by the markets and even by providing them with matching grants to
increase proportionally the income generated by the markets. These measures would provide powerful
incentives to UPs to develop these markets and would at the same time provide the UPs with an
important source of revenues. The argument that the income of markets must be distributed to all
unions, also to those that do not have important markets, should be put in the context of the global
resources of the UPs - most of the income of UPs comes from central government anyway and it is
very well possible to compensate UPs that do not have an important market, without taking income
away from UPs that do have an efficient income generating market.
Other measures to improve revenue from markets would be to increase the tolls paid by the market
vendors. Tolls (unit rates per bag, basket or quantity of produce sold) are now the same for all markets
in each district and are fixed by the districts authorities without differentiating between improved and
non-improved markets. Market revenue after development goes up through increased turn-over of
trading even with same unit rate. GOB does not favour unit tariff increase, as this would discourage
the poor vendors to use the unhygienic road side markets thus losing many social benefits. There are
also complaints that the leaseholders often charge more than the official tariffs from the vendors.
The higher toll would translate into higher revenues for the UPs only if the leasing system is fully
competitive, as mentioned above this is not the case and certain measures could be taken to improve
the situation such as the use of more transparent procedures and wide based competition to reduce
collusion in market leasing, with better information sharing arrangements, publicity arrangements,
tendering done by district level agencies and appropriate user participation under agreed guidelines.
Therefore it is recommended for the project to focus on developing the management capability of
market/ghat committees for operation and maintenance. The 'information sharing' approach will
contribute to this but the project will also include:
-     definition of procedures for cleaning and maintenance, removing responsibility from
the leaseholder;



75
Annex 1.1
Page 18 of 18
training of and advice to committees in market/ghat management, including the
preparation of maintenance plans;
monitoring of the flow of funds for, and effectiveness of, maintenance.
However, specific attention will be given to market leases as a source of funds for local revenue
generation. There are two aspects:
i)    the present allocation of market lease revenue gives only limited incentive to UPs to
generate the maximum funds from their markets and to develop those markets. The
present re-distributive mechanism to allocate some of the revenue from a large market
in a particular Union to other Unions in the Thana through the TDCC, is excessive (in
simple terms for markets with a lease revenue of more than Taka 50,000, that part of
the lease revenue which is retained at local level is allocated to the TDCC rather than
the UP);
ii)    at present the potential value of market leasing is not realised, and many of the
leaseholders make large profits - it is variously estimated that the total income from a
market is 3-11 times, and 5 times, the lease value. This is a complex socio-political
issue in the context of rural Bangladesh (one large NGO, BRAC, has become involved
in the leasing of markets but recognises that it is fraught with difficulties). However
practical possibilities have been identified:
-      leasing by users' collectives;
*     increasing the visibility and transparency of the lease bidding process.
*     monitoring of market toll revenue as a basis for assessing lease bid values.
The project includes a number of up-front actions as an interim measure before Board presentation of
the project, and further follow-up measures within about 18 months after Board presentation in the
light of further studies (funded under the project).
(a) The up front actions related to market/ghat leasing and management are given in para. 7.1 of this
annex.
(b) In addition to the above interim measures, a study on improving revenue mobilisation by UP's
including better systems for ghats and markets management and leasing should be completed by about
December 1997 (a year after IDA Board date), and its recommendations reviewed and implemented in
consultation with IDA/SDC before about June 1998. The study should examine, among other things,
(i) the case for and feasibility of increasing unit toll rates of markets after they are developed, and (ii)
suitable procedures for management of ghats with user participation, preferably on the lines of the
UMMCs.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROAD AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAUNTENANCE PROJECT
INSTrnlUTONAL DEVELOPMENT OF
LOCAL GOVERNMENT ENGIEERING DEPARTMENT
1.    Introduction
LGED originated from a small cell in the Local Government Division (LGD) during the late
1970s. This led to the formation of the Works Programme Wing in 1982, and of the Local Government
Engineering Bureau in 1984, which was upgraded to a Department in 1992. LGED's task load has
increased at a very rapid rate during this period of growth. Its tasks have been widened and it now
implements a large portfolio of donor-supported and GOB financed programs. Its responsibilities
include construction and maintenance of FRBs and rural roads, drainage structures and mnarkets; small-
scale water resources schemes; construction of schools and cyclone shelters; food-aided rural
infrastructure programs; provision of technical supervision and advice to local government bodies; and
infrastructure works with pourashavas (secondary towns). Annex 1.4 gives more information about
road and market related rural infrastructure development projects being executed by LGED.
Most of the donor-financed infrastructure projects implemented by LGED include institutional
development activities. In addition:
*      sThe Institutional Support Project (ISP) of the SIDA/NORAD financed Rural Employment
Support Program (RESP) provides long-term institutional support to LGED through three units
- the Training Unit (TU), the Technical Design and Management Unit (TDMU) and the
Physical Planning and Management Unit (PPMU). The third phase of ISP was appraised in
September 1995 and the report finalised in December 1995.
*     In parallel with the RDP-18 project, the Asian Development Bank (ADB) is financing three
technical assistance components - Training, Maintenance Management, and the Management
Capability Strengthening Project (MANCAPS).
The aim of the first phase of MANCAPS is to carry out an institutional and management
analysis of LGED, and make recommendations for its future management development, focusing on
four issues -institutional management, institutional organisation, financial management and engineering
management. MANCAPS has prepared a draft action plan for the management strengthening of
LGED'. The MANCAPS report concludes that:
*  LGED is effective and successful in the implementation of the tasks assigned.
* Its strengths lie in teamn work, field orientation, dynamic leadership, hard work, informal decision
making, and emphasis on training and management of its external relations.
* LGED is now reaching maturity and entering a new phase in its development, where it is important
to consolidate and even further strengthen the organisation.
Draft Action Plan - Directions in Management Development of LGED. MANCAPS, March
1995.



77
Annex 1.2
Page 2 of 5
*  LGED should become the most efficient delivery channel for rural and semi-urban infrastructure
development.
* With tihe rapid increase in work-load most LGED personnel are heavily loaded, and this has led to
a number of ad-hoc organisational arrangements. The current organisation has large management
spans, matrix organisational structure, and complex inter-action with ISP.
The MANCAPS study therefore recommends:
*  A comprehensive re-organisation of LGED to reduce the management span, including the
introduction of a 'circle' level of management between the 64 Districts and headquarters.
* The formation of seven departments at headquarters each headed by a Superintending Engineer,
and the creation of a second Additional Chief Engineer post.
* The integration of ISP functions into the established LGED structure including the creation of an
LGED Design Cell to prepare manuals, specifications and design standards and to provide
technical supervision and co-ordination of the work of design consultants.
* The strengthening of the planning, and management information systems (MIS) functions including
monitoring and evaluation (M&E), and the preparation of a MIS master plan.
The existing LGED organisation structure and the organisation chart proposed by MANCAPS
are attached (Attachments 1 and 2). The IDA/SDC appraisal mission agreed with many of the
recommendations of MANCAPS and, in order to contribute to the management strengthening of LGED
a number of them will be implemented and financed under the project.
The RRMIMP-2 will finance 7 positions as incremental staff:
- The position of a second Additional Chief Engineer in charge of Design, Planning, and Monitoring
and Evaluation (this position is referred to in the organisation chart as Additional Chief
Engineer - 1)
* The position of Superintending Engineer (Design, Quality control and Research and Development)
to head the Design Cell. under the second Additional Chief Engineer.
* Two Assistant Engineer positions (Roads and Structures) in the Design Cell.
* The positions of a Transport Economist and a Monitoring & Evaluation expert in the Monitoring
and Evaluation cell under the second Additional Chief Engineer
* The position of a Sociologist (Participation and Land Acquisition) in the Planning Cell under the
second Additional Chief Engineer.
This will contribute to institutionalising functions now taken up on a temporary basis under
Technical Assistance provided by the ISP. These positions will be funded from the GOB revenue
budget from the fifth year of the project on. This will be done in such a way as to avoid conflict or
duplication with the next phase of the ISP, and SIDA/NORAD have welcomed this participation in the
strengthening of the institutional structure of LGED.
The staffing of these positions will provide the capacity to implement institutional and human
resource development activities under the project in respect of structures and road pavement design
standards, land acquisition policy, participation in rural infrastructure development, and SEM&E.

Page 3 of 5
ADB intends to fund the implementation of MANCAPS recommendations related to improved financial
management and internal auditing. The MANCAPS recommendations concerning the reduction of the
span of control through more delegation of responsibilities and the creation of regional offices (circles)
headed by Superintending Engineers, is in the process of being implemented by the Government. IDA
will seek Government's commitment on implementing main MANCAPS recommendations (see para.
2.32 in SAR).

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE
PROJECT
PRESENT AND PROJECTED
FINANCING OF RURAL INFRASTRUCTURE DEVELOPMENT AND
MAINTENANCE, AND LIST OF ON-GOING PROJECTS
This Annex presents (a) the list of projects managed by LGED, (b) estimate of resources
needed for rural infrastructure development and maintenance over the next 10 years, (c)
estimate of available resources, (d) share of foreign/domestic resources, and (e) estimate of
future Government-budget allocation needed for rural roads maintenance.
(a)    List of projects managed by LGED
1.    LGED, as an efficient and successful agency, is entrusted with most of the
rural infrastructure (rural roads and markets) development and maintenance
projects funded by foreign agencies and the Government. These projects are
generally designated as Rural Development Projects (RDPs) in a series, as they
are designed mainly to implement the rural development strategy which the
Government announced in 1984 (see para. 1.37 and 1.43 in the SAR). Some
of these projects have other titles, and have components other than rural
infrastructure (such as minor irrigation, schools, etc.) Table 1 of this Annex
presents the current list of these ongoing projects, their total costs, annual
allocation for the most recent year, and rough estimate of the value of rural
infrastructure components in each project. The Table shows that the total cost
of the portfolio is about 41.5 billion Taka (about US$1 billion equivalent), and
annual budget allocation (from domestic and foreign funds) about 5.4 billion
Taka; annual allocation for the infrastructure component is about 3.8 billion
Taka in 1995/96.
2.    In addition, there are food-aided rural infrastructure projects, as listed in Table
2 of this annex; this amounts to about 1.4 billion Taka in 1995/96.
(b)    Estimate of Resources needed for rural infrastructure development and maintenance
3.    The Rural Infrastructure Strategy prepared by IDA/LGED  (April 1996 -
Report No. BDSR-15732) estimates future resource requirements for
developing existing rural roads and markets over the next 10 years, as well as
the increasing maintenance costs which would go up as the road network is
upgraded. These are based on an ambitious scenario, and a "realistic scenario"
taking into account mainly implementation capacity. If funds are available, the
realistic scenario can be implemented, but this will require some increase, over
existing level, of annual development and maintenance funds available from
foreign and local sources. Tables 3 and 4 indicate the estimated resource
requirements for development (improvements) and maintenance, respectively,
under the two scenarios.



82
Annex 1.3
Page 2 of 13
4.    The assumptions relating to Table 3 (development) are given below:
(i)    The present FRB network is 8,403 km. An additional 5,500 km of
FRB will be created from existing Rural Roads I (RR1) roads, due to
recent increase of classified growth center markets from 1,400 to
2,100. However, the size of the RR1 and Rural Roads 2 (RR2)
networks will remain unchanged because one can expect some increase
in the total length of roads, with upgrading from Rural Roads 3 (RR3)
roads.
(ii)   Ambitious Scenario:
*     All existing and additional FRBs upgraded to full bitumen-
surfaced standard, including bridging all gaps, replacing all
damaged structures, and repairing all "poor" structures.
*     20% of RR1 roads upgraded to WBM standard or equivalent
(for simplicity, it is assumed that they are all upgraded from
earthen standard).
*     All gaps on RR1 and RR2 roads bridged, all damaged
structures on RR1 and RR2 roads replaced, and all "poor"
structures on RR1 and RR2 roads repaired.
*     All unimproved Growth Center markets improved.
*     All jetties serving Growth Centers improved.
This equates to the following scope of work:
Upgrading of FRB to BC standard:        13,178 km
Construction of FRB structures:        45,897 m
Upgrading of RR1 to WBM standard
(or equivalent standard):              6,535 km
Construction of RR structures
(excluding repairs):                   232,274 m
Construction of improved markets:       1,890 markets
Construction of improved jetties:      700 jetties
The total investment cost is Taka 89,662 million (US$2,242 million).



83                        Annex 1.3
Page 3 of 13
(iii)   Realistic Scenario:
*     80% of existing and additional FRBs upgraded to full bitumen-
surfaced standard, including bridging all gaps, replacing all
damaged structures, and repairing all "poor" structures.
*     10% of RR1 roads upgraded to WBM standard or equivalent
(for simplicity, they are all upgraded from earthen standard),
including all structures works.
*     60% of gaps on remaining RR1 and RR2 roads bridged, 60%
of damaged structures on remaining RR1 and RR2 roads
replaced, and 60% of "poor" structures on remaining RR1 and
RR2 roads repaired.
*     50% of unimproved Growth Center markets improved.
*     All jetties serving Growth Centers improved.
This equates to the following scope of work:
Upgrading of FRB to BC standard:        10,542 km
Construction of FRB structures:         36,718 m
Upgrading of RR1 to WBM standard
(or equivalent standard):              3,268 km
Construction of RR structures
(excluding repairs):                   143,300 m
Construction of improved markets:       945 markets
Construction of improved jetties:      700 jetties
The total investment cost is Taka 62,732 million (US$1,568 million).
5.    Table 3 shows that to achieve targets under the realistic scenario, which will
substantially improve the condition of rural roads and markets to good
standards, a total amount of about 63 billion Taka (in 1996 prices) would be
needed; with an average annual development expenditure of about 6.3 billion
(about US$155 million equivalent).
6.    Projected Maintenance Expenditures:  Table 4 shows estimated increase in
maintenance requirements during 1996-2006, for both scenarios. For the
'realistic scenario'. it shows that average annual maintenance for this period
would increase from 1.3 billion Taka in 1996 to about 2.9 billion Taka in
2006, which implies: annual increase of 0.16 billion Taka for maintenance, and
a cumulative annual average maintenance over the first 5-years of 1.78 billion
Taka, and a cumulative average maintenance over 10 years of 2.1 billion Taka.



84
Annex 1.3
Page 4 of 13
(c)    Estimate of currently Available resources for rural infrastructure
7.     The main sources of funds at present (for development and maintenance) are
discussed below. They total an average (based on the last two years) of Taka
6.3 billion. About 54% of this is from foreign sources, and 46% domestic
resources (see para. 9 below). It is expected that because of the good design of
these projects focused on key rural infrastructure and their poverty-orientation,
efficient management, and the continuing high poverty level of the country,
this level of foreign assistance would continue in future. The present annual
development spending is about 5.4% of the country's annual development
budget (foreign and domestic).
(i)    Block Grant for Union and Thana Development
The nationwide block grant from Government's budget for FY94/95
was Taka 1,500 million, and for FY95/96 Taka 2,000 million. The
conditions applied to this Block Grant state that 25%-60% should be
allocated to the "transport and communications sector - roads, bridges,
culverts, etc". Thus, the maximum funds available from this resource
for rural transport infrastructure works are:
FY94/95:      Taka 900 million
FY95/96:      Taka 1,200 million
However, different Unions and Thanas will have different priorities for
use of the Block Grant.  Therefore, it is considered reasonable to
estimate the resources available for rural transport infrastructure on the
basis of the median figure, i.e., 42.5% of the Block Grant. This gives
resources of:
FY94/95:      Taka 637.5 million
FY95/96:      Taka 850 million
Note: Some infrastructure projects require local contributions from the
Union Parishads, and the main source of this local financing is the
Block Grant. Care has been taken to avoid double-counting in the
estimates provided in this note).
(ii)    LGED Revenue Budget for Maintenance
Governments' regular revenue budget allocation for rural road
maintenance (all spent through LGED) maintenance is as follows:
FY94/95:      Taka 550 million
FY95/96:      Taka 650 million
100% of this allocation is utilized for maintenance works.



85
Annex 1.3
Page 5 of 13
(iii)   Donor and Government Financed Rural Infrastructure Projects (RDPs
and others)
The Donor and Government Financed Rural Infrastructure Projects,
excluding those using international food-aid, are listed in Table 1. The
percentage of the total budget allocation applied to physical
infrastructure works has been estimated in Table 1 on the following
assumptions:
i.     RDPs, and similar donor-supported projects which emphasize
roads or roads and markets, 70%;
ii.    donor-supported projects in other sectors, but which include
roads, 30%; and
iii.    GOB-financed projects, 90%.
These estimates are intended to exclude any local contributions from
the Block Grant, in order to avoid double-counting. Based on this
analysis the Project Funds for rural infrastructure works, excluding
food-aid, are:
FY94/95:       Taka 3,328 million
FY95/96:       Taka 3,835 million
(iv)   International Food-Aided Infrastructure Projects
There are five international food-aided rural transport infrastructure
projects in LGED  at present, which are listed in Table 2.  This
includes the CIDA funded Road Maintenance Project (RMP) which
focuses on rural roads maintenance through employment of destitute
women in the villages, and it is important part of the rural road
maintenance program.
(v)    Summary of Available Resources (development and maintenance)
The estimated resources available for rural infrastructure (development
and maintenance) are summarized below:
Item                        1994/95          1995/96
1. Block Grant for Union and Thana Development  637              850
2. Revenue Budget for Maintenance               550              650
3. Food-Aid                                     1,417           1,420
4. Project Funds                                3,328           3,835
Total                        5,932            6,755
The average annual figure over the two financial years is Taka 6,343 million.

8.     Road Maintenance Resources
Based on the above analysis and using the 1995/96 data, the funds available for
road maintenance can be estimated as follows. (These exclude maintenance of
markets, which are generally fully covered, by markets own lease revenue).
Block Grant:                  Taka 42.5 million
LGED Revenue Budget:          Taka 650 million
Food Aid (RMP):               Taka 455 million
UP Contribution to RMP:       Taka 45.5 million
Project Funds:                Taka 120 million
Total:                        Taka 1,313 million
This estimate is derived as follows:
(i)    5 % of Block Grant for transport infrastructure is assumed to be
used for maintenance in addition to UP contribution to RMP;
(ii)    100% of LGED revenue budget for maintenance;
(iii)    Food-aid is the RMP expenditure funded through CIDA;
(iv)    UPs make a 10% matching contribution to the RMP from their
own revenue;
(v)    Project funds. - it is estimated that, out of the 1995/96 ADP
allocation to infrastructure development projects (including
donor financed projects) of about $150 million, about 2% is
spent on maintenance.
These amounts totaling 1.3 billion Taka are adequate to meet
present maintenance needs, but has to go up significantly to
meet future needs (Para. 6). Of the current total maintenance
expenditure, 575 million Taka (about 44%) is from foreign aid.
(d)    Share of foreignldomestic resources

11.    With reference to these figures, the following observations can be made:
(i)    The 43 % domestic share for development for rural infrastructure is
higher than the domestic share in total development budget. The share
of domestic contribution with Annual Development Program (ADP) for
1995-96 was 37%.
(ii)    Domestic share for maintenance is even higher (56%). Overall
domestic funding for the sector is about 3 times the total maintenance
expenditure required (about 1315 million Taka, see para. 6). The
share of foreign expenditure in maintenance is largely from RMP
which is a well-designed poverty-targeted, food-aided program which
may continue under RRMIMP-2 project, it is being agreed with
Government that they would provide alternative funds to sustain
maintenance, in case there is reduction in RMP allocation in future (see
also para. 13).
(iii)   The domestic funds for development in 1995-96 (pre-election budget)
was about 500 million Taka more than in the previous year budget.
(iv)   Annual development allocation for rural roads/markets (5.4 billion
Taka) in 1995/96 is about 5.4% of the country's total development
budget (covering foreign and domestic resources).
12.    Annual Future  Needs (development and  maintenance) under Realistic
Scenario: Annual future resource requirements (using 5-year maintenance
average) would be 8.1 billion (see paras. 5 and 6); as against this, the current
level of resource availability is about 6.8 billion (54% foreign, 46% domestic;
see para. 9); this indicates the need for about 19% increase in annual
resources to meet future needs if Bangladesh is to meet the realistic 10-year
targets proposed in the Strategy Paper. The implementation capacity exists for
this level. The increase in financial resources needed would seem justified in
view of the proven major economic and poverty alleviation impacts of such
investments.



88                         Annex 1.3
Page 8 of 13
(e)    Estimate of minimum Government budget allocation needed for future maintenance
(during the project period)
13.   As noted in para. 5, as the road network is improved, the future maintenance
requirements will increase from about 1,300 million Taka in 1996 to about
2,900 million Taka in 2006. The current maintenance needs are fully met from
various sources, including 650 million Taka from Government's budget for
rural infrastructure and maintenance allocated to LGED (para. 8). The
minimum level of Government budget allocation needed during the project
period, discussed in SAR para. 2.17, is estimated on an approximate basis as
follows:
Annual increase needed during the 10-year period to reach "realistic scenario"
targets: (2,900 million - 1,300 million = 1,600/10 = 160 million Taka).
Assuming only 80% development targets would be met (because of resource
constraint), the increase in maintenance level will be 128 million Taka. Of
this, 25% increase is expected from UP's resources and the rest about 96
million Taka from increase in budget allocation. Adjusting for inflation, an
annual about 100 million Taka (about US$2.5 million) is assumed as minimum
increase needed from the 1995-96 level of 650 million Taka (about US$16
million): 750 million Taka in 1996-97; this implies 850 million Taka (US$21
million equivalent) in 1997-98, and thereafter at an annual increment of
US$2.5 million equivalent to the last project year (2001-2002).

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
IMPACT OF RURAL INFRASTRUCTURE DEVELOPMENT
1.    The IFPRI Study
A major study on the impact of rural infrastructure development in Bangladesh, carried out by the
International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI) with the Bangladesh Institute of Development
Studies (BIDS) was published in 19901. This study surveyed 129 villages which were classified as
"developed" and "undeveloped" based on an infrastructure index which considered access to transport,
markets, schools, banks, modem inputs, electricity, and local administrative offices. From this sample
16 villages, seven "developed" and nine "undeveloped", were selected and detailed household surveys
conducted. The study found that, comparing the two categories, the villages with "developed"
infrastructure were significantly better off in a number of areas:
*     Farmgate prices of rice were 7 % higher.
--    Fertilizer prices were 14% lower and fertilizer utilization was 92% higher.
105% more land was irrigated and 71% more sown with HYV seeds.
Household income was 8% higher.
*;    The wage rate was 12% higher.
There were particular benefits for the poor - wage income of landless laborers was 36%
higher, and the proportion of female workers was 135% higher.
2.    Impact of FRB Road Improvements
There is increasing emphasis on socio-economic monitoring and evaluation (SEM&E) activities in the
Rural Development Projects implemented by LGED. Through the SEM&E activities of different
projects, a data-base of time-series information on the effect of FRB improvements on traffic (traffic
level; modal mix; and frequency, reliability and cost of transport services) is progressively being
accumulated. It is these changes in traffic, resulting from the road improvements, that generate the
longer-term economic and social impacts of the investments.
The evidence from the SEM&E work, including that carried out under the RRMIMP, is that FRB road
improvements to all-weather standard result in a growth of motorized and non-motorized vehicular
traffic and some transfer of pedestrian trips to more efficient motorized and non-motorized modes, a
reduction in transport charges and an increase in the total movement of goods and people. Provision of
an all-weather road tends to stimulate the operation of bus services (except on short roads) and auto-
rickshaw (tempo) services. Use of rickshaw services also tends to increase, but rickshaw traffic grows
at a slower rate than motorized services. However the use of bullock carts on the FRBs tends to
decline.
R. Ahmed and M. Hossain. Developmental Impact of Rural Infrastructure, Bangladesh.
International Food Policy Research Institute (IFPRI) in collaboration with Bangladesh Institute of
Development Studies (BIDS), 1990



95
Annex 1.4
Page 2 of 5
The FRB road investments under the ongoing RRMIMP project have already had a demonstrable socio-
economic impact, as a result of increased traffic volumes, a switch to more efficient modes of
transport, and reduced transport costs. Based on the SEM&E survey of nine completed project roads in
the first year of project completion (1995):
*     Total traffic volumes (passenger-km and tonne-km) on the nine roads increased by 120% for
passengers and 300% for goods. This data set includes one road where there was a huge
increase in goods traffic volume. However, comparing the median values passenger traffic
volume increased by 100% and goods traffic volume by 140%.
*     Average daily motorized traffic on the nine roads increased by 154%. The major increases
were in buses (from zero to 14 vehicles per day), motor cycles (178% increase) and auto
rickshaws (100%). Non-motorized traffic increased by 90%, the major increase being in
passenger rickshaws and rickshaw vans (197%). Bullock cart traffic decreased by 54%.
*     Average unit transport costs decreased by 69% for passengers and 48% for cargo. The benefits
to rickshaw operation of a smooth running surface are shown by the fact that the transport
charges of rickshaw van operators reduced by nearly 40%.
The same study also showed that along improved roads compared to non-improved roads, net farm
income is about 9% higher, cropping intensity 1-2% higher, and fertilizer and pesticide use
respectively 4-5% and 34% higher. The overall economic return from  the road improvement
investments is about 26% based on partial benefits of transport cost savings.
The report of findings from the monitoring of the first phase of RDP-14, the GTZ-financed Tangail
Infrastructure Development Project, was published at the end of 19942. Its findings on the impact of
improving FRB roads to bitumen-surfaced standard are as follows:
*     Within one year of FRB improvement, transport charges decreased significantly. Average
passenger charges dropped by 18.7%, and average cargo charges by 23.3%. The most
significant decreases in charges were for passenger rickshaws and rickshaw vans.
*     On two FRB roads where improvements had been initiated at the start of the monitoring study
and were essentially complete by the end of the study, the total number of road users (based on
average daily traffic) increased by 43% over a period of about one year, the number of non-
motorized vehicle trips increased by 85% and the number of motor vehicle trips by 135 %. The
number of pedestrian trips declined. Regular bus services had commnenced on one of the roads.
These traffic growth rates can be compared with an average of 12.7% normal annual growth of
non-motorized traffic over a ten-year period, and 7.0% for motor vehicles, derived from a
control road where traffic counts were taken in 1984 and 1994.
Performance monitoring of RDP-6 roads commenced with monsoon season traffic counts in 1993,
which are repeated twice per year. The latest findings3, derived from four rounds of traffic counting,
are that:
2          Final Report on Socio-Economic Monitoring and Evaluation Carried Out During Phase I of the
Tangail Infrastructure Development Project. November 1994.
3          Performance Monitoring of RDP-6, Preliminary Analysis of Rounds 1,2,3 and 4. June 1995



96
Annex 1.4
Page 4 of 5
The market improvements result in a reduction in crop spoilage of 2-4% of the value of the
crops marketed due to better protection from the elements and faster sales turnover. This is
sufficient to generate a 12 % EIRR from the investment in market improvements.
*     Between 1992 and 1994 there were increases in all eight markets in the number of sellers
attending (41%), the total turnover of the markets (48%) and the number of permanent shops
(18%). In four
of the eight markets the market turnover increased by substantially more than the number of
sellers. However in three of the markets the situation was reversed, with increased competition
among sellers to market their goods.
*     Between 1992 and 1994 the auction lease value increased in seven of the eight markets, by an
average of 35%. Toll collection increased by an average of 70%.
The findings from the monitoring of the first phase of RDP-14 in respect of the effects of improving
facilities at Growth Center markets are as follows:
For the three markets improved, auction lease value has increased by nearly 100% compared
with 28% for Growth Center markets in the District as a whole.
Land prices in the vicinity of the markets have increased by more than 180%.
The average daily number of market visitors has increased by 22%.
The total cargo brought for sale has increased by 24%, with an increase in estimated weekly
market turnover of 71 %.
The monitoring of the RDP-4 project examined the changes that resulted from improvement of Growth
Center markets. The main conclusions are that:
The number of permanent shops doubled between the benchmark and final surveys, the total
number of shops increased, and they were open for more days per week, indicating that the
Growth Centers are developing as commercial trading centers.
The number of visitors to Growth Center markets increased by 31 % on market days and 23%
on non-market days.
It should of course be noted that the significant increases in the level of economic activity in improved
markets result from the combination of the improvements to the market and the better road access, ie.
from the integrated development of the rural transport and trading network.
4.    Employment Generation
An important impact of rural infrastructure development projects is on direct and indirect employment
and income generation. It is estimated that, up to and including the 1994/95 Financial Year, 15 rural
infrastructure projects implemented by LGED have created some 70 million person-days of direct paid
employment on implementation of project activities. This does not include the direct employment
created on the various food-for-work programs implemented by LGED.
Longer-term employment and income-generation opportunities are created from the stimulus that the
improvement of infrastructure gives to agricultural production and to other economic activities:



97
Annex 1.4
Page 4 of 5
*     The market improvements result in a reduction in crop spoilage of 2-4% of the value of the
crops marketed due to better protection front the elements and faster sales turnover. This is
sufficient to generate a 12% EIRR from the investment in market improvements.
*     Between 1992 and 1994 there were increases in all eight markets in the number of sellers
attending (41 %), the total turnover of the markets (48%) and the number of permanent shops
(18%). In four
of the eight markets the market turnover increased by substantially more than the number of
sellers. However in three of the markets the situation was reversed, with increased competition
among sellers to market their goods.
*     Between 1992 and 1994 the auction lease value increased in seven of the eight markets, by an
average of 35%. Toll collection increased by an average of 70%.
The findings from the monitoring of the first phase of RDP-14 in respect of the effects of improving
facilities at Growth Center markets are as follows:
*     For the three markets improved, auction lease value has increased by nearly 100% compared
with 28 % for Growth Center markets in the District as a whole.
*     Land prices in the vicinity of the markets have increased by more than 180%.
*     The average daily number of market visitors has increased by 22 %.
*     The total cargo brought for sale has increased by 24%, with an increase in estimated weekly
market turnover of 71 %.
The monitoring of the RDP-4 project examined the changes that resulted from improvement of Growth
Center markets. The main conclusions are that:
*     The number of permanent shops doubled between the benchmark and final surveys, the total
number of shops increased, and they were open for more days per week, indicating that the
Growth Centers are developing as commercial trading centers.
*     The number of visitors to Growth Center markets increased by 31 % on market days and 23 %
on non-market days.
It should of course be noted that the significant increases in the level of economic activity in improved
markets result from the combination of the improvements to the market and the better road access, ie.
from the integrated development of the rural transport and trading network.
4.    Employment Generation
An important impact of rural infrastructure development projects is on direct and indirect employment
and income generation. It is estimated that, up to and including the 1994/95 Financial Year, 15 rural
infrastructure projects implemented by LGED have created some 70 million person-days of direct paid
employment on implementation of project activities. This does not include the direct employment
created on the various food-for-work programs implemented by LGED.
Longer-term employment and income-generation opportunities are created from the stimulus that the
improvement of infrastructure gives to agricultural production and to other economic activities:



98
Annex 1.4
Page 5 of 5
*      The RDP-14 monitoring study concludes that more than 2,700 permanent jobs have been
created indirectly as a result of the project infrastructure investments, most of them in the
expansion of transport operations but also in newly-established shops and workshops along the
improved FRB roads and near the improved markets.
The RDP-4 monitoring study estimated that, in addition to short-term direct employment on
project activities, long-term employment in the project area had increased by about 14% (the
majority in the non-agricultural sector) over the three-year period of the survey from 1989
to 1992. While the infrastructure investment had contributed to this, it was difficult to isolate
its impact from that of other initiatives in the area.
5.     Macro-Economic Analysis
As part of the IDA/LGED Rural Infrastructure Strategy Study7, macro-level analysis of the rural road
network has been carried out. There is a close association between the spatial distribution of good rural
roads across the country and the distribution of agricultural production densities, showing that
Govermment's past policies under the Strategy for Rural Development Projects, and the resulting
regional investments, have led to a generally rational distribution of resources.
An analysis of the relationship between agricultural productivity and the condition of the rural road
network has been carried out, using a nationwide District-level regression model. The key findings are
as follows:
*      With statistical significance at the 5 % and 1 % confidence levels respectively, the densities of
good Feeder roads and paved Rural Roads have positive productivity elasticities. The analysis
suggests that an increase of 10% in the density of good Feeder roads is associated with an
increase of almost 1 % in agricultural productivity. The same increase in the density of paved
Rural Roads is associated with a 0.5 % increase in agricultural productivity.
*      Analysis using the density of all rural roads does not yield significant results, indicating that it
is the condition, not the extent of the network that is important.
*      There is a negative relationship between agricultural productivity and gap density (measured as
the number of gaps per km of FRB and Rural Road).
7           SAI Infrastructure Division of the World Bank in association with LGED. Bangladesh Rural
Infrastructure Strategy Study. December, 1995.

Annex 2.1

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS I1PROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
SELECTION CRITERIA, IMPLEMENTATION PROCEDURES AND
TIME FRAME FOR PHYSICAL COMPONENTS
A.    Procedures Used in the Selection of FRBs, Growth Centre Markets and Ghats
1.    The FRB, market and ghat investment schemes have been selected (as listed in Annex 2.2)
using the 6-step procedure which is summarised below:
1.1    An inventory was prepared of all FRB roads and Growth Centre markets in the project
area. The inventory of Growth Centres included those designated in the early 1980s
and the additional markets designated as Growth Centres in 1993. In preparing the
inventory a strict definition of FRBs eligible for consideration under the project was
applied, based on two criteria:
(a) All FRBs should connect to a Growth Centre located remote from the RHD road
network;
(b) No Growth Centre should have more than one FRB road connection.
1.2    The inventory of FRB roads and Growth Centres was then screened to prepare ong
lists of 'possible' investment schemes excluding all FRBs and markets included in the
RRMIMP and RDP-6 projects', all other FRBs improved to good, all-weather bitumen-
surfaced standard under other programs, and all Growth Centre markets under the
Pourashava Parishad or at serious risk from erosion.
1.3    Using the long-list of possible schemes as the starting point, participatory meetings
were organised in each of the 97 Thanas in the project area, attended by local
representatives including Union Parishad members, NGOs and beneficiaries. At these
meetings the local representatives were requested to discuss, reach a consensus on, and
rank, their priorities for FRB and Growth Centre markets. They were also asked to
identify priority ghats for improvement in the Thana. The meetings were facilitated by
the preparation consultants' staff, but the consensus and decisions were reached by
local representatives through open debate. During this field work consultations were
also held with TNOs, LGED staff and some Members of Parliament.
1.4    The locally-defined priorities were used to prepare short-lists of 'probable' FRB
upgrading and Growth Centre market improvement schemes. The short-lists were
based on indicative targets for the extent and distribution of the physical investment
schemes across the project area, aimed at achieving approximately equivalent coverage
at project-end in relation to the infrastructure needs of the Northwest Region and the
Three FRB roads which RDP-6 will upgrade only to HBB standard were included for possible bitumen-
surfacing.



100                               Annex 2.1
Page 2 of 8
Greater Dhaka Area. In preparing the short-lists the greatest weight was given to the
locally-defined priorities determined during the participatory meetings. However some
adjustments were made to reflect rational selection criteria, as follows:
(a)    Most FRBs less than 2km long were excluded;
(b)    A few FRBs were added where particular requests had been made and where
initial field investigations indicated that the roads were important;
(c)    Growth Centre markets located very close to existing, improved markets were
excluded;
(d)    Some priority was given to complementing existing RRMIMP investments by
short-listing unimproved Growth Centre markets located on FRBs improved
under the RRMIMP; and
(e)    Priority was given to short-listing both FRBs and the markets that they serve,
but in some cases FRBs were short-listed without the accompanying market.
1.5   a)     All short-listed FRBs were subjected to detailed economic appraisal, based on
initial inventory surveys to prepare estimates of improvement costs, and on
traffic surveys to provide the data for estimation of economic benefits. The
final list of selected FRBs was made based on the results of the economic
analysis, rejecting all roads with an EIRR of less than 12% and selecting those
with the highest EIRRs.
b)     Economic and physical data was collected from the field for all short-listed
Growth Centre markets and for all ghats identified locally as priorities. The
final list of selected Growth Centre markets was derived from the short-list,
primarily based on the locally-identified priorities but:
(i)    Eliminating any markets located on FRB which do not provide, and
which will not be upgraded under the project to provide, all-weather access;
(ii)    Choosing between alternatives where adjacent Thanas had prioritised
markets located very close together - in such cases the more important market
was chosen based on the economic data collected; and
(iii) Eliminating certain markets where the field data raised serious doubts
about viability of improvement, most commonly where the available land area
is very small, or where the market has very low lease value, attendance and
turnover.
c)    The final list of selected ghats was made, from the priorities identified by local
representatives, as follows:
(i)    Since most of the priority ghats serve markets, all ghats serving
Growth Centre markets selected for improvement under the project or



101                                Annex 2.1
Page 3 of 8
improved under RRMIMP were included except where the utilisation of the
ghat is very low; and
(ii)   The more important of the remaining ghats were selected, importance
being defined in terms of throughput of boats and use for river transport rather
than simply for cross-river ferry services.
1.6   The TDCCs were subsequently requested by LGED, through the TNOs, to confirm
their agreement with the selection of the investment schemes which was subsequently
confirmed after voting by each TDCC. Such formal approval is required under
Government's procedures.
B.    Criteria for Selection of New Components or Revision of List of Selected ComDonents
2.    FRBs. Growth Centre Markets and Ghats
2.1    General: It is recognised that the FRB roads, growth centre markets and ghats for
inclusion in the project have been pre-selected during the appraisal process, as listed in
Annex 2.2, and as discussed in Section A above. The following selection criteria
would be used in case new FRB roads, growth centre markets or ghats are to be added
to the project either as new items or as replacement of existing locations in the list. It
is expected that such changes in the list in Annex 2.2 would be made only during mid-
term review or later, unless otherwise agreed. The selection criteria are listed below:
2.2    General Procedure: FRBs, growth centre markets and ghats shall be: (i) selected and
prioritised through participatory meetings involving interested parties, such as main
user groups, NGOs, UP members and TDCC members, held in the Thana where the
proposed works will be carried out; and (ii) endorsed by the Thana Development Co-
ordination Committee (TDCC) as a priority project for improvement in the Thana.
2.3    UP's Financial Contribution:  UPs should contribute a minimum of 10% of the cost
of growth centre markets, other rural markets and river ghats. UP's contribution for
SRRs will range from 10-20%, as detailed in Section 3 below.
2.4    Criteria
(a) FRBs (selected for full development)
i. The FRB must be at least 2 km in length and be the most important road
connection between a Growth Centre Market and the Road and Highways
Department Road (RHD) network.
ii. The FRB must connect a Growth Centre Market, which is located remote from the
RHD network, to a RHD road, which is passable throughout the year by motorised
vehicles.



102                              Annex 2.1
Page 4 of 8
iii. The FRB must connect a Growth Centre to the RHD network in one direction,
unless a specific and acceptable justification is provided to connect the growth
centre in two directions.
iv. The FRB investments must provide a minimum 12% Economic Internal Rate of
Return (EIRR), based on benefits to existing diverted and generated traffic.
(b)    Growth Centre Market
L. The market, which must have an annual lease value of at least Taka 75,000, has to
be located in a Growth Centre so designated by the Planning Commission of the
Borrower and be at least 5 km from an improved Growth Centre Market.
ii. The market must not be subject to any serious risk of erosion by river action and
must have access to a road, which is passable throughout the year by motorised
vehicles, or to a confirmed road development under other projects to be
implemented within a year.
iii. The relevant authority must confirm that the land required for the improvement
works is publicly owned and is not the subject of any ownership dispute.
iv. The local government body must confirm its commitment to contribute 10% of the
cost of the market improvements.
v.  Market vendors and other user groups should be consulted in preparing designs,
and should be given active role in market management committees.
vi. The market management committee and appropriate Government agencies should
take measures to increase lease revenue following improvements, to allocate
sufficient funds for maintenance, and should agree to provide to LGED and IDA
follow-up data on market use, revenue and project benefits.
vii. Borrower will use participatory processes involving market vendors and other
users to improve structure and operation of existing market management
committees, and to improve market leasing system to enhance revenue.
(c)    Ghats
L. The ghat has to be located on a perennial waterway and be used as a landing place
for river transportation, not just for cross-river ferry services.
ii. The ghat must directly serve a Growth Centre Market or connect to a road network
at an important point for the transfer of goods between water and land
transportations.
iii. The relevant authority must confirm that the land required for the improvement
works is publicly owned and is not the subject of any ownership dispute.



103                                 Annex 2.1
Page 5 of 8
iv. The local government body must confirm its commitment to contribute 10% of the
cost of the market improvements.
v. Country boat owner/operator associations (such as BCBOA) and other user groups
should be consulted in preparing designs, and should be given active role in ghat
management committee.
vi. The ghat management committee and appropriate Government agencies should take
measures to increase lease revenue following improvement, to allocate sufficient
funds for maintenance, and should agree to provide to LGED and IDA follow-up
data on ghat use, revenue and project benefits.
vii. GOB will: (a) cause ghat management committee to be established for all
developed ghats modelled on market management committee;  (b) ensure that
operation of these ghats and markets will have adequate user participation.
3.    Selection of Structures on Other FRBs (i.e. FRBs not selected for full development)
Except as otherwise agreed with the Association, for purposes of Part A of the Project, the
selection of structures to be constructed on other FRBs shall be carried out in accordance with the
procedures and criteria set forth below.
3.1    General Procedure:
LGED, with the assistance of the preparation consultants, and in consultation with
user groups and TDCC, will select the structures to be included in this sub-component,
and prepare the annual construction program, for approval by the IDA prior to project-
start. Each scheme will comprise construction of one or more structures on a particular
FRB road. The annual construction program will be drawn from the selected schemes
but, in the same manner as for other components where schemes have been pre-
selected, some modifications to the pre-selected schemes could be made under mutual
agreement between LGED and IDA.
3.2    Criteria:
(a)    Each proposed structure must either bridge an existing gap or replace a poor or
damaged structure on an FRB road, all as defined in the LGED road inventory.
It could also be new structure for improving cross-drainage between farming
areas.
(b)    The FRB  road on which the proposed structure or structures will be
constructed must (i) serve a Growth Centre Market located remote from the
FRB road network and (ii) be the most important road serving the Growth
Centre Market.
(c)    The construction of the proposed structure or structures must result in a
continuous FRB alignment between a Growth Centre Market and the Thana
HQ/RHD network which, at the minimum, is passable by motor vehicles in the
dry season and by non-motorised transport throughout the year.



104                                 Annex 2.1
Page 6 of 8
(d)    Each proposed structure that exceeds 30 linear metres in span shall be subject
to an economic appraisal and a minimum 12% EIRR.
4.    Selection of Structures on Rural Roads (SRR)
4.1    General:      The SRR Component will comprise the construction of small structures
to span existing gaps, replace damaged structures or provide additional drainage
structures to allow new cross-drainage where required on RR1 and RR2 roads ( also
R3 roads if
local communities prefer them), together with ancillary earthworks on the roads. Each
scheme will comprise of a length of RR1/RR2/RR3 road plus the structure(s) to be
constructed on it. All schemes will be selected through participation of the local
government bodies, rural communities and other user groups - using the 'Information
Sharing' and 'Users Input' approaches (see annex on community participation), and all
formal proposals for investment will be submitted to the project by the Union
Parishads. The proposed scheme should meet the following criteria:
4.2    General Criteria:
a)    The proposed scheme must increase the connectivity of the Rural Road network, i.e.
the implementation of the scheme should result in a continuous alignment of road,
passable by wheeled vehicles, and serving a rural area and connecting it either to an
important local place or to a higher level of the road network which is also a
continuous alignment passable by wheeled vehicles. Alternatively, it could be for
improving cross-drainage where an existing road embankment blocks such drainage.
b)    Schemes will be limited to rural drainage structures (culverts and small bridges not
exceeding 20 m length) on rural roads, ancillary earth works on rural roads (as
approaches to the structures or elsewhere on the rural roads) will be included for an
amount of 15% of the cost of the drainage structures.
c)    The proposed scheme must not be scheduled to be taken up under any other program.
d)    This will be a national program.  All Union Parishads (UPs) will be allowed to
participate on a first come-first-served basis, according to the date of registration. The
participating UP has to deposit an initial Tk.10,000 into LGED SRR account for
registration. LGED will undertake to provide the completed structure within one year
of registration. This guarantee will apply only to the first 1500 structures registered for
each year, because of possible capacity limitations, and further there may also be a
limit on the total number of structures per UP.
e)     After registration, LGED  will inspect the selected site and make drawings and
estimates and discuss with the UP choices of types and cost estimates. After selection
by the UP, LGED Thana Engineer will invite bids from small contractors and eligible
labour contracting societies (LCS)/Labour Groups, and select lowest evaluated bidder
following usual procedures acceptable to IDA.  Earthworks outside the structure



105                                 Annex 2.1
Page 7 of 8
approaches will be awarded to LCSs/labour groups of poor women/landless labourers
based on negotiated contracts and unit rates under procedures acceptable to IDA.
f)    Before award of contract, the UP must deposit the remaining contribution (above the
initial deposit) to LGED's SRR account. UP's total contribution will be 10% of total
costs (structure plus 15 % added as ancillary earth works for road improvements/spot
improvements) during the first three years of the project (tentatively from July 1996 to
June 1999) and 20% for the next (last) two years of the project. UP's who deposit
funds for registration before December 31, 1998 (allowing some lead time for
construction period), will be eligible for the 10% rate, and those who register after this
date will have to pay at 20% of costs. Government will contribute 10% when UP
contribution is 10%, so that total domestic contribution is 20%.
g)     The site will be selected by UPs through a participatory process involving two stages:
(i) selection by UP residents through a public meeting for discussing alternatives using
user-inputs techniques (such as, map-making etc.), most likely with assistance of
LGED's community organisers (which assistance may not be available in all cases). (ii)
Voting by UP council on the proposed sites.
h)    The UP should undertake to provide for regular maintenance of the structures and
earthworks.
i)     UP's should agree to provide to LGED data regarding extent of the funding done
through new resource mobilisation, data on employment of the poor, length of road
made passable, and also some data on traffic growth (All these through a proforma to
be filled on completion of works and a year after completion).
j)     UP should contribute its share partly in advance as described earlier. It can raise new
resources (preferred method) or use UP's ADP allocation or other revenues.
5.    Selection of Other Components: (such as small rural markets) would be according to selection
criteria to be agreed between LGED and IDA.
C.    Monitoring of Land Acquisition
6.1    LGD will set up a committee headed by a Deputy Secretary (who will directly report to
the LGD Secretary) with representatives from LGED, which will: (i) ensure that the Ministry's
administrative approval is given within seven days of request by LGED; (ii) liaise with respective
district commissioners to speed up the process of land acquisition and compensation. LGED will report
to IDA monthly on progress of these actions.
D.    Participatorv Processes
7.1    The project components, to be eligible, should use participatory approach in selecting
components; and as far as feasible in design and implementation of the project. Main
project components (roads, river jetties, markets), have already been selected using
active involvement of beneficiaries; changes in these lists will be done through
participatory processes defined earlier in this annex. (see also Annex 2.3 in SAR which



106                                Annex 2.1
Page 8 of 8
describes participation processes in more details). Rural structures will be selected
using specially designed participatory processes and "user input" techniques. In the
case of growth center markets, the design of facilities will be done in full day meetings
with vendors and others who would be the main users of these market facilities; they
will also be given a key role in managing the markets (regarding level of user charges,
auctioning of market lease, maintenance arrangements and funding), through market
committees of users, reporting every six months to an "assembly" of market users,
with special emphasis on information sharing. These procedures would be reviewed
during project implementation with IDA, and changes if necessary will be agreed. For
road components, user committees will be set up to give feed back on design and
quality of on-going works.
7.2    To ensure the participatory process to be more transparent and well implemented, the
project would finance 17 positions of local specialists in community participation
(sociologists or equivalent) and one consultant with international and local experience;
of these one community specialist will be placed in each project district, and three
community specialists (supervisors) will be assigned to advise and assist the Project
Director and LGED headquarters. In addition, suitable LGED personnel at Thana level
will be trained as "community organizers". These positions will be filled by March 31,
1997.  Services of NGO's would also be used, as needed, in promoting the
participation process, particularly in market/ghat planning.
7.3    The participation specialists will also assist poor workers and destitute women to
organize labor contracting societies (to work as contractors in labor based road works
and road maintenance) in two pilot districts, with the help of NGOs. However,
selection/formation of LCS will have to be recognized by UPs.
7.4   In addition, LGED, during project implementation, will pilot, with the help of NGO's
as needed, new methods for community-level participation in road maintenance in at
least two pilot districts, and provide a report on its findings. This pilot program will be
started, in consultation with IDA, before June 30, 1998, and a report on lessons
learned will be provided to IDA before December 31, 2000.
7.5    LGED will also set up by December 31, 1997, District level road user committees with
representatives from different user groups to promote information sharing and selection
of priorities and to facilitate feed back from users on quality of ongoing works.
7.6    To enhance participation of women, LGED would nominate one of its senior staff(the
sociologist in the LGED headquarter's office provided as incremental staff) to look
after gender issues. LGED has also agreed to recruit more women engineers as its staff
and to give special emphasis to training of women members of UPs under its
participation training programs under the project so as to strengthen their role in local
development matters. LGED/UPs will also help increase the number of women
members in the market and ghat user committees.
