114                                 Annex 2.3
Page 1 of 11
BANGLADESH
RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAIENANCE PROJECT
COMMUNITY PARTICIPATION IN THIE PROJECT
RURAL INFRASTRUCTURE DEVELOPMENT
1.    Introduction
Participation of major stakeholders and beneficiaries has been incorporated into the design of the
project from the start of preparation. The Feeder Roads, markets and ghats for improvement have
already been selected through a process of participation to identify local priorities (see Annex 2.1).
The remaining components will follow similar procedures. During the course of project preparation,
major opportunities have been identified to expand the scope of participation. SAR, paragraphs 3.1 to
3.5 sunmmarize the main participatory features of the projects.
It is a very fortunate situation for the project preparation that all parties are very positive about the
participatory approach. The Local Government Engineering Department (LGED) has become an
enthusiastic promoter of the participatory approach; this is very important because co-ordination by
LGED at the national level ensures that successful approaches will be replicated in the whole country.
Also the National Planning Commission has made participation a major emphasis of their new
development plan under preparation. This is clearly implied by the title of the document under
preparation: "Participatory Perspective Plan for Bangladesh 1995 2010".
As part of the preparation work a series of studies have been carried out on different aspects of
participation, the findings of which are presented in four working papers produced in early 19951. This
note, which supplements discussions in SAR (paragraphs 3.1 to 3.5), summarises the findings from the
work on participation, and the participatory measures that will be applied in the implementation of the
different components of the project.
2.    Context
*     In the context of LGED rural infrastructure development activities, there is an array of
stakeholders in participation:
LGED, and its staff at HQ, District and Thana level.
The rural communities such as rural social leaders, landless men and women, men and
women professionals, destitute men and women, etc.
Particular target and interest groups among the rural communities - e.g. poor women
and men, men and women market users, boat operators, lessees, etc.
'Community Participation in Rural Infrastructure Development' by Dr. Dee Jupp.
'Use of Labour Contracting Societies in Rural Infrastructures' by Mr. Mohammed Faizullah.
'Financing Local Level Infrastructures' by Mr. Mohammed Faizullah.
'Institutional Issues in Participation' by Dr. M. Rashiduzzaman.
(These are included in Project Files; see Annex 4.1)



115
Annex 2.3
Page 2 of 11
-      NGOs, youth clubs, women's organisations etc., who may have a role to play in
particular aspects of participation.
-      Local government bodies - Union Parishads (UP) including their women members,
Thana Development Co-ordination Committees (TDCC), Market Management
Committees (MMC) including women representatives, and Ghat Management
Committees (GMC).
-      Officials of Central Government working at local level - Deputy Commissioner (DC),
Thana Nirbahi Officer (TNO), District Women Affairs Officer etc.
-      Local Government Division (LGD) responsible for local government, and National
Institute of Local Government (NILG).
-      Project Beneficiaries: including users of rural roads, structures, markets, river jetties,
i.e. most of the rural population in the project areas.
*      There are four levels of Participation:
1.    Information Sharing
2.     Consultation
3.     Shared Control over Decision-Making
4.     Initiation of Action
Participation should be perceived as encompassing all aspects of rural infrastructure
development. However it must be practical and manageable, and will involve different
stakeholders, and operate at different levels, for different aspects:
-      Planning and Selection of Infrastructure Schemes
-      Detailed Design of Infrastructure Schemes
-      Implementation of Infrastructure Works
-      Monitoring of Infrastructure Works
-      Operation and Maintenance of Infrastructure
-      Financing of Infrastructure Improvement and Maintenance.
*      Many aspects of LGED's activities already involve participation, and it has embarked upon a
number of initiatives to develop more participatory approaches:
-      it is a rural infrastructure development agency with a genuine poverty focus in its
work, has a significant presence throughout the country at local-level (Thana and
District) and inter-acts with different stakeholders at local-level.
the concept of LGED  staff as 'social engineers' is emerging, training in socio-
economic issues is being developed, and LGED is taking steps to strengthen its 'social'
staffing capacity - most importantly by the conversion of selected staff in each Thana
office to the post of 'community organiser', and the provision of training to these
community organisers.
LGED is already involved in developing planning capacity at union and thana level,
e.g. through extensive training, the use of the Union and Thana Plan Books, and is
refining procedures to increase the level of participation. LGED's existing training
programs needs to be reviewed and tailored according to the needs of the project.



116
Annex 2.3
Page 3 of II
LGED already works with UPs and TDCCs in the implementation of different
programs, e.g. the Growth Centre Connecting Roads (GCCR) program, and the
Integrated Food for Development (IFFD) project which LGED implements with
CARE.
LGED pioneered the concept of Labour Contracting Societies in Bangladesh to exploit
the opportunities of infrastructure works to provide employment for the rural poor men
and women (see attachment 1)
LGED is now expanding this concept to employ disadvantaged, assetless women for
routine maintenance, tree caretaking on FRBs, and other infrastructure building
activities.
As part of the development of the FRB maintenance system, LGED is involving the
UPs in monitoring of maintenance works.
LGED is now addressing the needs of women at improved markets.
*     LGED's participatory approach has been further developed in the preparation of the RRMIMP-
2 project - a participatory approach has been adopted for the pre-selection of FRBs, Growth
Centre markets and ghats for improvement under the project, involving group discussions at
Thana level with representatives including women members of each Union (together with the
Thana Engineer and the TNO) to identify and rank local priorities for improvement.
*     Activities under the project should aim to build upon, reinforce and extend existing LGED
initiatives in participation. However there are some institutional issues that extend beyond
LGED and relate to the Local Government Division of the Ministry of Local Government,
Rural Development and Co-operatives (MLGRD&C).
3.    Community Participation in Infrastructure Development
*     The project area covers 14 Districts, 97 Thanas, 960 Unions and about 22 million people.
Initiatives under the project in respect of community participation must be both practical and
manageable within the scale of the project.
*     Field investigations have been carried out to assess the scope and identify the mechanisms for
community participation in selection of schemes (SRR), in detailed design (markets and ghats),
and in monitoring (SRR, markets and ghats). Specific attention was given to the participation
of women in these aspects.
*     The key findings from the field investigations are as follows:
Rural people including target and interest groups (the end-users) would like to become
more involved in the selection and design of rural infrastructure schemes - SRR,
Markets, Ghats. They have something valuable to offer in terms of their local
knowledge of needs and  priorities, and  in  recommending  detailed  design
improvements.



117
Annex 2.3
Page 4 of 11
Simple mapping exercises by road users (men & women) provide a good basis for
community discussion on priorities for SRRs, and for presenting proposals to officials.
Group discussions and ranking exercises provide a mechanism for users' requirements
and priorities to be incorporated into the design of market and ghat improvements.
End-users are ill-informed about proposed development initiatives, about decision-
mraking processes and about procedures for operation and management of rural
infrastructure (e.g. markets). End-users are also ill-informed about sources and
allocation of funds. More information will result in greater transparency and will allow
'monitoring from below'.
Women are increasingly using roads and markets, and should be involved in
community participation processes. Market improvement schemes offer an opportunity
to stimulate increased use by women. Women groups, and NGOs that promote
income-generating activities for women, should be involved in determiining the need
for, and the design of, any special market facilities for women and in monitoring of its
implementation.
Market and ghat users indicate that they would be prepared to pay increased tolls for
improved facilities as long as they are consulted about the improvements.
Community discussions indicate that people have some reservations about the role of
NGOs in participatory planning, particularly in acting on their behalf as 'advocates' to
higher authorities. NGOs may also be reluctant to take on this role.
However there are possibilities for NGOs to support LGED in developing participatory
planning capability. A participatory process demands people to develop their capacity
to question about the issues that relate to them. So, NGOs who have the objectives of
social mobilisation to ascertain people's rights need to be selected.
*     The findings from the field investigations indicate that initiatives under the project in respect of
community participation should focus on:
i)    providing more information to rural men and women on infrastructure development
and management in their locality;
ii)   involvement of communities (men and women) in selection of SRR;
iii)   involvement of men and women users in the design of market and ghat improvements;
iv)   involvement of men and women groups in result based monitoring. To enable them to
do the monitoring using selected indicators will require field testing and training since
they will need to understand and to be familiarised with the indicators in order to play
an effective role.
*     Based on these conclusions, the project will incorporate two approaches to community
participation:
1 .    Throughout the project area 'Information Sharing' and feed back mechanisms will be
incorporated into the project. The aim is to inform rural people about:
broad plans in place for the area under the project, how these have been made,
and the financial implications including contributions that will be made by local
bodies.
aspects of the project where people can make an input to selection and design
(SRR, markets, ghats), and the channels for making their inputs.



118                                 Annex 2.3
Page 5 of 11
-      decisions that are made about, reasons for such decisions, and arrangements for
implementation of, investment schemes in their locality.
-      procedures for the operation and maintenance of infrastructure, sources and
allocation of funds, and responsibilities of local bodies (particularly in respect
of Rural Roads, markets and ghats).
The UPs will be an important partner in the process of information sharing. They will
require training and support from the LGED Community Organisers (who will also
require training in information dissemination techniques) and/or from other non
government organisations. A range of appropriate methods will be used for
dissemination of different types of information including:
-      'banging' of drums - a traditional method for spreading news. This should also
be done in the residential areas so that women get the information.
-      distribution of flyers.
-      public meetings.
-      posting of notices in prominent places, e.g. presentation of plans for market
improvement, and listing of members of the market management committee, in
Growth Centre markets.
-      advertising (of the SRR schemes).
-      use of local institution such as schools, madrasha, NGOs and social leaders.
This information sharing approach will include feed back from the beneficiaries and
sharing of information on problems faced, lessons learnt, strengths and weaknesses of
the implemented activities, special provisions for women in different activities, and
facilitate 'monitoring from below'. The project will monitor the performance and
effectiveness of the information sharing approach and methodologies.
2.    In a more targeted manner, community participation will be taken a stage further by
adopting a 'Users Input' approach:
i)    for the detailed design of Markets and Ghats, the approach will be applied
extensively to the proposed improvement schemes, but with representative
samples of users. Group discussions and ranking exercises will be used to
define local priorities for specific improvements and to provide inputs on
detailed aspects of design. This Users Input approach will be integrated into
the standard LGED procedure for physical planning and design of market
improvements. Draft plans and designs for improvement will be posted in each
market/ghat for review prior to finalisation.
ii)    for the SRR component, an extensive application of this approach will be made
in selected 'pilot' Districts, and in sample communities in those Districts,
identification of priorities for SRRs through mapping exercises will be
facilitated; the communities (men and women) encouraged and assisted to
represent their priorities to local officials; and participation in monitoring will
be included. Because of the national activities of this component (i.e. also
outside the project area of all the other components) the extensive approach
cannot be applied everywhere, yet for all the projects some level of
participation of beneficiaries, at least consultation by UP will be done.

The implementation of this approach will require capacity for facilitation of community
participation. The main mechanism for this will be to develop the capability of the
LGED Community Organisers, and the project will provide them with training and
monitor their performance. However the project may also experiment in specific areas
with other mechanisms including the temporary employment of university graduates
living in the rural

Special attention will be paid to ensuring that the approach incorporated inputs by
women as users' of infrastructure, and to involving women in the design of market
facilities to encourage women to use the markets for income-generating activities and
monitoring of their implementation. .
The users input approach will also be applied to the pilot program to improve NMT
(see Annex 2.4).
The project will be responsible for ensuring quality control of the users input approach
and for monitoring its effectiveness..
4.    Participatory Processes in the Different Components of the Project
Participation is build in into the different components of the project. The main features of each
component are summarised below.
4.1.   Feeder Roads Type B (FRB) Component
The FRB roads to be improved under this component have been selected using a participatory
approach involving consultation with local bodies - Union Parishads, TDCCs and NGOs - to derive a
short-list of roads based on local priorities, which were then subjected to economic appraisal.
4.2.   Maintenance component: Road User Committees
There is a need for involving road users in setting priorities for the development and
maintenance of the network. As roads are improved bus and truck operations become more important
and the opinions of bus and truck operators do matter, this is also true for the business community in
general. Since the road network provides also access to social services, the opinions of officials and
users of health and educational infrastructure are also important. Therefore the project emphasizes the
creation of Road User Committees. These committees will approve maintenance schemes and may be
involved with the approval of future investment schemes, these committees will include all above
mentioned stakeholders. After examining different options LGED proposes to create these Road User
Conmnittees at Thana level.



120
Annex 2.3
Page 7 of 11
4.3.   Structures on Rural Roads (SRR) Component
It is in the SRR component that community participation plays a most important role. Under
the project a fund will be created to provide matching grants and the project will provide technical
services, but the decision to build a structure, where and when to build it and the local financial
contribution will be decided by the local people in collaboration with their elected local
representatives. The LGED Community Organisers will assist in this process using the "information
sharing" approach and in selected UPs using the "users input" approach.
The procedures of this component have been detailed during meetings with UP Chairmen and
the UP Chairmen association.
This component will include a national information campaign in order to create a demand from
the bottom up. Another important aspect is increasing UP resources by the transfer of central
government income to local government, matching grants will further increase resources and encourage
improved local revenue collection. Involving local NGOs, youth clubs and women's organisations will
increase local community participation and execution of part of the works by Labour Contracting
Societies (LCSs) will provide income to local poor men and women.
4.4.   Markets and Ghats Components
The design of markets and ghats will be based on participatory processes in order to take into
account users needs.
But the project will also formulates recommendations and implement changes in the
management of the markets and ghats. These changes aim at increasing participation by the users.
Proposed changes will aim at increasing the number of users as members of the existing but hardly
functioning Market Management Committees (MMC), and to provide further measures to improve the
functioning of these MMCs such as providing salaries to MMC members and the creation of an
assembly of all market users to supervise its activities periodically. Training and assistance will be
provided through the Community Organisers.
Market vendors will play a key role in managing the improved facilities through the users
committees; it is expected that procedures evolved in consultation with market users will set up
"assembly of market-users" for each market, to which the market committees will report every six
months. Similar procedures will be used for ghat management country boat owners/operators will play
key role in ghat management. There will also be more women-user members in these communities.
4.5.   Non Motorised Transport (NMT) Component
The Non Motorised Transport component aims at improving transport by rickshaws and
bullock carts. In order to work closely with the operators of these means of transport and to ensure
their interest and collaboration these activities will be executed by experienced local NGOs and other
stakeholders with only minimal supervisory involvement from LGED. Dissemination will be done in
close collaboration with the Bangladesh National Forum Group of the International Forum for Rural
Transport and Development, local NGOs and other community groups.



121
Annex 2.3
Page 8 of l1
4.6.   Human Resource Development (HIRD) Component
The HRD component will include the training required to improve the participatory aspects of
the project. First of all this component will include training of the Community Organisers (see also
above) but also of other LGED staff such as the Thana Engineers in order to make them aware of the
need for community participation, to guarantee their understanding of the approach and their full co-
operation, and finally give them the ability to manage a participatory process.
In the training of UP members and staff, understanding and implementing community
participation will be an important part of their training.
The trained LGED staff and specially the Community organisers will enable the beneficiaries
(including women) to assume and develop their capacity as stakeholders.
LGED training program under the project will give special emphasis to training of the women
members of UPs.
4.7.   Technical Assistance (TA) Component
In order to guarantee a successful implementation of the participatory approach the project will fund a
number of specialist positions to assist with the implementation of the approach. In order to provide
field training and supervise the LGED Community Organisers based in each Thana, the project will
provide a full-time Sociologist in each of the 14 districts covered by the project. These sociologists
will be assisted by two Senior Sociologists at the project headquarters. The project will also fund a
permanent position of a Sociologist (Participation and Land Acquisition) at the LGED headquarters in
the Planning Cell under the second Additional Chief Engineer. Finally the international DSM
consultants will provide 24 person-months of a Participation Specialist to advise on the development of
the methodologies and the implementation of the participatory approach, to develop the related training
programs, to advise on the monitoring of the effectiveness of the participation, and to define training
and human resource development needs to develop participatory capability in LGED.
5.    Institutional Issues
*     In addition to activities in the field, the project will contribute to the development of
participatory capability in LGED through:
-     organisation and conduct of seminars for senior staff, drawing on experience from the
project, from other agencies in Bangladesh and from other countries;
-     further development of the training course on socio-economic issues to incorporate
community participation, gender and development, case studies and practical sessions;
incorporation into the course of practical experience from the project, and extension of
training to all XENs and TEs, and to other engineering staff;
-     applying the experience from the project, particularly in respect of the 'Information
Sharing' and 'Users Input' approaches, to the refinement of the Union/Thana Plan
Book planning processes.
-     ensuring effective performance in participatory process by the engineers. Their
accountability would be linked to performance of the participatory aspects and their
performance evaluation would be done on the basis of these.

6.    Gender Issues
The project help enhance women's participation by employment of women for small works;
better facilities in markets created in consultation with women-users; an employment of more
women engineers; encouraging more women-user members in market committees; and training
of the women members of UPs in project participation processes.
7.    Role of NGOs
The project includes many innovative features and will promote beneficiary participation in
many different ways, including the use of NGOs, because NGOs are in a better position to
organise the participation of poor beneficiaries in the area of rural development. There are
many areas in which NGOs would be contracted to help implement project components: in
implementing the non-motorised transport (NMT) pilot component; in assisting training and
participation of country boat owners involved with the river jetties, and training of poor
workers in organising themselves into labour contracting societies for doing earthworks in two
project districts and in piloting greater community involvement in maintenance. In addition, in
the large nation-wide component of rural drainage structures (culverts) which will be
implemented with local community participation (in selection, funding and supervision), it is
expected that following national advertisement of this competitive component, many small,
local NGOs will come forward to assist in these areas. Informal NGOs of market vendors and
users would likewise be involved in helping the design, maintenance and management of rural
markets under the project.

Attachment 1
Income-Generation for the Poor
*     LGED pioneered the concept of LCS as a means of targeting employment on infrastructure
works at the rural poor men and women. The concept has now been taken up by a number of
LGED projects, and by other agencies, under several different names - as a general term,
'labour groups' may be used. There are substantial variations in the different systems used in
respect of:
'targeting': e.g. the LGED/CARE Road Maintenance Project employs only assetless
women while a Project Implementation Committee set up by a UP for a food for work
scheme may simply employ 'local labour';
tasks: labour groups are variously used for construction earthworks, pipe culvert
casting and installation, HBB works, tree planting and caretaking, and routine
maintenance;
contractual arrangements: LGED does not enlist LCS as contractors, whereas under the
Early Implementation Project (EIP) of the Bangladesh Water Development Board they
are enlisted as Class D contractors (i.e. the lowest level of contractor). However LCS
engaged in construction works normally receive some advance payment.
payment: LGED pays labour groups engaged in maintenance regularly but in arrears.
The groups under the EIP receive staged payments including an advance. Labour
groups engaged in culvert making and installation normally receive an advance for
materials.
*     The labour group concept has the advantage that it targets employment directly at the rural
poor and eliminates middle-men, and offers the potential for assisting beneficiaries
subsequently to move on to other income-generating activities. However the formation and
social/economic development of such groups is time-consuming and labour-intensive. Further,
LCS engaged in earthworks construction normally only have a life span of only one
construction season (since there is no continuity of construction earthworks in the locality
where members of the group live).
*     There is considerable scope for the project to provide employment opportunities for the rural
poor through its infrastructure works. The scope for use of contracting groups has been
assessed as follows:
i)    embankment earthworks on FRBs must be mechanically compacted, and it is difficult
to combine use of labour groups for the earthworks with contractors for compaction.
Further, such labour groups would only have a life of one construction season. Labour
groups will not be used for FRB embankment construction. However they will be used
for ancillary earthworks on the SRR component, in conjunction with the introduction
of simple compaction equipment. But LGED will not expend significant effort on the
formation and development of labour groups for this activity wherever labour groups
are available through NGOs;



124                                Annex 2.3
Page 11 of 11
ii)    labour for pipe culvert casting and installation offer greater prospects for sustainability
and longer-term income-generation. The project will utilise such groups for some pipe
culvert works under the SRR component. The groups will require training and skill
development, and substantial advance payment to procure materials (or supply of the
materials by LGED);
iii)   labour groups have great potential for routine maintenance of FRB roads (shoulders,
embankments, cleaning of structures) combined with tree planting and caretaking.
Under the project this work will be targeted at disadvantaged, assetless women,
building on the procedures already being developed in LGED. The long-term
continuity of this work allows the project to assist group members to be self reliant -
through forced savings and/or credit provided by NGOs - to other income-generating
activities.
*     Labour groups must be trained in the technical aspects of their work and in work management,
supervision and payment procedures - the training in technical aspects is particularly important
for PCCI and routine maintenance groups, and responsibility for such training, and for
supervision of the works, will lie with LGED and the project.
*     The social aspects of group formation, mobilisation, gender and development, and 'self
reliance' represent very time-consuming tasks which LGED is not well placed to undertake. It
is preferable for LGED to:
-     work with other organisations (Bangladesh Rural Development Board and NGOs) who
are already involved in group formation and in the provision of credit. Thus, for
ancillary earthworks, groups which have already been formed by other organisations
can be used. Yet depending on the socio-economic situation of the area, willingness to
work on infrastructure, availability of labour group through NGOs will vary. Option
will be kept open for the project to form its own labour groups, if necessary.
-     develop longer-term relationships with 'affiliate' NGOs to focus on group development
but it is not advisable to groom new NGOs..
However LGED will take the lead in defining the targets (e.g. assetless women) for particular
types of labour group.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMIENT
AND MAINTANCE PROJECT
PILOT PROGRAM TO IMPROVE NON-MOTORISED TRANSPORT (NMT)
1.    Background
Non-motorised transport (NMT) plays a very important role in the rural transport system in
Bangladesh, and foremost among the different types of NMT are the cycle rickshaws.
There are estimated to be at least 300,000 cycle rickshaws in rural areas, comprising passenger
rickshaws (which mainly carry people but also move goods) and rickshaw vans (which mainly move
goods but also carry people). Cycle rickshaws provide affordable, flexible, demand-responsive and
extensive transport services in rural areas. They complement motor vehicle services on improved roads
and provide the main means of transport for rural people and their goods on lower levels of the
network where, because of poor road conditions, motor vehicles operate only with difficulty or not at
all. Cycle rickshaws give rural people in Bangladesh who cannot afford to own any means of transport
of their own a level of mobility which is not possible in other rural societies that do not have such
vehicles. A recent study on the impact of non-motorized transport on rural household's activity
patterns showed that of 100 sample households none had any motorized vehicle, only 12 percent had a
bicycle and 4 percent owned boats. The study further revealed that less than 1 percent of the activities
to which the household had to have access could be accessed solely using motorized vehicles; the rest
had to be done using either NMT (13 percent) or walking (41 percent) or any combinations of,
predominantly non-motorized modes (31 percent). Accordingly, there exists an enormous potential
demand for NMT to assist in meeting household's basic socio-economic transport needs. The potential
for motor vehicles to meet rural household's day to day transport demand is negligible because they are
not affordable: 31 percent of the households ruled out use of any type of vehicle, even in the case of
emergency, simply because they are beyond their financial means. Of the 69 percent who can afford
vehicle use, only 20 percent can afford motorized vehicles, while the remaining 80 percent can afford
only NMT. It was found that the time saved by the introduction of NMT would be used mainly for
productive purposes (44 percent and 23 percent for resp. work and domestic tasks). NMT are well-
suited to many of the mobility demands of rural people - travel over relatively short distances and
movement of relatively small volumes of goods. The RRMIMP-2 project traffic counts carried out on
98 unimproved FRB roads in the project area give an indication of the level of use of cycle rickshaws -
there is an average of 360 cycle rickshaw vehicle trips per day on each of these roads. It is estimated
that at least 400,000 people in rural areas are employed in the production, operation and repair of cycle
rickshaws. Most of the rural cycle rickshaws are owned by the men who pull them, and rickshaw
operation is a very important source of employment for the rural poor. A recent survey by the
Bangladesh Bureau of Statistics found that rickshaw's share is 30 percent of the transport sector's total
contribution to the GDP.
There has tended in the past to be an assumption that it was not necessary to pay attention to
NMT, except as a hindrance to motor traffic, as they would rapidly disappear with the improvement of
the condition of the road network and economic development. There is some evidence of decline in the
ownership of bullock carts and in their use on improved rural roads. However the numbers of cycle
rickshaws and bicycles in rural areas is continuing to increase, especially on improved roads: recent



126                                   Annex 2.4
Page 2 of 5
studies show an increase of non-motorized vehicles on the improved road network of about 40 percent
one year after improvement to about 60 percent three years after improvement. NMT will continue to
play an important role in the rural transport system, and remain an important means of income-
generation for the rural poor, for the foreseeable future.
Although these figures indicate the large potential of NMT in meeting the transport needs of
the rural poor, there is the potential danger that the positive impact will be biased towards males and
the wealthier classes. Women's activity patterns show a low involvement in direct income generating
activities (on average 6.3 hrs per day is spent on domestic tasks, only 1.1 hr on income generating
activity). Cultural factors play a central role here and the proposed NMT project will include careful
investigation of the social, cultural, and economic factors related to the use of NMT.
The bicycle is the first means of independent, wheeled transport that a rural Bangladeshi can
afford to own. The ownership and use of bicycles can therefore be expected to continue to increase as
rural incomes increase. In rural areas bicycles have the advantages that they can be used to carry
significant amounts of goods as well as the rider, and they can be used on earthen tracks and narrow
footpaths (where larger vehicles cannot move) as well as on the road network.
2.     Design, Manufacturing and Financing Issues
The design of cycle rickshaws - passenger rickshaws and rickshaw vans - is based on bicycle
technology. They use a standard bicycle frame with the rear wheel removed, bicycle front forks, and
the bicycle front brake (rear brakes are not fitted). The extended rear frame, the rear axle, and the
passenger or goods-carrying body of the cycle rickshaws are fabricated in local workshops and bolted
on to the bicycle frame. There have been some innovations in design - stronger wheels on rickshaw
vans and minor variations in body designs - but to a very large extent the cycle rickshaws have
remained unchanged for many years, except for some regional adaptations.  The result of the
unchanged technology is that cycles rickshaws are widely available throughout the country and can be
produced, maintained and repaired anywhere. On the other hand the present designs are technically
deficient in many ways - they require great effort in pedalling, they have limited load space, and have
insufficient braking capacity. They are also subject to frequent breakdown - broken wheels, punctured
tyres, damaged frames and front forks - and require a lot of maintenance and repair.
Bullock carts are a traditional technology in Bangladesh, and are produced by highly skilled
carpenters in roadside workshops. The traditional, large wooden cart wheels, sometimes with iron
plated rims, are inefficient, and are a source of damaged to improved roads - in 1993 the Local
Government Division issued a circular that requires bullock carts used on paved roads to be fitted with
rubber-tyred wheels.
There could be potential to increase the load carrying-capacity and utility of bicycles in
Bangladesh through the introduction of bicycle trailers, particularly since the same basic design could
also be used as a hand-cart - a similar initiative is having growing success in Sri Lanka. Small scale
introduction and testing of such transport innovations that are successful in other countries is included
in the pilot program, with special attention for the transport needs of women, including addressing
current design constraints.



127                                Annex 2.4
Page 3 of 5
There have been several previous initiatives to improve the design of NMT in Bangladesh, and
in particular to improve the design of cycle rickshaws. These initiatives have had no significant impact
on the many cycle rickshaws in use in rural areas, for several reasons:
*     The emphasis was purely on technical improvement to make the cycle rickshaws more
efficient, based on 'outsiders' perceptions of what was required, rather than basing innovations
on the needs and priorities of the rickshaw pullers, makers and users and setting the technical
developments in the context of the social and economic conditions within which cycle
rickshaws function.
*     The emphasis was on producing completely new designs of cycle rickshaws which were
different from the traditional technology, and which proved to be more expensive. The
successful adoption of these improved designs therefore required the rickshaw owners to invest
in a completely new, costly and, in their eyes unproven, technology. This they were reluctant
to do. Also, the new designs tended not to be suited to manufacture in the existing workshops.
*     To overcome the problems of cost, there was a tendency to try and introduce the new designs
through co-operatives. However co-operatives are rare among rural rickshaw pullers who
operate as individual owner-riders, although they do often form into local associations to
protect their common interests. No attempts were made to co-ordinate technical innovation
with the provision of credit for purchase of the new cycle rickshaws.
Market prices of new passenger rickshaws vary from Tk 8,000 to Tk 9,000, while prices of
rickshaw vans are slightly lower at Tk 6,000-7,000. Prices of used vehicles vary from Tk 3,000 to Tk
3,800. Monthly net income of rickshaw pullers varies from Tk 1,500 to Tk 1,700, with similar income
levels for rickshaw van pullers. Both categories reported that the monthly income reported is the only
source of income. Depending on the family size, their monthly expenditures vary from Tk 1,000 to Tk
1,500, making it very hard to put aside savings.
Commercial banks do not finance the purchase of NMT, regarding it as too risky. A number of
NGOs who operate as rural credit financing agencies offer loans to the rural poor which can, inter-alia,
be used to buy cycle rickshaws. However, in most cases the loans are not tailored specifically to
rickshaw purchase - thus, for example, the maximum credit limit may be too low for a poor person to
buy a new cycle rickshaw.



128                                  Annex 2.4
Page 4 of 5
3.    Objective, Benefits and Strategy for the Pilot Program to Improve NMT
The objective of the pilot program is to improve the performance and safety, increase the
reliability, improve the efficiency and load-carrying capacity, and increase the passenger comfort of
NMT.
The benefits will be reduced operating costs and improved levels of transport services at lower
fares, thus increasing the contribution of NMT to an efficient rural transport and trading system, and
sustaining and increasing the opportunities for the rural poor to generate income from production,
operation and repair of NMT. Furthermore, in combination with upgrading rural roads to all year
accessibility (not necessarily for motorized vehicles, but at least for NMT) under the project, mobility
and accessibility of the rural poor to centers of economic activity, health and education will be
improved substantially.
The strategy for the pilot program to improve NMT is determined primarily by learning from
past failures, and adopting an approach that will maximise the chances of success. The key features of
the strategy are as follows:
The program will focus first on improvements in cycle rickshaw design, second on design
improvements for bullock carts (with emphasis on alternatives for narrow-rimmed, damaging
wheels). Thirdly, the program will also examine the problems faced by women in using NMT
for their transport tasks and will introduce adapted designs (e.g. women bicycles) to cater for
women's transport needs. On a small scale, the program will demonstrate successful
technologies, developed abroad, such as bicycle trailers and wheelbarrows. The program will
learn by experience, and have the flexibility to adjust priorities based on that experience.
*     The program  will not attempt to introduce a 'better rickshaw'. Rather it will focus on
improvements to the design of specific aspects of the cycle rickshaw - eg. bodywork,
transmission, braking, front fork, wheels, etc. - which can be retro-fitted to existing rickshaws
as well as incorporated into newly-manufactured vehicles, and which can be produced in the
existing workshops. This will allow the many thousands of existing rickshaw pullers to benefit
from the improvements without making a large investment in a new vehicle.
*     The program will work from the start together with its target groups - rickshaw owners and
pullers, rickshaw makers, bullock cart owners and makers, and women. It will be guided by
their priorities for design improvements, and will work in partnership with them to develop,
test, modify and finalize the improved designs.
*     The program will emphasize the promotion and dissemination of the improved designs within
the project area and to other parts of the country, and the provision of credit to the rural poor
to purchase the improved designs. To this end it will from the start generate the interest of,
and involve, rural credit financing agencies and government, NGO and private sector actors
who can contribute to dissemination.
*     One successful technology to be demonstrated is a simple wheel-making technology, developed
and proven by the Intermediate Technology Development Group (ITDG), which can be used



129                                 Annex 2.4
Page 5 of 5
by existing small workshops to make strong, light, low-cost wheels for rickshaws, for rubber-
tyred bullock carts, for bicycle trailers and for other NMT.
4.    Implementation of Pilot Program
The pilot program will be in two Phases - Development and Dissemination - each of two years
duration. LGED will sub-contract the implementation of the program to an NGO, with the first phase
beginning in January 1997. The contracted NGO will combine the capability to work in a participatory
manner with grassroot NGOs and target groups in rural areas with the technical capacity needed to
develop the improved designs and the resources to organise and conduct training of local workshops in
the project area. Limited provision is made in Phase 1 for technical assistance to support the NGO in
technical and socio-economic aspects. LGED will select the NGO to execute the program, following
the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as
Executing Agency. To avoid delays in implementation of the component, LGED will invite shortlisted
NGOs no later than September 1, 1996, to submit their proposals and award the contract for Phase I
by March 10, 1996.

EQUIPMENT REQUIREMENTS FOR THE PROJECT
1.    Strategy
An inventory of the existing stock of equipment - construction, transport, laboratory and office
equipment - in each of the fourteen project Districts has been carried out.
The equipment procurement strategy for the project is:
a)    To provide the necessary vehicles, and office and training equipment, for efficient
operation of the Project Implementation Office (PIO).
b)    To ensure that each project District has:
-     sufficient reliable construction  equipment, in good  working  order so  that
implementation of civil works is not delayed by lack of equipment;
-     a well-equipped laboratory, and field testing equipment, so that quality control of civil
works can be maintained through laboratory and field testing of soils and materials;
-     sufficient vehicles - four-wheel drive, pick-up, motor cycles and bicycles - for effective
operation of the District and Thana offices, and to ensure the mobility of staff that is
necessary for effective supervision of works;
-     essential office and communications equipment for efficient project administration and
reporting;
-     essential training equipment.
c)    Initially to equip the PIO, and the five Districts in the Greater Dhaka Area which,
because they have not yet benefited from an RDP, have only very limited equipment.
Subsequently to procure additional equipment for the remaining nine project Districts to
replace existing equipment which will reach the end of its useful working life during the course
of project implementation.
d)    To introduce in each of the project Districts the items of low-cost, appropriate, locally-
made construction and maintenance equipment that the project will develop, for use on project
civil works and for demonstration to contractors.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT
AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT (RRMIMP 2)
TRAINING COMPONENT UNDER THE PROJECT
1.    Objectives, Policy and Administrative Issues
Training is the essential method for acquiring knowledge, skills and for changing attitude in order to
cope with the quickly changing world. Development of competence of staff within LGED is
prerequisite in order to: assume the wider role played by LGED in rural infrastructure development -
not only related to hard-core engineering but also related to other aspects of development, like,
monitoring impact of infrastructure on socio-economic development, community participation in
infrastructure planning etc.; take up the challenging tasks of increased work-load; and cope with the
rapidly changing technology with regard to infrastructure planning, development and maintenance.
With donors' support LGED has taken a very ambitious program for the staff and some groups outside
LGED . On top of the program undertaken by LGED with regard to training of LGED staff, there is
still some opportunity to support LGED in its effort to develop trained manpower in order to cope with
the added responsibility warranted by this project and other projects. The following sections deal with
the current aspects of training in LGED; objectives, policy and administrative aspects of the training
program to be undertaken under this project.
2.    Training and LGED
The training activities at LGED started in the former Works Program Wing (WPW) in 1981 which
gained a real momentum in 1984 with the establishment of Local Government Engineering Bureau
(LGEB). At present all training programs in LGED are coordinated and implemented through the Head
Quarter Training Unit (HQTU) at Dhaka and fifteen other District Training Centers (DTC). The
Training Unit is headed by a Superintending Engineer (SE) who is assisted by an expatriate Training
Adviser and local training specialists and two Senior Training Specialists (STSs), three Training
Specialists (TSs), one Assistant Training Specialist (ATS) and fifteen District Training Officers
(DTOs). District Executive Engineers of LGED provide administrative support to the DTOs when
required.
The responsibilities of the LGED Training Units include:
* develop an overall policy for LGED training
* assess training needs for different categories of staff
* develop training materials for all courses
* arrange and conduct training courses at HQ and district levels in order to fulfill national training
requirements in the sector
* arrange and conduct on-the-job-training during preparation and implementation of schemes
* provide assistance for specific training programs of all projects under LGED
X Action plan for Direction in Management Development of LGED, Report of the Management Capability
Strengthening (MANCAPS) project, ISO, Swedish Management Group, 1995



138                                   Annex 2.6
Page 2 of 5
* arrange conferences, seminars, workshops, study tours etc. for LGED
Training Unit provides training for Projects Directors (PDs), Executive Engineers (XENs), Thana
Engineers/Assistant Engineers and other staff at the Thana and District level. The training program is
based on the a need assessment made five years ago. Currently another need assessment exercise is
being conducted by the Training Unit. Future training program will be based on the needs assessed
from this exercise.
HQTU is responsible for the overall coordination of all training courses. The courses offered by the
HQTU are conducted by the concerned TS. DTOs conduct those courses which are conducted in the
district level. The HQTU and District offices of the Training Unit are organized and well equipped
with modern teaching aids and the staff are professionally competent.
* In addition to the implementation of the National Training Program, HQTU of LGED also
centrally coordinate the training courses planned under different projects implemented by LGED.
The TU also provides assistance to different projects in conducting project specific training
courses. The TU has also shown its flexibility in responding to the needs of LGED arise from time
to time due to the change of training needs - like training on environmental and socio-economic
aspects - and targeted trainee - like, contractors, laborers.
3.    Objectives of the Training Program Under the Project
3.1   Immediate Objectives:
* to increase capability of LGED staff to implement project components effectively;
* to increase capability of local government bodies (Union Parishads, Market and Ghat Management
Committees) with respect to local level infrastructure planning following users input approaches;
* to increase technical and management capability of local contractors.
3.2    Long-Term Objectives
* to increase sustainability and efficiency of rural infrastructure investments with optimum input
from all actors in the process.
4. Training Strategies
The training component of the project will have the following strategies:
*     the component will play a complementary role to the ongoing training activities of LGED;
* the courses offered under the training component will be chosen in such a way that it will have
sustainable impact;
* the project will finance on priority basis training related to the components either first introduced
with an option for institutionalization or other components with an options for institutionalization
in this rural development project - participatory planning, equipment management and cost



139                                 Annex 2.6
Page 3 of 5
accounting, socio-economic monitoring and evaluation (SEM&E), capability enhancing of local
contractors, management and maintenance of markets and ghats etc.; and
on-the-job training will be encouraged in order to link training with real working situations.
5.    Training Under RRMIMP 2
5.1    Local Training
The project specific training activities under the project will include, inter alia, the following:
(i)    Training on Management/Project Management
(ii)   Planning (including condition surveys), contracting, supervision and inspection, and
monitoring and reporting of road maintenance.
(iii)   Courses on construction method, quality control, and reporting procedures for
construction of FRBs and FRB structures, markets and ghats.
(iv)   Courses on construction method, quality control and reporting procedures for
construction of drainage structures on rural roads, which is a major component of the
project.
(v)    planning and implementation procedures for the SRR component and the provision of
advisory support to the Union Parishads.
(vi)   Rural transport issues
(vii)  Community participation in infrastructure planning with an especial emphasis on the
selection, design, operation and maintenance of schemes under the structures on rural
roads, markets and ghats components.
(viii)  Local bodies training (Union Parishads, market and ghat Management Committees)
with an emphasis on information sharing and users input approaches regarding the
implementation procedures for the SRR component and management and maintenance
of markets and ghats.
(ix)   labour groups training on tree plantation and caretaking, routine embankment
maintenance, culvert pipe production and installation of pipe culverts.
(x)    Contractor training with emphasis on technical and management aspects of contracting,
management of construction equipment, bill payment procedures etc.
(xi)   Socio-economic monitoring and evaluation (SEM&E) related training.
In addition to the above trainings the project will finance the national training program of LGED.



Annex 2.6
Page 4 of 5
5.2    Foreign Training
The project will finance two types of foreign training: overseas study tours in the Asian region by
LGED staff and attendance of LGED staff involved in the project at relevant overseas short training.
The study tours will encompass, inter alia, local level participatory planning approaches and
maintenance procedures practiced in some selective Asian countries.
The short overseas training courses for LGED staff involved in the project will focus, inter alia, on
the construction management, project management with especial emphasis on the computer application
of project management and economic analysis of rural infrastructure projects.
6.     Cost Break-up
A provision of 36.60 million Taka (0.92 million US$) was kept for training and study tours under the
project. A tentative break-up of the total amount is provided below:

7.   Administrative Issues
The Project Director will have the overall responsibility to implement the training component of the
project. He will be assisted constantly by the Senior Training Coordinator (STC) of the Project
Implementation office (Headquarters) and the District Training Engineers in this respect. The Project
Implementation Office will maintain close liaison with the Training Unit of LGED in relation to the
planning and implementation of the training courses of the project. Before the beginning of each
financial year, the Project Director with the help of the STC and the Training Unit will chalk out
detailed training program for training related component of this project for the following financial
year. The Project Director will ensure that the training programs undertaken under the project is
included in the Training Calendar of LGED with name of the specific courses and duration of such
courses. The courses which are envisaged to have direct implication on the planning and
implementation of the project will be undertaken as soon as the start of the project.



141                                     Annex 2.6
Page 5 of 5
For the overseas training, project related personnel - both working in the field and at the PIO - will get
preference. Upon returning from the overseas training courses, the trained personnel - individually or
in a group - will present the salient features of the overseas training in a seminar organized by the
project. The trainee(s) (overseas) will also submit to the Chief Engineer, LGED a detailed report on
the overseas training courses elaborating on the courses undertaken, training methodology adopted for
conducting such courses and relevance of such training in relation to rural infrastructure development
in Bangladesh. Unless otherwise required due to extraordinary circumstances, the overseas trained
project personnel should not be transferred from the project area within one year of returning to
Bangladesh from the overseas courses to ensure their contribution to the project. The overseas trainees
will get daily allowances as per SDC rule as SDC is the major contributor of this component.
The Project Director with the help of STC will review at least on a half yearly basis about the progress
of implementation of training component with the Training Unit. Deliberations of such a meeting will
be recorded and copy of the meeting minute should be circulated to all concerned. The Project Director
with he help of STC will also review the modules prepared by the Training Unit for project specific
training and arrange revision of modules, wherever necessary.
