BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT
AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT (RRMIMP-2)
OUTLINE TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR STUDIES
1.           TOR FOR SMALL-SCALE CONTRACTING INDUSTRY STUDY
Objectives of the Study
The objectives of the study are:
1 .    To develop a better understanding of the nature and characteristics of small-scale civil
engineering contractors who work, or wish to work, for LGED and of the technical, financial,
commercial and management constraints to their development.
2.    To advise on improvement of the existing measures that are being applied, and additional
measures that could be adopted, by LGED under the RRMIMP-2 to increase the capability of
contractors in the project area, particularly in respect of their technical and management skills,
their ability to bid for and implement higher value and more technically demanding contracts,
and their level of ownership of equipment.
3.    To identify broader measures that could be adopted by LGED, and by LGED in co-ordination
with other agencies such as the Roads and Highways Department (RHD) and in the context of
future donor-supported projects, to develop the small-scale civil engineering contracting
industry in Bangladesh and to promote the development of a thriving plant hire contracting
industry.
Scope of Works
1 .    Review of LGED and RRMIMP-2 pre-qualification, tendering and contracting documents and
procedures, and of their experience with small-scale contractors (through discussions with
LGED staff at HQ, project, District and Thana level) and of earlier initiatives to develop
contractor capability.
2.    Review of the activities in respect of contractor development, and of equipment management
by LGED, that are being carried out under RRMIMP-2.
3.    Review  of initiatives to develop standard contract documents for donor-financed small
infrastructure works in Bangladesh, and to establish common pre-qualification of contractors
by LGED and RHD.
4.    Preparation of an overview of the extent and characteristics of the small-scale contracting
industry, primarily through examination of LGED's records and procedures for pre-qualifying
contractors to work on RDP projects, and of their experience of these contractors. This,
together with analysis of other sources of information will provide the basis to develop an
assessment of the extent of the small-scale contracting industry, and a classification of different



143                                   Annex 2.7
Page 2 of 11
categories of small-scale contractors based on key indicators such as equipment owned, number
of skilled and qualified staff, and types of work that they are able to undertake.
5.     Based on this analysis, a detailed survey (which might include workshops with groups of
contractors) of a cross-section of different categories of small-scale contractors in the project
area. This cross-section will include contractors pre-qualified for and working on RRMIMP-2
contracts (some of these in the RDP-7 areas will have several years of experience of such
work, while others in the Greater Dhaka area will have only limited experience); and other
contractors who wish to work for LGED, including those who have failed to pre-qualify and
those who undertake smaller works for the RHD. The purposes of the survey are to:
i)     define the business structure and financial situation of the different categories of small-
scale contractors, and identify the financial constraints that they face in taking on
different types of contract with LGED and other agencies;
ii)    define the numbers and types of unskilled, skilled, and qualified staff that they employ,
and the basis of employment (e.g. casual, fixed-term or permanent);
iii)   assess the perceptions of small-scale contractors about the long-term security of their
businesses, the types and level of commercial risks involved, and the constraints that
they face in investing in longer-term business development (e.g. in employing technical
staff on a permanent basis);
iv)    examine the attitudes towards and constraints to investment in equipment, and attitudes
towards the development of plant hire companies;
v)     assess needs for developing the technical and management capability of the contractors.
6.     Discussions with other interested parties in Bangladesh - including other government agencies
(and in particular the RHD and the Institutional Development Component of its RRMP2
project), IDA and SDC and other donor agencies, the Dhaka office of the IFC, leasing
companies, equipment manufacturers and importers, banks who have small-scale contractors as
clients, and contractors' associations - to assess:
i)    issues relating to financing of small-scale contractors by commercial banks;
ii)    the feasibility of and mechanisms for co-ordinated actions to develop the small
contracting industry;
iii)   the feasibility of and mechanisms for, financing the purchase of construction
equipment, including supporting the development of plant hire companies, and the
possible role of the IFC;
iv)    the potential role of contractors' associations in assisting the development of the small-
scale contracting industry.
7.     Preparation of a report of findings and conclusions, together with specific recommendations
on:
i)    improvements to existing measures, and additional measures to be adopted, by LGED
under the RRMIMP-2 to increase the capability of contractors in the project area,
particularly in respect of their technical and management skills, their ability to bid for
and implement higher value and more technically demanding contracts, and their level
of ownership of equipment;



144                                 Annex 2.7
Page 3 of 11
ii)    broader measures by LGED, and by LGED in co-ordination with other agencies and in
the context of future donor-supported projects, to develop the small-scale civil
engineering contracting industry in Bangladesh, and to encourage the development of a
plant hire contracting industry, with recommendations on institutional mechanisms and
implementation modalities to apply these measures.
A summary of findings will be prepared, and a one-day workshop will be conducted with
LGED, project, and financing agency staff, representatives of small-scale contractors and other
interested parties (as defined in Para. 6 of the scope of works) to review the findings prior to
the finalisation of the report.
Inputs
It is envisaged that the study will require:
*     A two-month input by an international specialist with extensive experience, including Asia
region experience, of the development of small-scale civil engineering contracting industries.
*     Inputs of two months each by two national consultants - a contracting specialist and an
enterprise development specialist, each with extensive relevant experience.
*     All necessary support services including transport, office services and communications.
LGED and the RRMIMP-2 project will ensure all necessary advisory support and access to relevant
information and personnel.
2.         TOR ROAD CONSTRUCTION CONTRACTOR UNIT COSTS STUDY
Objectives of the Study
The objectives of the study are to:
*     Develop a model which can be applied by the RRMIMP-2, and by the other RDPs in LGED,
for the compilation of standard unit cost rates for the major work items in the construction of
improved FRBs, culverts and bridges on FRBs and Rural Roads, and improved facilities at
Growth Centres, and in the periodic maintenance of FRB roads and structures. Also to define
the most reliable sources of the information to estimate actual costs incurred by contractors.
*     Apply this model to prepare standard RRMIMP-2 unit cost rates for the major items of
construction and maintenance works, and define the procedure for annual updating of the unit
cost rates.
Scope of Works
1.    Review of the existing RRMIMP-2 contract documents, Bills of Quantities, and estimates of
unit cost rates.
2.    Discussions with other RDPs and with the LGED Design Cell to review and analyse variations
in Bills of Quantities and estimates of unit costs rates within LGED.
3.    Review of the full economic rates for hire of LGED equipment to contractors developed in
each of the 14 project Districts under the RRMIMP-2.



145                                  Annex 2.7
Page 4 of 11
4.     Review, with a sample of RRMIMP contractors in different project Districts, of their
compilation of unit cost rates (rate analysis), and of their estimates of:
-      labour and equipment productivities;
-      labour and equipment cost rates;
-      material costs;
-      other direct cost components;
-      overheads and profits that they must incorporate into their unit cost rates;
-      lump sum costs.
5.    Cross-checking of the competitiveness and validity of the cost, productivity, overhead and
lump sum estimates used by contractors in their rate analyses. This will comprise:
-      field investigations in the same project Districts including discussions with materials
suppliers, LGED staff and other sources of cost data, and site analyses of productivity
rates and of labour costs;
-      identification and analysis of other sources of cost information, including available
statistical data and relevant reports produced by LGED projects.
6.    Preparation, review with RRMIMP and other project and LGED Design Cell staff, and
finalisation of recommended formats for calculating standard unit cost rates for all major
construction and periodic maintenance work items.
7.     Using these formats, preparation of draft recommended standard RRMIMP-2 unit cost rates.
Comparison of these with the existing project rates and, based on review of findings with
relevant RRMIMP and LGED Design Cell staff, preparation of final recommended standard
unit cost rates.
8.    Preparation of a final report of findings and recommendations which fully addresses the study
objectives, and defines the procedure for annual estimation of up-dated standard unit cost rates.
Inputs
It is envisaged that the study will require:
*     A six week input by an international specialist with extensive experience, including Asia region
experience, of small-scale contracting and cost estimation.
*     A two month input by a national consultant civil engineer with extensive experience of the
costing of civil works and the ability to work effectively supporting the international specialist
to collect realistic cost data from the field.
*     All necessary support services including transport, office services and communications.
LGED and the RRMIMP-2 project will ensure all necessary advisory support and access to relevant
information and personnel.
3.                TOR FOR FRB REGIONAL PRIORITISATION STUDY
Objectives of the Study
The objectives of the study are to:
*     Develop guidelines for prioritising the needs for investment in improvement of FRBs between
the 64 Districts in the country, which can be applied in making decisions on the geographic



146
Annex 2.7
Page 5 of 11
allocation of financing agency resources for RDPs and GOB resources for FRB improvement.
*     Develop guidelines for selecting and prioritising specific FRB roads to be improved in a
particular area.
The findings of the study will be applied, inter-alia, as inputs to the identification and preparation of a
new RDP as a follow-up to the RRMIMP-2 project.
Approach, and Scope of Work
The purpose of the study is not to produce a prescriptive master plan for the development of the FRB
network in Bangladesh. Rather, it aims to:
i)    provide rational guidelines for selecting and prioritising the geographic areas for new RDP
projects, for defining the scope of FRB works in those areas, and for geographic targeting of
GOB FRB investments. This will be based on assessment of the present level of improved FRB
provision in relation to the road network needs and to the economic status and potential, and
the social situation, in the different Districts of the country;
ii)    develop standard guidelines, which can be applied across different FRB investment programs,
for selecting and prioritising specific FRB roads for improvement, taking account of the needs
for economic viability and for local participation in the selection of investment schemes to meet
local priorities.
The guidelines must also take account of the future availability of GOB revenue financing for routine
and periodic maintenance of the improved FRBs, in order to sustain the investments made.
The study will involve:
1.    Review with LGED of its existing FRB data-base (comprising a comprehensive road inventory
and maps), complemented by selective field surveys and collection of additional data from
LGED District Offices as necessary, to define the present extent, surfacing and condition of
the FRB network (including structures on FRBs) by District.
2.     Review of the criteria actually used by LGED to define this network; the costs of routine and
periodic maintenance of improved FRBs; and the present and likely future availability of GOB
revenue funds for such maintenance. Based on this, and to the extent necessary, refine the
criteria and define a smaller, 'core' maintainable FRB network and its distribution across the
Districts, and assess the cost of improving and upgrading this network to good, all-weather
bitumen surfaced standard.
3.     Definition of easily quantifiable indicators to compare the present level of provision of
improved, bitumen surfaced, good condition FRBs in the 64 Districts in relation to the present
economic status, future economic potential and social situation of those Districts. This will
draw upon, but refine and expand, the indicators used in the IDA/LGED rural infrastructure
strategy study.
4.     Using these indicators, and with selective additional field surveys as necessary, preparation of
an analysis of the present level of improved FRB provision by District.
5.     Based on the analysis of the maintainable FRB network and the present level of improved FRB
provision:
-      preparation of guidelines for selecting and prioritising Districts for FRB investments;



147
Annex 2.7
Page 6 of 11
preparation of guidelines for assessing the scope of FRB improvement works in any
District;
presentation of recommendations on any specific areas which are high priorities for
FRB investment, and on the scope for investments there;
-     definition of procedures that can be applied by LGED to update the analysis of the
network and the level of FRB provision.
6.    Review  with LGED of the methodologies used by different LGED  rural infrastructure
programs to select FRB investment schemes; of the LGED socio-economic monitoring and
evaluation (SEM&E) activities and findings; of efforts to harmonise SEM&E in LGED; and of
methodologies used in LGED for local participation in selection of priorities for FRB
improvement.
7.    Based on this review, and complemented by selective field investigations, preparation of
guidelines for selecting and prioritising specific FRB roads to be improved in a particular area,
including local participation in the identifications.
8.    Review of all findings and recommendations with LGED and the IDA and the SDC prior to
finalising the report.
Inputs
It is envisaged that the study will require:
*     A ten week input by an international transport economist/planner with specialist experience,
including Asia region experience, in the planning and development of rural road networks.
*     Ten person-months of inputs by a team of experienced national consultants whose combined
expertise will cover transport economics, rural transport planning and rural road engineering.
*     All necessary support services including transport, office services and communications.
The consultants will work closely with the Rural Infrastructure Maintenance Cell, the Planning Unit
and the Management Information Systems Cell in LGED. LGED and the RRMIMP-2 project will
ensure all necessary advisory support and access to relevant information and personnel.
4.            TOR FOR LONG-TERM SOCIO-ECONOMIC IMPACT STUDY
Objective of the RRMIMP-2 Impact Study
The objective of the RRMIMP-2 impact study is to evaluate the impact of FRB, Rural Road, market
and ghat investments, and the resulting increased efficiency in the transport and trading system, on
economic and social development within the influence area of the investments. The study will:
i)    analyse and quantify the effects of the FRB, Rural Road and ghat investments on transport;
ii)    analyse and quantify the effects of the market investments on marketing;
iii)   in respect of economic development, evaluate and quantify the impact of the different
categories of transport investments, and of market investments, on agricultural production and
productivity, business activity, direct and indirect employment, income-generation and poverty
alleviation, and consumption and investment.



148
Annex 2.7
Page 7 of 11
iv)    in respect of social development, focus on evaluating and quantifying the impact of the road
improvements on the efficiency and use of rural health (including family planning) and
education services.
The study will evaluate the impact of the investments on all sectors of the rural population in the area,
but will be designed in such a way that the impact on sub-sectors of the rural population which are of
special interest - the poor, destitute women, women engaged in income-generating activities, transport
operators including rickshaw pullers, vehicle owners and market traders - can be evaluated.
Methodology
The methodology will be based on, but refined and extended from, that used for the RDP-7 impact
study. In addition to the RDP-7 FRB roads for which some data has already collected, the study will
focus on RRMIMP-2 FRB roads and markets to be improved (including markets where the adjacent
ghat will also be improved), and Rural Roads where SRRs are to be constructed, in the 1996/97
Financial Year. Samples of these FRB and RR roads and markets (including ghats) will be selected for
detailed investigation, together with 'control' roads, markets and ghats which will not be improved
during the duration of the study. Benchmark Surveys will be carried out prior to the construction
works, and Follow-Up Surveys will be carried out at two stages after the works have been completed -
one year after completion to collect data for analysis of short-term effects and impacts; and three years
after completion to collect data for analysis of longer-term effects and impacts.
Based on the RDP-7 study the survey methodology will comprise:
i)    selection of survey villages within the influence area of, but at different distances from, each
sample road and market;
ii)    surveys in each village with a sample of households to collect household level data. The sample
will be stratified to cover different land-owning classes - landless and marginal (up to 0.4 ha.),
medium (0.4-1.0 ha.) and large (more than 1.0 ha.) - plus the population sub-groups which are
of special interest. The Benchmark and Follow-Up Surveys will use the same household
samples;
iii)   community, 'key informant' and any other surveys to collect other necessary data, e.g. in
relation to markets, schools, heath centres, business enterprises, price and wage levels, and the
effects of the road improvements on transport; and in relation to 'special interest' sub-samples
such as transport operators and market traders.
Scope of Work and Implementation Arrangements
The RRMIMP-2 impact study will be carried out under contract to LGED by the same consultants who
are already carrying out the RDP-7 impact study. These consultants have specialised expertise in the
conduct of rural impact studies, and this arrangement allows the data and findings from the RDP-7
study to be incorporated into the larger study.
The study will be managed by the LGED Additional Chief Engineer (Design, Planning, Monitoring
and Evaluation) and the consultants will co-ordinate with the SEM&E unit the RRMIMP-2 Project
Implementation Office. The services of LGED field staff will be used to the extent possible for data
collection but the consultants will be fully responsible for the efficient and effective conduct of the
study, including supervision of all data collection.



149                                  Annex 2.7
Page 8 of 11
Prior to commencing work the consultants will prepare, for review and approval by LGED and the
IDA, a proposal for the conduct of the study which will:
i)    define the possible impacts to be evaluated, and the indicators which will be used to evaluate
those impacts;
ii)    summarise the types of data to be collected, the methods of data collection, and the analytical
procedures that will be adopted to derive findings on the impact of the infrastructure
investments;
iii)   define the arrangements for incorporation of the data-base and findings from the RDP-7 study
into the larger study;
iv)    define the sampling frame - numbers of FRB and RR roads and markets, numbers and location
of villages, sizes of stratified survey samples - to ensure that the findings on the impacts of the
investments on different sectors of the rural population are statistically significant. This
sampling frame will take account of the fact that there will be some decay of the original
sample - as a result of death, people moving away from the area, etc. - between successive
surveys;
v)    define the procedure for use of 'controls' to isolate the impacts of the investments from other
economic and social changes that take place in the study area over the duration of the study;
vi)    present a work plan for the conduct of the study;
vii)   define the resources and budget required for the conduct of the study.
The study will be carried out in three Phases:
Phase 1:      Benchmark Survey. July 1996 to March 1997
Prior to commencing this Phase the consultants will present their proposal for review and approval.
The Phase 1 work will involve:
-      the preparation of the questionnaires;
-      recruitment and training of survey staff;
-     pre-testing of questionnaires;
-      field work and sampling data collection to select the stratified samples of households;
-      conduct of field benchmark surveys;
-      data processing; and
-     preparation of report on Benchmark Survey.
(Between Phases 1 and 2 the second phase of the RDP-7 study will be carried out).
Phase 2:      Follow-Up Survey and Analysis of Short-Term Effects and Impacts
July 1998 to March 1999
The Phase 2 work will involve:
-      refinement of the methodology;
-      preparation of survey questionnaires;
-      recruitment and training of survey staff;
-      pre-testing of questionnaires;



150                                 Annex 2.7
Page 9 of I1
submission of a Phase 2 Inception Report which will present the refinements to the
methodology and the questionnaires, and the detailed program for field data collection;
field work and sampling data collection of households - this will collect follow-up data
on broad activity levels such as land ownership and occupational changes in
households;
conduct of field follow-up surveys;
data processing; and
preparation of report on the first Follow-Up Survey and findings on short-term effects
and impacts.
Phase 3:     Follow-Up Surveg. Analysis of Longer-Term Effects and Impacts. and
Final Report on Impact Study. July 2000 to June 2001
The Phase 3 work will involve:
-     refinement of the methodology;
-     preparation of survey questionnaires;
-     recruitment and training of survey staff;
-     pre-testing of questionnaires;
-     submission of a Phase 3 Inception Report which will present the refinements to the
methodology and the questionnaires, and the detailed program for field data collection;
-     field work and sampling data collection of households - this will collect follow-up data
on broad activity levels such as land ownership and occupational changes in
households;
-     conduct of field follow-up surveys;
-     data processing; and
-     preparation of report on the second Follow-Up Survey and the Final Report on the
-     Socio-Economic Impact of the rural infrastructure investments.

ON THE UNION PARISHAD
Objectives of The Study
The broad objectives of the study will be the following:
* Review of the existing situation concerning revenues and expenses at the Union Parishad
and Thana levels, with special emphasis on local resources mobilization.
* Make an inventory of the present obligations of the UP's and establish a required budget to
fulfill these obligations.
* Suggest ways and means to fill the gap between actual revenues and required budget, in
particular covering the procedural, financial and institutional aspects of local resource
mobilization.

Page 10 of ] 
Major Tasks and Scope of Work of the Consultancy Service
Major tasks of the consultancy service will include, inter-alia, the following:
* Review various studies carried out in Bangladesh for local resource mobilization at the Union
and the Thana levels.
*  Critically examine the rules and procedures for levy of taxes, fees etc. by the Union Parishads
and the thana level, identify difficulties/shortfalls and make proposals for revision of
rules/procedures.
* Critically examine the issue of collection of taxes/fees by the Union Parishads and the thana
levels. Compare actually raised revenue with the potential revenue income, identify
difficulties/shortfalls and make proposals for solution of rules/procedures.
* Identify new tax based and non-tax based revenue generation by the Union Parishads and the
thana levels and prescribe operational mechanisms for the same.
- Examine the possibility of distribution of other taxes, such as, Land Transfer Tax to the Union
Parishads and the Thana level.
* Examine the institutional capability of the Union Parishads and the thana level in the collection
of taxes/fees etc, identify problems and propose solutions including appropriate staffing.
* Critically examine the procedures for leasing of markets, water bodies, ghats and other
resources by the Union Parishads and the thana level, identify problems and make proposals
for improving the same.
* Critically examine the distribution pattern of the tax/fees collected and resource generated by
the Union Parishads, identify difficulties and submit proposals for their improvement.
* Identify the fund required to be contributed by the Union Parishads and the thana level for
over-all development and infrastructure construction/maintenance activities, funds generated at
present, identify shortfall and make proposals as to how the  short-fall can be met; in
particular, review and suggest measures for increasing contribution and participation of UP's
and local committees in maintenance.
* Examine the training requirement of the Union Parishads and the thana level for local resource
mobilization, existing training facilities and identify future need of training, preparation of
training courses and appropriate training modules.
* Review the financial information system of the Union Parishad and the Thana level including
the techniques of budgeting and budgetary control and suggest the necessary changes in the
design and financial statements for observance of proper financial rules and monitoring of
funds.
* Any other issue which the consultants will consider relevant and useful on the subject.



152                                 Annex 2.7
Page 11 of 11
The study will cover 14 sample unions - one union in each new district of the project area of
RRMIMP-II and 4 thanas - one in each greater district of the project area i.e. Dhaka, Pabna, Bogra and
Rajshahi. In selecting the unions and the thanas, appropriate statistical criteria will be adopted by the
consultants to make the samples representative ones.
Time Table
The study will be completed within nine months after its commencement.
Reporting

The draft final report will be thoroughly reviewed by LGED at this stage in order to

comments of LGED on the draft final report. The time limit proposed is 1 month.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE
PROJECT
MAINTENANCE OF RURAL INFRASTRUCTURE
1.1   Introduction
This annex sumnmarizes the extent and objectives for maintenance of Feeder Roads B (FRB),
Rural Roads (RR), structures on FRBs and RRs and other infrastructures (markets, ghats) which are
maintainable condition in the project area of the RRMIMP-II or which will be improved under the
Project. It describes the responsibilities and organizational setup for the implementation of a sustained
maintenance program, the funding requirements and the measures to strengthen human and institutional
capacities. For the later it proposes possible links to the ongoing Institutional Development Component
(IDC) of the 2nd RRMP (RHD Project funded by ODA & IDA). Regarding the implementation of
maintenance it relies on the "Guidelines for Implementation of Rural Infrastructure Maintenance
Program", LGED, July 1995.
1.2   The Challenge for Maintenance of Road Assets
The flat and low lying terrain as well as heavy rains and high flood levels in the country
require high embankments and numerous bridges and culverts to assure an all-weather transport
network linking growth center markets to the national roads and to ensure the access for the rural
population to those poles of development. Sufficient routine and periodic maintenance will
safeguard these expensive national assets over an extended number of years. Maintenance works
generate short and long term incomes to small contractors, landless farmers and destitute women.
The upkeep of infrastructure will have a positive impact on agricultural production and income
through reduction of transport costs. Generally speaking, sustained improvement of the life of
rural (and urban) population and thus poverty alleviation will not be possible without an adequate
access to economic, social and welfare services.
2.   Objectives of Routine and Periodic Maintenance Components of RRMIMP-II
The overall development objective of the proposed project is to increase rural incomes and
reduce rural poverty by establishing an improved, sustainable rural transport and trading infrastructure.
The routine and periodic maintenance of the infrastructures shall guarantee that they function over an
extended period of time and safeguard the investments made. In line with the objectives of the project
(see Annex 2.1) the strategy shall focus on i) planning, progranmming and implementation of routine
and periodic maintenance for FRBs and structures, and important RRs in maintainable condition, ii)
supporting the maintenance of SRRs, Markets and Ghats by local bodies at Thana and Union Parishad
(UP) level, iii) use of the private sector for the execution of works (contractors, LCSs) and iv) support
to human, institutional and technical capacity building at all institutional levels of the Project.
3.    Rural Road Network and other Rural Infrastructures
3.1   Inventory and Condition of Rural Road Network in Project Area
The total rural road network of the Project area (Greater Dhaka, Rajshahi, Pabna and
Bogra Districts) which is under the current responsibility of LGED, amounts in rounded
figures, to (details of total length of the rural road network in km and structures in lin.m are in
Table 1) are as follows:
3,200 km of Feeder Roads Type B (FRB), and
29,800 km of Rural Roads (9,450 km of RI, 10,150 km of R2 and 10,200 km of R3).

3.2   Inventory of Structures on Feeder Roads-B and Rural Roads (SRR)
Many of the rural roads were first built under food for work schemes, with no funds
for mechanical compaction or for provision of structures. The rural road network in the project
area is thus characterized by a large number of gaps. The LGED inventory shows that there
are 16,600 linear meters of structures and 9,600 lm of gaps on FRBs, and 58,700 Im of
structures and 86,700 Im of gaps on RRs. Details are presented in Table 1
3.3   Inventory of Hats and Ghats
There are a total of 425 growth center markets in the project area, out of which 100
markets have been covered under the RRMIMP and 136 markets have been proposed for
improvement under RRMIMP-Il. Moreover, a total of 41 ghats have been identified for
upgrading under RRMIMP-II.
4.    Present Responsibilities of LGED and External Support
4.1    The Local Government Engineering Department(LGED)
The task and authority of the Local Government Engineering Department (LGED), an
agency of the LGD, is extensively described in Annex 1.3.
LGED's responsibilities which are directly relevant to the maintenance of rural
infrastructures in the area of the RRMIMP-2 project are:
* routine and periodic maintenance of FRBs, including structures.
*  the maintenance of important RR1 roads, and the maintenance of structures on Rural
Roads.
* the provision of technical supervision and advice to local government bodies to assist them
in fulfilling their responsibilities for the operation of rural infrastructures.
These responsibilities of LGED have to be seen equally in the national context of rural
infrastructure development, in which the department's broader infrastructure activities include
small-scale water resources, irrigation and flood control, rural water supply and sanitation,
urban infrastructure development in secondary and rural towns, and construction of cyclone
shelters and primary schools. Following the objectives of the Strategy for Rural Development
Projects, LGED emphasizes the use of labour-intensive methods for construction and
maintenance works wherever appropriate.
4.2    External Assistance to Maintenance of Rural Infrastructures
Due to the fact that the Government is responsible for maintenance, only few donor-
assisted projects (e.g. RDP-6 and RDP-7) gave attention to maintenance with the exception of
maintenance by contractors in the first year after construction. This course is changing and
more external assistance is made available to strengthen the capacity of local institutions. The
ADB financed RDP-18 is to be mentioned, where a long-term technical assistance on
maintenance management, training, and management capability strengthening is provided,
through Rural Infrastructure Maintenance Cell (RIMC). The RESP/ISP provides support to
RIMC, focusing on road condition surveys. This is in the context of the improvement of the
data base for planning in general at Thana level. Discussions during appraisal have shown that
the coordination and integration of data collection and utilization should be further enhanced.
The ISP Training Unit offers training in maintenance to District and Thana Engineering staff.



156                                 Annex 2.8
Page 4 of 12
5.    Local Government Bodies
5.1    Union Parishad and TDCC
Since the last local government reform in 1991, the Union Parishads (UP) have been
given increased responsibilities for the maintenance of rural infrastructures (e.g. priority
setting in planning, preparation and supervision of contracts, financial autonomy), the Project
will have to put much efforts on capacity building at that level. Through the Union Market
Management Committees (UMMC) they are responsible for operation and maintenance of
markets, too. More details of the local government system are given in Annex 1.4.
An important platform for planning and programming of rural infrastructures is the
Thana Development Coordination Committee (TDCC), regrouping the Chairmen of all Union
Parishads as the voting members; the TDCC constitutes the lowest government body. More
direct participation by the users for planning and programming of rural infrastructure works
will be enhanced by the Project. The LGED Thana Engineer sits on the TDCC as non-voting
member.
In conjunction with the Local Government Division the project will further analyze
existing shortcomings in resource mobilization and will provide practical assistance to the local
government bodies in the project area, with emphasis on greater cost recovery from the leasing
of improved markets and retention of lease monies by local government bodies. Local
financing issues are discussed in more detail in Annex 1.04.
5.2   Present Exteral Assistance at Thana and UP-level for Infrastructure Management
Only routine maintenance of RRI and RR2 is substantially covered through well
designed food for work programs (Road Maintenance Program, RMP) giving job opportunities
to the poor. Funding is through counterpart funds generated by Canadian food aid. The
management support by CARE Bangladesh has been integrated into LGED in 1995-96.
6.    Maintenance Systems and Practices
6.1   Road (routine and periodic) Maintenance System, incl. structure
Since its establishment in 1992, LGED's Rural Infrastructure Maintenance Cell
(RIMC) has made considerable progress in establishing the framework for road maintenance by
setting up a preliminary road inventory system, introducing national maintenance guidelines
(July 1995) and establishing a training program. The assistance by ADB is in its early stages.
Although coordination meetings among staff of projects supporting capacity building for
maintenance (RDP-6, RDP-7 and ISP) have started, no detailed plan of action for the further
development of an appropriate maintenance management system could be presented to the
mission during appraisal. Such a plan of action shall be required prior to negotiation of the
project.
Thus it was agreed that the project will, in coordination with RIMC, introduce, test
and refine a planned routine and periodic road maintenance system for the LGED network in
the project area covering all FRB roads, important RR1 roads, and structures on FRBs and
RRs. This will demonstrate how maintenance work can be planned and implemented effectively
. It is expected that experience gathered within the project will be replicated nationally by
LGED.
Since there will be a significant stock of improved, bitumen-surfaced FRBs in the
project area at the start of the project, the implementation of planned road maintenance will be



157                                 Annex 2.8
Page 5 of 12
given immediate priority. The project will finance periodic maintenance (with the following
sliding scale: 80% years 1-3, 40% in year 4 and 20% in year 5). Routine maintenance will be
financed from the GOB revenue budget. The project will finance periodic maintenance of all
FRBs & important RRs, and all structures on FRBs & RRs in the project area.
6.2    Monitoring and Review of GOBILGED Guidelines
The guidelines of GOB/LGED define the following maintenance categories:
* Routine Maintenance (day to day work including earthwork and pavement patching)
* Periodic Maintenance (pavement re-sealing and structures)
* Emergency Maintenance (mainly flood damages during monsoons)
* Rehabilitation-type Maintenance (restoration of structure to its intended purpose)
The Project will follow these guidelines but will closely monitor the proposed
maintenance programs for the fourth category. It is a category of works which is normally not
to be funded by maintenance budgets. The risk lays in investing funds for rehabilitation instead
of maintenance. Furthermore, such funds often are used also for improvement and upgrading
of roads and structures. During mid term review the experiences will be analyzed and
eventually corrective measures be proposed.
Although emergency maintenance needs can by definition not be predicted, the
presently used system of block allocation to districts seems neither to be sufficient nor properly
accounted for. Experiences will be collected and reported at mid term review with the aim to
propose a more rational and efficient allocation of budgets if and when required.
6.3   Planning and Programming
Planning and implementation of LGED maintenance is in principle under the
responsibility of the Thana Engineer (TE). There is one Sub-Assistant Engineer (SAE) to
second the TE specifically for maintenance, although this does not happen in practice. The
Executive Engineer (XEN) has the approving and overall supervision authority of all such
maintenance activities in his district. In order to match with the available budgets, desirable
maintenance works per district and Thana need to be prioritized in an iterative programming
exercise. The GOB/LGED guidelines indicate criteria for prioritization:
i)    FRB constructed under foreign- aided projects
ii)    FRB with high traffic volume
iii)   other FRB (partially paved, missing structures)
iv)    Structures on Important Rural Roads (after meeting
demand of FRB) with priority on RCC structures
While these criteria are practical at the present stage, where there is limited network,
needing mainly routine maintenance, a rational network approach will have to be introduced to
properly integrate periodic maintenance of pavements. While it is estimated that periodic
pavement maintenance will be required every 5 years in average, the detailed and economically
optimal timing and level of intervention has to be defined individually for the road section
based on:
* pavement and surface condition expected future deterioration, dependent on type and
volume of traffic
* available funds
The project shall propose simple and flexible procedures to obtain the necessary data
and define the yearly periodic and routine maintenance program. Sufficient time will be



158
Annex 2.8
Page 6 of 12
allowed to discuss the proposed annual programs prior to approval. The schedule of the
guidelines shall be monitored and any improvement can be proposed if deemed necessary. For
example, the inspection of the roads has to be carried out in May, which coincides with the end
of the construction season when LGED staff is generally very busy with supervision.
However, with the introduction of the AE & SAE maintenance positions, this bottleneck
should be removed. In the context of increased participation the project shall assist LGED in
developing and introducing procedures to expand membership of the District Maintenance
Committees to include user representatives (bus and truck, auto-rickshaw and rickshaw
operators) in order to promote sharing of information and user input into selection of
maintenance priorities.
7.    Maintenance Needs and Funding
7.1    Maintenance Needs, Costs and Preliminary Estimate
An initial assessment has been made for the requirements of all FRBs (about 3,200
km), important RRs (1,000 km) and existing structures on FRBs & RRs (45,000 linear meters)
in the project area. Tables 3 and 4 present an estimate of the needs and costs (including unit
cost) of routine and periodic maintenance within the project area, over the period 1995/96 to
2003/04. The tables are based on:
-     the existing stock and condition of the LGED network as defined in the road inventory,
excluding RR3 roads.
*     assumptions about the road improvements that will be carried out over the next nine
years (new stock).
-     the assumption that periodic maintenance should be applied to paved roads (FRBs &
RRs) and structures (FRBs & RRs) in maintainable condition, and that periodic maintenance is
required every five years.
-     unit costs of routine and periodic maintenance which are derived from the consultants
and LGED figures.
The maintenance requirements of roads and structures will increase progressively as
the roads and structures in the project area become due for maintenance following the
improvement/construction under existing projects (RDP-7 & RDP-6) and the new project
(RRMIMP-II). Details of the annual construction programs for the physical components are in
Annex 2.03 The subsequent maintenance requirements of, and the performance of routine and
periodic maintenance on the LGED road network in the project area will be monitored and
reported upon by the PIO with the assistance of the DSM consultants. The first year routine
and periodic maintenance program for the project area will be planned and prepared prior to
the project start.
7.2    Maintenance Funding
The maintenance funds allocated from the GOB revenue budget for LGED have
continuously increased from 3000 lakh Tk (US $7.5 million) in 1992-93 to 5500 lakh Tk (US
$13.8 million) in 1994-95, with a further increase to 6500 lakh Tk (US $16.3 million) for
1995-96. The main source of funds for maintenance of Rural Roads is the CARE Road
Maintenance Program (RMP). RMP was previously in the Ministry of Relief and funded
through Canadian food aid; it was transferred to LGED in 1995-96. RMP includes routine
maintenance of about 72,000 km of earth roads (mainly RRs and some FRBs) in about 3,600
Unions throughout the country (20 km per Union). Crews of destitute women (10 per Union)
are paid to maintain the roads.

8.     Implementation
8.1    Routine Maintenance and Periodic Maintenance
The routine maintenance and periodic maintenance for the project are classified as
follows:
a)     Routine Maintenance of the Earth shoulders and Embankment Slopes, Care taking of
trees and Cleaning of waterways in structures:
-      trimming/filling and compaction of earth shoulders and slopes and reforming the
camber
-      cutting/removal of vegetation from road shoulders, slopes and structures
-      repair of rain-cuts , pot holes, ruts and rat-holes in the embankment
-      re-fining of turfing
-      care taking of trees



160                              Annex 2.8
Page 8 of 12
removal of debris from waterways/siltation
cleaning of bridge deck drains and weep-holes in abutments and wing walls
b)    Routine Maintenance of Road Pavement and Structures:
repair of potholes
sealing/patching of cracks
repair of depressions
repair of edges
filling and compaction of hard shoulders
replacement of missing bricks in structures
minor concrete repair to structures
c) Periodic Maintenance of Road Pavement and Structures:
-     resurfacing
-     repairs of hard shoulders
-     replacement/repair/painting of all sign posts and guard posts
-     replacement of brickwork in structures
-      replacement of poor concrete and replastering
-      reforming of the railing on the bridge deck
-     painting of steel work
8.1.1  Work Standards and Implementation Methods
Routine maintenance of embankment earth shoulders and embankment slopes,
care taking of trees and cleaning of waterways in structures will be carried out by
groups of disadvantaged women, providing them with long-term employment. LGED
will enter into direct contracts with the group.
Routine maintenance of the pavement and structures will be carried out by the
contractors. Experiences in RDP-6 and RDP-7 show that the contractors are not
interested in small maintenance contracts. The project will thus seek to propose and
test alternative methods. One option could be, to let a contract for routine maintenance
of the carriageway covering maintainable network of a district or several Thanas.
Another option is the "term contract", which will let the contract of whole network of
a district for a given period (usually one year). Other options could also be explored
and tested by the project. The RIMC is still an early stage to develop the contracting
arrangements for routine pavement maintenance. The project will assist the RIMC in
developing and refining routine maintenance contracting procedures, including with
respect to the testing of different options in the field.
The periodic maintenance works will be carried out by the local contractors
prequalified under the project using the same contracting procedures as for FRB
improvement and upgrading works. The DSM consultants will prepare the engineering
designs, tender documents and engineers' estimates and will assist the PIO in the
evaluation of tenders and the processing of contract awards. Contracts for periodic
maintenance will be let over the period September to November each year so that
works can commence the following January. The periodic maintenance works will be
supervised by LGED with the assistance from the DSM consultants who will certify all
payments to the contractors. Quality control procedures and contractor defects liability
will be the same as for FRB construction works.



161                                Annex 2.8
Page 9 of 12
The project will assist LGED at District and Thana level in all aspects of
maintenance planning, implementation and management, inventory and condition
survey, contracting inspection and supervision as well as monitoring and reporting.
The project will provide low cost equipment and tools for routine maintenance
works, together with supervision vehicles since mobility of LGED staff is essential if
maintenance at dispersed sites is to be properly planned and supervised.
8.1.2 Procurement
Civil works for routine (carriageway) and periodic maintenance of roads will
be procured by National Competitive Bidding (NCB). Contract documents will be
prepared based on those used on RDP-7; moreover, they will be made consistent both
with GOB regulations and the latest World Bank Standard Bidding Documents for
Procurement of Smaller Contracts. Prequalification of contractors for project works
will be completed in accordance with the Bank's guidelines prior to project start, and
the prequalification will be updated by screening out the poorly performed contractors
at Mid Term Review.
Civil works for tree plantation and care taking on project FRB will be procured
by direct, annual contract with groups of disadvantaged women at fixed payment rates.
Contract documents for procurement of these works will be based on existing LGED
procedures.
8.2    Maintenance Management System
Based on the experience of the RDP-7, a Project Implementation Office (PIO) will he
established with its headquarters in Dhaka, headed by a Project Director (PD). The PIO will
work directly with the offices of the LGED XENs in the 14 project districts and, through them
with the offices of the Thana Engineers. The project will finance the PIO operating costs, the
PIO incremental staff positions (including maintenance staff) at Headquarters, District and
Thana level in the project area to provide implementation support to the LGED staff.
Under the project management, there will be a maintenance unit comprising separate
maintenance staff at the Headquarters, District and Thana level. The maintenance unit will be
headed by one Assistant Engineer (a senior and experienced Engineer) at the Headquarters. He
will be supported and assisted by the Maintenance Advisers (expatriate and local) under the
DSM Consultancy. One of the Assistant Engineers (AE) at District level (either incremental
staff or LGED staff) and one of the Sub Assistant Engineers (SAE) at Thana level (either
incremental staff or LGED staff), will be exclusively responsible for all maintenance activities.
Their positions will be AE (maintenance) and SAE (maintenance) respectively. The SAE will
carry out the road inventory, condition survey, planning, programming, supervision,
monitoring and reporting of maintenance activities of all rural infrastructures in the respective
Thana with the help of DSM consultants. He will assist the Thana Engineer in the procurement
of all maintenance contracts. He will report his activities to the AE (maintenance) at district
who will coordinate the maintenance works of all Thanas in the district and will report to the
AE (maintenance) at Headquarters. The positions of SAE (maintenance) and AE (maintenance)
will be on permanent basis which will be continued even after completion of the project. This
will help to sustain the maintenance system at local level. After the completion of the project,
these positions will be integrated in the RIMC.
The maintenance unit of the project will coordinate with the RIMC and with other RD
projects on specific issues to share views and experiences. The maintenance system and
practices to be developed in the project area should also be discussed with RIMC so that the
system, if found effective, could be replicated nationally through the RIMC.



162                              Annex 2.8
Page 10 of 12
9.    Training
Training will be provided to LGED District and Thana staff to increase their capability to
implement maintenance works effectively. The training will include:
-     planning (including conduct of condition surveys)
-     inspection, supervision, monitoring and reporting
-     standardization and contracting of routine and periodic maintenance of pavements
-     work methods, techniques and implementation of routine maintenance (labour groups)
and   pavement maintenance
-     management and organization of Labour groups
The training will result in LGED staff at District and Thana level in the project area being able
and motivated to effectively plan, implement and manage routine and periodic maintenance.
The project will coordinate with the LGED training unit to design an effective training
program for maintenance in the project area in order to fulfill the objectives of the project's
maintenance component and also to avoid any duplication in training programs within LGED & the
project.
Training will be provided to local bodies (Union Parishads, Market and Ghat Management
Committee) in the Information Sharing and Users Input approaches, and in management and
maintenance of roads, structures, markets and ghats.
The project will establish a coordination with the IDC of 2nd RRMP(RHD) as far as possible
and will share information and experiences to contribute to Human Resource Development of LGED.
10.   Growth Center Markets and Ghats
The participation in selection and detailed design of growth center markets and ghats will
develop a sense of local ownership of the improved markets. The project will assist and support the
formation of Market Management Committees, and will provide them with training and assistance in
planning and managing the operation and maintenance of the improved markets. Maintenance of the
improved markets will be financed from the 15% of lease revenue allocated to the Union Parishad for
this purpose. The MMC shall play a key role in the decision for the use of the maintenance funds
which will be administered by the Thanas. The market management procedure will include, along with
other aspects, the preparation of a budget and procedures for markets maintenance, and the
implementation of routine and periodic maintenance. The day to day maintenance (cleaning) of markets
is the responsibility of the lessee which is hardly in practice. To ensure the regular cleaning, the MMC
will take over the responsibility for cleaning the market from the lease holder, but at his cost, and
employ disadvantaged people for this work. Alternatively, the amount of money required for a year for
cleaning and day to day maintenance of the markets, could be taken as security from lessee. One
twelfth of this amount could be released at the end of each month to the lessee, provided the MMC
certifies that the requisite work has been performed satisfactorily.
The project will provide assistance to the formation of Ghat Management Committee and to
establish an effective operation and maintenance system of the ghats, using the same procedures as for
the markets. Emphasis will be placed on the setting of toll rates which will generate sufficient funds
for establishing maintenance a system.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT
AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
ASSESSMENT OF IMPLEMENTATION CAPACITY
1.    Background
Since the project is larger and the implementation period shorter than the ongoing project
(RRMIMP-1), there is a question whether there is adequate implementation capacity to complete the
project as scheduled. Implementation capacity is related to mainly the following factors:
(a) the size of the project in terms of physical output;
(b) the implementation period;
(c) contractor capacity to complete the works; and
(d) LGED's capacity to manage the project( procurement, supervision, disbursement, and
monitoring).
These aspects with reference to the proposed project are discussed below: (a) . (b). and
:The national SRR component and NMT pilot component are reviewed separately as contractors
and agencies implementing them form a different category which does not compete with the rest of
the main project components which consist of the FRB roads, markets, and ghats. These latter are the
items which call on contractor capacity to the maximum. (The component on periodic maintenance is
an ongoing project through contractors and what the project will do is mainly to improve the quality
and only to small extent more coverage). In the first project, there were 500 kms of FRB roads, 65
growth center markets and no ghats, while the present project has 575 FRB roads, 135 markets, and
41 river ghats. Market improvements and ghat construction are comparable in the complexity, period
and cost of contracts ( about US $ 60,000 to 75,000 each) all done by small local contractors; one
market improvement contract can be equated to a contract of about 2km FRB road improvements (
from earth to BC paving; costing about $ 200,000) in terms contractual length and difficulties. On
this rough basis of output comparison, the physical output of this group of works under the first
project can be equivalent to about 630 kms of FRB improvements, as against 930 km FRB works
under the new project). This indicates a capacity increase of about 50% over the first project.
The above increase would be more intensive annually, considering the shorter span of
implementation period proposed in the second project ( five years). The first project actually would
take nine years, but effectively for comparison with new project only 7 years, because its first year
was lost due to delays in appointing supervision consultants and delays in issuing contracts which
will not happen in the present project as consultant's appointment is a condition of Board
presentation, and the first year bids would be invited before project approval by the Board; in
addition, the last year of the first project was mostly to cover the contractor's defect liability period
which, in the case of the new project, will fall outside the IDA-implementation period of five years
due to special arrangements being made (see para ...). Combining increased size and reduced time,
the new project needs to have a capacity increase of about 107 %. This increase is feasible due to the
following factors: larger project area, of about 75% ( increase from 8 districts to 14 districts); these
new areas have large number of suitable small contractors who operate there, as Bangladesh has a
long tradition of doing many projects through contractors; these contractors usually work in their
own localities, usually limited to one or two districts. (The first project's output in this group of
works was produced through about 280 smnall contracts involving about 100 different contractors,



174                                      Annex 3.3
Page 2 of 2
about 2.5 km road improvements per average contract, each contract running through about 10 to 15
months: Delete this later?). The project would further assist the contractors in
improving their capacity and work-quality through project supervision and training as done during
the first project. There has been adequate response from contractors with average three or four pre-
qualified contractors bidding for each contract. The experience of recent bidding for retroactive-
financing works under the new project in the new project districts showed a similar response for the
about 15 contracts recently bid and awarded, indicating adequate capacity and response in these new
areas as well.
Thus, about 75% capacity increase can be explained from the increased project area with its
own contracting capacity. The remaining extra capacity (of about 32% ) is expected from the fact that
the new project is not a pilot project and is built on lessons from the first project in trying out new
specifications, standards and procedures etc. ( see para..1...); LGED has become a much more
experienced body (now  implementing about US$1.5 billion worth of projects portfolio)  and
contractors have also become more experienced; the new project has also many measures to increase
contractor efficiency and capacity (larger size contracts of average 4-6 km, with 2-year contracts,
provision of mobilization advances for buying own equipment, higher standards in prequalifying
contractors, more training, etc.); it also will give about 100 km of the roads to a new category of
medium size contractors (for 10 km , US $1.0 m value contracts) who currently work on the main
roads projects, who will infuse new capacity to the feeder road sector). In addition, 45 km FRB
improvements have already started under retroactive financing before project approval. The second
project is much better prepared and its implementation in all aspects would be more efficient.
As regards the national components , the SRR components would be done through very small
Labour contractors ( contracts of about $8000 each), for which there is no shortage (see para.. on
SRR). The NMT component would be implemented almost entirely through experienced local NGOs
with minimal supervision from LGED.
2.     LGED's Capacity to Implement the Project
LGED's capacity, as noted, has expanded very much under various management
improvement measures, and would be further enhanced through the project and other donor support
(see para...). In addition, the project would provide incremental staff in the project areas, will have a
larger PIO with a separate deputy director for the new project districts as they need special attention
(see para....), and will have additional staff in LGED to look after the SRR components through
participatory specialists to be recruited; it will also have a large DSM consultants support appropriate
for the larger size project. A new feature would be technical audit , which will carry out regular six-
monthly inspection of work verification and technical quality as an additional control and monitoring
tool to ensure efficient and timely project implementation.
It should be emphasized here that the capacity increase of total 107% estimated above is
based on a 5-year implementation period for the new project. However, a sensitivity analysis shows
that if a 6- year implementation period is taken for the new project, the capacity increase needed is
only about 72%, which can be easily met from the area expansion factor alone (75%); the increased
capacity from efficiency factors would, in this case, provide considerable slack in capacity. This
means that one strategy for reducing any risk related to implementation capacity would be to extend
the implementation period by one year to 6 years at the time of the mid-term review, if considered
appropriate, based on an assessment of the performance and conditions at that time.

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
MONITORING AND EVALUATION PLAN AND PERFORMANCE INDICATORS
A. Monitoring And Evaluation Plan
1.    The proposed project would be monitored and evaluated both in terms of quantity and quality
from the perspective of input, output, outcome and impact. In order that the indicators should serve an
meaningful purpose, the project objectives and components/inputs has been adequately linked with the
expected processes, outputs and impacts. The 'input indicators' would measure the quantity of
resources provided, 'output indicators' would measure the quantity services that have been created,
'Outcome indicators' would measure the quantity of direct results that have been achieved through the
provision of project goods and services and the impact indicators would measure the degree to which
wider sector/project objectives are being achieved through the direct outcome of project activities.
2.    The input monitoring and evaluation would focus on weather all the required resources
including counterpart funding for maintenance and GOB's financing, are in place in timely fashion to
implement the program activities and services. The input information would include mainly on the
progress of land acquisition, and timely contract awards for the procurement of civil works, goods and
consulting services. The Input indicators would be regularly monitored and assessed through quarterly
and semi-annually and annually reports with an emphasis to identify problems in the delivery of inputs
so that they may be resolved before causing significant delays in progress achievements. The output
monitoring and evaluation would focus on the immediate results of activities, as demonstrated, for
example in kms of road build, number of structures completed, number of markets developed and
number of river jetties (ghats) constructed. LGED will monitor continuously all the input and output
indicators through progress reports and send the reports to IDA. The summary of the progress reports
would reflect the progress in respect of the proposed indicators, and in case of significant changes, the
reports would specify the reasons as well as the remedial action proposed.
3.    The 'Outcome' evaluation would focus on the direct results that have achieved through the
provision of project's services. This would be monitored by measuring number of vehicle usage in case
of feeder roads, mobility increased by increasing number of all weather rural roads, amount received
on leasing of markets and ghats, amount spent on regular maintenance of markets and ghats. The
Impact evaluation would measure the degree to which the project objectives are being achieved through
the direct outcome of the project activities. This would include efficient service delivery to number of
thanas, accessibility to number of poor communities in the rural areas, safety, cost efficiency, improve
service quality to reduce transportation cost, income generated from developed market and ghats; and
employment generated. It is proposed to launch a project workshops where all the stakeholders would
be invited to decide the responsibilities of each stakeholders and specifically focus on how and who
would maintain the baseline date for monitoring evaluation of all above referred indicators.
4.    During negotiations, assurance would be sought that: (a) quarterly and annual progress reports
for all project components would be prepared by the PD and submitted to the Chief Engineer LGED,
who in turn will submit them to IDA; (b) a joint mid term review would be conducted by July 1999,
whichever is earlier; and (c) an Implementation Completion Report would be submitted by the
Government to IDA within six months of the closing date of the project. The detailed format of the
quarterly and annual progress report will be agreed at negotiations.



183                                  Annex 3.7
Page 2 of 5
5.     A sumnmary of performance indicators prepared in consultation with LGED, is provided in
Tables A and B. Except for impact indicators, the input, output and financial indicators, LGED would
monitored on a computer based program, which would also be used by IDA during the supervision
missions. The achievement of key indicators would be reflected in the supervision report form 590.
The guidelines for measuring the outcome and impact indicators would be designed during the project
launch workshop in consultation with the UPs, TDDC, and LGED. Based on the availability of the
base line data, the input, output and outcome indicators would be monitored during every supervision
mission, but the Impact indicators would be reviewed at the project start, mid-term review and
implementation completion.

correspondence (which are estimated at six staff weeks (to be checked) per year throughout project
implementation).

BANGLADESH
SECOND RURAL ROADS AND MARKETS IMPROVEMENT AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
LAND ACQUISITION ACTION PLAN: SUMMARY
1.    Background
The infrastructure works comprise construction of large numbers of small, widely scattered rural
schemes. Land acquisition is required only for improvement/upgrading of FRB roads. Strucures on other
FRB roads and on Rural Roads (SRR) will be built within existing alignments on public (khas) land. The
local government bodies will confirm, before implementation of any market or ghat scheme commences,
that there are no disputes over the public land on which the improved facilities will be constructed. If any
additional land is needed, no formal land acquisition will be involved, and the local government body will
arrange for purchase of the additional land prior to implementation.
FRB road improvement/upgrading works will be on existing road alignments. Land, or more
precisely asset, acquisition will be required to acquire:
Narrow strips of land adjacent to the existing embankments in order to widen and/or raise them to
meet the defined pavement cross-section standard.
*     On some FRB roads, land for minor re-alignment of sections of the existing embankment.
*     Standing trees, crops and small buildings within the widened road alignment.
2.    Impacts of Land Acquisition
Impacts have been assessed from a Land Acquisition Assessment (LAA) study based on surveys of
the land acquisition impact of FRB improvements under the RRMIMP and the RDP-6 projects; and a
Preliminary Inventory Survey of all FRB roads short-listed for improvement which included estimation of
the land area, and numbers of buildings and trees to be acquired.
The LAA confirmed the inadequacy of present land acquisition practices in Bangladesh. The level
of compensation is generally below the full replacement value; not all affected persons receive
compensation; and there can be long delays in payment of compensation, with physical occupation of the
land before payment is made.
Table I presents a summary of land acquisition impact by District. The project will acquire about
254ha. of land, 73% of it cultivated agricultural land, the remainder homestead land; about 260 small,
pernanent 'pucca' buildings; many more 'kutcha' buildings; and large numbers of trees'. The affected
persons will be owners of the agricultural, homestead and commercially developed land; owners of trees
and standing crops; owners of homestead dwellings and commercial buildings; mortgagees of land,
sharecroppers and agricultural labourers; renters of homestead dwellings; and owners and employees of
businesses operating from commercial buildings.
The environmental impact of loss of these trees will be more than compensated by the tree
plantation program on all improved FRB roads.



188
Annex 3.8
Page 2 of 10
Large numbers of people will be affected. About 24,000 households (estimated as 42 per km of
FRB road) will suffer losses. About 72% of affected households will lose agricultural land, and about 36%
homestead land. Land acquisition will be 'neutral' in its effect on different sectors of the rural population,
ie. affected households are a normal cross-section of landowners. The level of effect will be small. Land
acquisition equates to about 0.44ha per km of road or, on average, strips of land 2.2m wide on each side of
the existing embankments. Thus most households will lose narrow strips of land (plus standing trees and
buildings on that land) from their plots. Two-thirds of affected households will lose less than 5% of their
total land-holding, 87% less than 10%, and 98.5% less than 20%. The acquisition of land may result in a
few households having to re-locate their homesteads but numbers will be small, and their location cannot be
determined until land acquisition is underway on each road.
Table 1: Impact of Land Acquisition

to be          Affected

. Manikganj          9.52         3.52        13.05               2            1,546

Note: Figures may not add due to rounding.
3.     Land Acquisition and Compensation Policy
Land acquisition will be carried out, within the legal framework of the Bangladesh Requisition of
Immovable Property Ordinance of 1982 and its subsequent amendments, by application of the agreed Land
Acquisition Action Plan (LAAP). The LAAP accords with World Bank Operational Directive OD 4.30 in
respect of minimising land acquisition, and ensuring full and prompt compensation of all project affected
persons (PAPs) for loss of assets and actual temporary loss of income so that PAPs are not adversely
affected.



189                                Annex 3.8
Page 3 of 10
The land acquired will be the minimum needed to improve and upgrade the FRB alignments to the
defined technical standard. The alignments of the widened embankments will be carefully designed to avoid
all religious/historical sites, schools and large/important buildings; and to avoid buildings where permanent
businesses operate and to acquire lower value land wherever possible. Sections of road will be re-aligned
only for safety reasons (to increase radii of curves), or where re-alignment has less asset acquisition impact
than upgrading the existing alignment.
The compensation policy is presented in Table 2. Given that the level of impact on PAPs will be
small, it focuses on monetary compensation for losses. PAPs will be compensated at full replacement value
for loss of assets, estimated based on assessed local current market values of the assets. PAPs will be given
advance warning of land acquisition, and a three-month eviction period following payment of
compensation, so that they can take actions to deal with the disruption caused, and to minimise temporary
displacement problems. PAPs will also be allowed to salvage trees, standing crops and building materials
as additional compensation for any temporary displacement problems.
Since the area of land acquired from individual plots will be small, and PAPs will be given advance
warning of land acquisition, it is not necessary to compensate mortgagees, renters or sharecroppers of
agricultural land; renters of homestead dwellings; casual agricultural labourers; or for seasonal crops.
Where acquisition of homestead land results in the household which owns the land and the dwelling on that
land having to re-locate its homestead (defined as the plot size being reduced below 0.01 ha.) the household
will receive sufficient compensation to purchase a replacement plot and construct an equivalent dwelling.
The losses, and rights to compensation, of genuine uthulis (persons who construct buildings on
private land with the permission of the landowner) and squatters (persons who construct buildings on
private or public land without the permission of the landowner) are recognised. As owners of buildings they
will be compensated for the value of the buildings. If they are operating businesses from those buildings
they will be compensated for temporary loss of income. If they have trees or perennial standing crops on
public land they will be compensated for the value of these. In the unlikely event that serious problems are
encountered with a large concentration of genuine uthulis/squatters within the alignment of a particular
road, LGED will adopt additional contingency measures, including the possibility of re-location assistance
(provision of a plot of land or of a cash grant equivalent) to deal with this.
While PAPs will be fully compensated, exploitation of the procedure and over-compensation will
be minimised. Measures will be adopted to discourage the construction of 'fraudulent buildings' by
landowners, uthulis or squatters simply in order to obtain compensation. Also, the project will not
compensate for temporary selling platforms and the like which can bc uplifted, removed and re-located
elsewhere without loss of value.
